Proof of Hyle from Divisibility of Simple Bodies
Author proves existence of hyle by arguing divisible bodies must be continuous and then eliminating magnitude and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound syllogism proving the existence of hyle (prime matter) and evaluate each sub-argument's validity.

Core Idea
Hyle is the only possible substrate for disjunction after eliminating magnitude and form.

In this lesson from Hidayat al-Hikma, we follow a rigorous proof for the existence of hyle. The argument has two stages: first, proving that bodies which undergo disjunction (like water and fire) must be continuous; second, showing that the substrate of disjunction cannot be the mathematical body or the corporeal form, so it must be prime matter. The proof integrates refutations of atomism and substantial lines/surfaces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall conclusion
The author aims to prove that hyle exists as the underlying substrate for disjunction in all bodies. Keep this target in mind.
2
Establish the necessity of continuity
The author argues that bodies receptive to disjunction (e.g., water, fire) must be continuous in themselves. If they were not, they would be composed of parts. Those parts would either be indivisible (leading to atomism, already refuted) or divisible into substantial lines/surfaces (also refuted) or into bodies (contradicting simplicity). Thus, continuity is required.
3
Analyze the possible substrates of disjunction
Given a continuous body that undergoes disjunction, the substrate that receives this change could be: (1) the mathematical magnitude (al-miqdar), (2) the corporeal form (al-sura al-jismiyya), or (3) something else. The author rejects the first two because if either were the substrate, then continuity and disjunction would coexist in the same subject, which is impossible.
4
Conclude to hyle
Since the first two possibilities are impossible, the only remaining possibility is a third thing, which is the hyle (prime matter). This conclusion follows by elimination.
5
Address the objection
An objector asks: if we already describe bodies as 'receptive to disjunction', why prove they are continuous? The author answers that the description is based on sense perception, but real continuity is not immediately known; it requires proof. This clarification resolves the doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول فيها يعم الأجسام. فصل في إثبات الهيولى. القابلة للانفكاك مثل الماء والنار، يجب أن يكون في نفسه متصلا واحدا، وإلا لزم الجزء الذي لا يتجزى ويلزم من هذا إثبات الهيولى في الأجسام كلها؛ لأن ذلك المتصل قابل للانفصال، فالقابل للانفصال في الحقيقة إما أن يكون هو المقدار أو الصورة المستلزمة للمقدار أو معنى آخر، لا سبيل إلى الأول والثاني وإلا لزم اجتماع الاتصال والانفصال. القابلة ههنا بحث وهو أنه لما وصف الأجسام بكونها قابلة للانفكاك فلا حاجة إلى الاستدلال بكون الجسم متصلا؛ إذ بداهة العقل حاكمة أن الشيء ما لم يكن متصلا لم يكن قابلا للانفكاك والجواب: أن مراده أن بعض الأجسام القابلة للانفكاك بحسب الحس يجب أن يكون في نفس الأمر متصلا على ما يفهم من قوله: 'في نفسه'، ومن كلام الشارح أيضا حيث قيد قوله : 'كما هو عند الحس'، ولا يخفى أنه يحتاج حينئذ إلى الاستدلال. متصلا الاتصال كون شيء بحيث يوجد لأجزائه بعد فرض وقوعها حدود مشتركة، والمتصل هذا المعنى يطلق على فصل الكم ومن خواصه قبول الانقسام بغير نهاية. وإلا لزم إلخ أي وإن لم يكن متصلا واحدا فلا بد أن يكون مؤلفا من أجزاء وتلك الأجزاء إن لم تكن قابلة للقسمة أصلا لزم الجزء الذي لا يتجزى، وقد تبين بطلانه سابقا، وإن كانت قابلة للقسمة، فإما في جهة واحدة فلزم الخط الجوهري، وإما في جهتين فلزم السطح الجوهري وبطلانهما بمثل ما مر في نفي الجزء، ولا يتصور أن تكون قابلة للقسمة في الجهات الثلاث؛ لأن الكلام في الجسم المفرد، وعلى تقدير قبول تلك الأجزاء قسمة في جهات ثلاث تكون أجساما، فلم يكن ذلك الجسم مفردا، بل صار مركبا، وقد كان الكلام في المفرد هذا خلف. لأن إلخ خلاصة الدليل أن الجسم المتصل في الواقع يطرأ عليه الانفصال بمعنى حدوث هو يتبين، أو عدم الاتصال عما من شأنه الاتصال، ولا بد له من قابل وقابله في الواقع إما الجسم التعليمي أو الصورة الجسمية المستلزمة له، وهما باطلان فتعين أن يكون معنى آخر، وهو الهيولى.
The first art concerning what is common to bodies. Chapter on proving the hyle. [Some bodies] that are receptive to disjunction, like water and fire, must be in themselves continuous and one, otherwise the indivisible part would be necessitated. And from this follows the proof of hyle in all bodies; because that continuous [body] is receptive to disjunction, so that which is receptive to disjunction in reality is either the magnitude or the form entailing the magnitude, or another meaning. The first and second are not possible, otherwise the coexistence of continuity and disjunction would be necessitated. There is here an investigation: since the bodies are described as being receptive to disjunction, there is no need to prove that the body is continuous; because the intuition of reason judges that a thing, unless it is continuous, is not receptive to disjunction. And the answer: that his intention is that some bodies that are receptive to disjunction according to sense must be in reality continuous, as understood from his statement 'in itself' and from the commentator's statement where he qualified his statement 'as it is according to sense'. And it is not hidden that then there is need for proof. 'Continuous': continuity is a thing such that its parts, after assuming their occurrence, have common boundaries; and the continuous in this sense is applied to the category of quantity, and among its properties is infinite divisibility. 'Otherwise it would necessitate' etc., meaning: if it is not continuous and one, then it must be composed of parts; and those parts, if they are not divisible at all, the indivisible part is necessitated, whose falsity has been shown previously; and if they are divisible, then in one direction the substantial line is necessitated, and in two directions the substantial surface is necessitated, and their falsity is like what was mentioned in the negation of the part; and it is not conceivable that they are divisible in three directions, because the discussion is about the simple body; and on the supposition that those parts accept division in three directions, they would be bodies, so that body would not be simple but would become composite, and the discussion was about the simple — this is a contradiction. 'Because' etc. The summary of the proof is that the continuous body in reality is subject to disjunction, meaning the occurrence of a 'he' [change?] or the absence of continuity from what is characterized by continuity, and it must have a receptive substrate; and its receptive substrate in reality is either the mathematical body or the corporeal form entailing it, and both are false, so it must be another meaning, which is the hyle.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic proof for the existence of hyle (prime matter). The author begins by stating that some bodies which are receptive to disjunction (e.g., water, fire) must be in themselves continuous and one; otherwise, the indivisible atom would be necessitated, which has been refuted. From this, he argues that proof of hyle follows in all bodies. The reasoning is: the continuous body is receptive to disjunction, but the real receptacle of disjunction cannot be the magnitude (al-miqdar) or the corporeal form (al-sura al-jismiyya) that entails magnitude, because if it were, continuity and disjunction would coexist in the same subject, which is impossible. Therefore, there must be a third thing: the hyle (prime matter). The author then addresses an objection (bahth): if we already describe bodies as 'receptive to disjunction', why do we need to prove they are continuous? Isn't it obvious that only a continuous thing can be divided? The answer is that the intended meaning is that some bodies are receptive to disjunction according to sense (i.e., we observe them being divided), so they must be continuous in reality, not just in appearance. This requires proof. The author then defines continuity as having common boundaries between parts, a property of the category of quantity, and notes that the continuous admits infinite divisibility. He then enumerates the consequences if the body were not continuous: it would be composed of parts. If those parts are indivisible, atomism follows (already refuted). If they are divisible, they must be divisible in one direction (giving a substantial line) or two directions (substantial surface), both of which have been refuted earlier. They cannot be divisible in three directions because then the parts would be bodies, rendering the original body composite, contradicting the assumption of a simple body. Thus, the only possibility is that the body is continuous. Finally, the summary: when a continuous body undergoes disjunction (either by separation or loss of continuity), there must be a substrate that receives this change. This substrate cannot be the mathematical body or the corporeal form, so it must be the hyle. Thus, the proof is complete.

المقدار
الصورة الجسمية
الانفكاك

B) Because if magnitude were the substrate, continuity and disjunction would coexist
C) Because magnitude does not exist in bodies
D) Because magnitude is eternal

Key Takeaway
The proof of hyle relies on eliminating all other possible substrates of disjunction, leaving only prime matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar compound syllogism to prove the existence of a substrate for change in the case of rarefaction and condensation (takhliq wa takhthif) in bodies.
?

The Categorical Proof that All Bodies Are Composed of Hyle and Form
Author proves that every body is composed of hyle and form using a categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves every body is composed of hyle and form, and identify its major premise, minor premise, and conclusion.

Core Idea
The proof that all bodies are composite is a categorical syllogism: corporeal form depends on a subject, so every body has a subject (hyle).

In this passage, the author presents a demonstrative proof that every body is composed of hyle (prime matter) and form (corporeal form). The proof uses a disjunctive premise (the corporeal nature is either self-sufficient or dependent) and eliminates self-sufficiency based on the proven inherence of form in hyle. Your task is to extract the underlying categorical syllogism and see how it yields the universal conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion to be proven
The author states 'fa tabayyana anna kulla jismin murakkabun min al-hayula wa al-sura' (thus it is shown that every body is composed of hyle and form). That is the conclusion we must derive.
2
Find the key disjunctive premise
The argument begins with a disjunction: 'al-tabi'a al-miqdariyya imma an takuna bi dhatiha ghaniyyan 'an al-mahall aw lam takun' (the quantitative nature is either in itself independent of a subject or not). This sets up an exhaustive division.
3
Eliminate the first option via a previously established fact
The first option (independence) is rejected because if it were independent, it could not inhere in a subject. But 'hulul al-sura al-jismiyya fi al-hayula qad thabata min qabl bi al-burhan' (the inherence of corporeal form in hyle has already been proven by demonstration). Hence, independence is impossible.
4
Extract the categorical syllogism from the disjunctive reasoning
The negated disjunct leaves the second option: the corporeal nature depends on a subject. This can be cast as a categorical syllogism: Major Premise: Whatever inheres in a subject depends on that subject (implied by 'hulul yastalzim al-iftiqar'). Minor Premise: Corporeal form inheres in hyle. Conclusion: Corporeal form depends on hyle. Then, since every body has corporeal form, every body depends on hyle.
The syllogism is in the first figure (Barbara).
5
Generalize to all bodies
The conclusion 'la yakhlu fardun min al-sura al-jismiyya 'an al-hayula' (no individual of corporeal form is free from hyle) is universal. Since every body has corporeal form, all bodies are composite.
This step uses an implicit universal quantifier over bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات الهيولى
وإذا ثبت أن ذلك الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة وجب أن تكون الأجسام كلها مركبة من الهيولى والصورة؛ لأن الطبيعة المقدارية إما أن تكون بذاتها غنية عن المحل أو لم تكن، والأول محال؛ لأنها لو كانت غنية بذاتها عن المحل لما أمكن حلولها في المحل؛ لأن حلول الشيء في الشيء يستلزم الافتقار إليه، والغناء ينافيه، والتالي باطل؛ لأن حلول الصورة الجسمية في الهيولى قد ثبت من قبل بالبرهان، فتعين الشق الثاني أي افتقارها بذاتها إلى الهيولى، فلا يخلو فرد من الصورة الجسمية من الهيولى، فتبين أن كل جسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة. فإن قيل: إن الصورة الجسمية ليست محتاجة في وجودها إلى الهيولى، بل الأمر بالعكس، سيجيء في بحث التلازم بين الهيولى والصورة أن الهيولى محتاجة إلى الصورة في وجودها وبقائها، والصورة ليست محتاجة إليها في وجودها وإلا لزم الدور، بل في تشخصها وتشكلها. قلت: مراد القوم من الافتقار الذاتي ههنا الافتقار بحسب الفردية المطلقة لا بحسب الماهية، ولا يلزم التناقض بين الكلامين؛ لأن الافتقار في مرتبة الفردية، والغناء في مرتبة الماهية، نعم لو كان الغناء والافتقار في مرتبة واحدة للزم ذلك.
Chapter on Proving the Hyle. If it is established that that particular body is composed of hyle and form, then it is necessary that all bodies are composed of hyle and form; because the quantitative nature is either in itself independent of a subject or not, and the first is impossible; because if it were in itself independent of a subject, it could not inhere in a subject, since the inherence of a thing in a thing necessitates dependence on it, and independence contradicts that, and the consequent is false because the inherence of corporeal form in hyle has already been proven by demonstration, so the second alternative is determined, i.e., its dependence in itself on hyle, so no individual of corporeal form is free from hyle, thus it is shown that every body is composed of hyle and form. If it is said: The corporeal form is not dependent for its existence on hyle, rather the opposite; it will come in the discussion of the correlation between hyle and form that hyle depends on form for its existence and persistence, and form does not depend on it for its existence, otherwise circularity would occur, but it depends on it for its individuation and configuration. I say: The meaning of 'essential dependence' here according to the scholars is dependence in terms of absolute individuality, not in terms of quiddity, and there is no contradiction between the two statements because dependence is at the level of individuality and independence is at the level of quiddity; yes, if independence and dependence were at the same level, that would follow.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a demonstrative proof that all bodies are composed of hyle (matter) and form (corporeal form). The argument proceeds as follows: (1) We have already established that a given individual body (e.g., a specific body) is composed of hyle and form. (2) The corporeal nature (al-tabi'a al-miqdariyya) is either self-sufficient and does not require a substrate, or it is not self-sufficient. (3) The first possibility is impossible because if it were self-sufficient, it could not inhere in a substrate, since inherence necessarily implies dependence on the substrate. But we have already proven by demonstration that corporeal form does inhere in hyle. Therefore, the second possibility is true: the corporeal form depends essentially on a substrate, which is hyle. (4) Consequently, every individual instance of corporeal form is inseparable from hyle, so every body is composite. 

An objection is raised: In the discussion of the correlation (talazum) between hyle and form, it is said that hyle depends on form for its existence and persistence, and form does not depend on hyle for its existence (otherwise circularity would result); rather, form depends on hyle only for its individuation and configuration. This seems to contradict the claim that form depends on hyle. The answer (jawab) clarifies: The dependence referred to in the proof is 'essential dependence' (iftiqar dhati) in terms of absolute individuality (fardiyya mutlaqa), not in terms of quiddity (mahiyya). The form as a quiddity is independent, but as an individual existent it requires hyle as a subject. There is no contradiction because the two levels are distinct. If they were at the same level, there would be a problem. This illustrates a key principle in Islamic logic: distinctions of levels (maratib) resolve apparent contradictions.

Key Terms
هيولى
صورة
طبيعة مقدارية
حلول
افتقار ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the proof that all bodies are composed of hyle and form, what is the key premise that eliminates the possibility that corporeal form is independent of a subject?
A) The corporeal nature is always self-sufficient.
B) The inherence of corporeal form in hyle has already been proven by demonstration.
C) Hyle is independent of form.
D) Bodies are simple and not composite.

Key Takeaway
The proof that all bodies are composite rests on a categorical syllogism: corporeal form inheres in hyle (proven), inherence implies dependence, so every individual form depends on hyle; hence every body has hyle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the proof that every body is composed of hyle and form in proper categorical syllogism format (major premise, minor premise, conclusion). Use the disjunctive step to justify the minor premise.
?

Resolving the Apparent Contradiction: Dependence at the Level of Individuality vs Quiddity
Author resolves an apparent contradiction by distinguishing quiddity-level vs individuality-level dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dependence at the level of quiddity (mahiyya) and dependence at the level of individuality (fardiyya), and apply this distinction to resolve apparent contradictions in metaphysical arguments.

Core Idea
Dependence at the level of individuality vs quiddity resolves the apparent contradiction between 'form depends on hyle' and 'hyle depends on form'.

The passage raises an objection: hyle depends on form for its existence, so how can form depend on hyle? The author clarifies that there is no contradiction because the dependence of form on hyle is at the level of absolute individuality (fardiyya mutlaqa), not at the level of quiddity (mahiyya). This distinction between levels is a powerful logical tool that prevents false contradictions. In this lesson, we will unpack this distinction and see how it resolves the tension.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector says: 'Corporeal form is not dependent for its existence on hyle; rather the opposite. Hyle depends on form for its existence and persistence.' This seems to directly contradict the proof that form depends on hyle.
The objector appeals to a later discussion on the correlation (talazum) between hyle and form.
2
Locate the key distinction introduced by the author
The author responds: 'Marad al-qawm min al-iftiqar al-dhati huna al-iftiqar bi-hasab al-fardiyya al-mutlaqa la bi-hasab al-mahiyya' (The meaning of essential dependence here is dependence in terms of absolute individuality, not in terms of quiddity). This sets up two levels.
3
Understand the two levels
Level of quiddity (mahiyya): The essence of corporeal form, as a concept, is independent of hyle for its definition. Level of individuality (fardiyya): Each specific, individual occurrence of form requires a subject (hyle) to exist in reality.
The same distinction applies to hyle: its quiddity depends on form (as it is pure potentiality), but its individuality may depend on something else.
4
Apply the distinction to the original proof
The proof that form depends on hyle is about individual instances: no individual form exists without hyle. This does not conflict with the claim that hyle depends on form for its quiddity, because they are at different levels. As the author says: 'al-iftiqar fi martabat al-fardiyya, wa al-ghina' fi martabat al-mahiyya' (dependence is at the level of individuality, independence at the level of quiddity).
5
Generalize the principle
The same technique—distinguishing levels (maratib)—is used widely in Islamic logic and usul to reconcile apparently conflicting statements. For example, an 'amm (general) text and a khas (specific) text are not contradictory because they operate at different levels of applicability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات الهيولى
وإذا ثبت أن ذلك الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة وجب أن تكون الأجسام كلها مركبة من الهيولى والصورة؛ لأن الطبيعة المقدارية إما أن تكون بذاتها غنية عن المحل أو لم تكن، والأول محال؛ لأنها لو كانت غنية بذاتها عن المحل لما أمكن حلولها في المحل؛ لأن حلول الشيء في الشيء يستلزم الافتقار إليه، والغناء ينافيه، والتالي باطل؛ لأن حلول الصورة الجسمية في الهيولى قد ثبت من قبل بالبرهان، فتعين الشق الثاني أي افتقارها بذاتها إلى الهيولى، فلا يخلو فرد من الصورة الجسمية من الهيولى، فتبين أن كل جسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة. فإن قيل: إن الصورة الجسمية ليست محتاجة في وجودها إلى الهيولى، بل الأمر بالعكس، سيجيء في بحث التلازم بين الهيولى والصورة أن الهيولى محتاجة إلى الصورة في وجودها وبقائها، والصورة ليست محتاجة إليها في وجودها وإلا لزم الدور، بل في تشخصها وتشكلها. قلت: مراد القوم من الافتقار الذاتي ههنا الافتقار بحسب الفردية المطلقة لا بحسب الماهية، ولا يلزم التناقض بين الكلامين؛ لأن الافتقار في مرتبة الفردية، والغناء في مرتبة الماهية، نعم لو كان الغناء والافتقار في مرتبة واحدة للزم ذلك.
Chapter on Proving the Hyle. If it is established that that particular body is composed of hyle and form, then it is necessary that all bodies are composed of hyle and form; because the quantitative nature is either in itself independent of a subject or not, and the first is impossible; because if it were in itself independent of a subject, it could not inhere in a subject, since the inherence of a thing in a thing necessitates dependence on it, and independence contradicts that, and the consequent is false because the inherence of corporeal form in hyle has already been proven by demonstration, so the second alternative is determined, i.e., its dependence in itself on hyle, so no individual of corporeal form is free from hyle, thus it is shown that every body is composed of hyle and form. If it is said: The corporeal form is not dependent for its existence on hyle, rather the opposite; it will come in the discussion of the correlation between hyle and form that hyle depends on form for its existence and persistence, and form does not depend on it for its existence, otherwise circularity would occur, but it depends on it for its individuation and configuration. I say: The meaning of 'essential dependence' here according to the scholars is dependence in terms of absolute individuality, not in terms of quiddity, and there is no contradiction between the two statements because dependence is at the level of individuality and independence is at the level of quiddity; yes, if independence and dependence were at the same level, that would follow.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a demonstrative proof that all bodies are composed of hyle (matter) and form (corporeal form). The argument proceeds as follows: (1) We have already established that a given individual body (e.g., a specific body) is composed of hyle and form. (2) The corporeal nature (al-tabi'a al-miqdariyya) is either self-sufficient and does not require a substrate, or it is not self-sufficient. (3) The first possibility is impossible because if it were self-sufficient, it could not inhere in a substrate, since inherence necessarily implies dependence on the substrate. But we have already proven by demonstration that corporeal form does inhere in hyle. Therefore, the second possibility is true: the corporeal form depends essentially on a substrate, which is hyle. (4) Consequently, every individual instance of corporeal form is inseparable from hyle, so every body is composite. 

An objection is raised: In the discussion of the correlation (talazum) between hyle and form, it is said that hyle depends on form for its existence and persistence, and form does not depend on hyle for its existence (otherwise circularity would result); rather, form depends on hyle only for its individuation and configuration. This seems to contradict the claim that form depends on hyle. The answer (jawab) clarifies: The dependence referred to in the proof is 'essential dependence' (iftiqar dhati) in terms of absolute individuality (fardiyya mutlaqa), not in terms of quiddity (mahiyya). The form as a quiddity is independent, but as an individual existent it requires hyle as a subject. There is no contradiction because the two levels are distinct. If they were at the same level, there would be a problem. This illustrates a key principle in Islamic logic: distinctions of levels (maratib) resolve apparent contradictions.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'essential dependence' (al-iftiqar al-dhati) mean in the context of the proof?
A) Dependence on hyle for the quiddity of corporeal form.
B) Dependence on hyle in terms of absolute individuality, not quiddity.
C) Dependence on hyle for both existence and essence.
D) Independence from hyle in all respects.

Key Takeaway
Apparent contradictions in metaphysical claims are often resolved by noting that dependence and independence operate at different levels: quiddity vs individuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the debate on free will, consider the two statements: 'All actions are created by Allah' and 'Humans have free choice in their actions.' Use the level-distinction to show they are not contradictory if one refers to the level of creation (quiddity) and the other to the level of acquisition (individuality).
?
Find another example from Islamic disciplines (tafsir, hadith, kalam) where a distinction of levels (e.g., essence vs existence, general vs specific, potential vs actual) resolves an apparent contradiction. Write a short explanation.

Refuting Immaterial Spatial Dimension
Author refutes the Ishraiqiyyun claim that spatial dimension exists immaterially.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's two refutations of the Ishraiqiyyun claim that spatial dimension exists immaterially and identify the dialectical weakness in the argument.

Core Idea
Void has degrees, so it cannot be nothing; abstract dimension cannot be self-sufficient.

The Ishraiqiyyun (Illuminationists) claimed that spatial dimension exists independently of matter, arguing that if it were material, occupation of a body in it would entail impossible interpenetration. The author refutes this by showing that dimension cannot be pure nothing (because void admits more and less) and cannot be an abstract entity (because it would then be independent of a subject, contradicting its observed dependence on matter). However, he concedes that the refutation relies on the assumption that material and abstract dimension share the same essence, which the opponent might deny.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
The author presents two ways to conceive of dimension abstracted from matter: either it is pure nothing (void) or an abstract entity. Identify these two options in the Arabic.
Look for the phrases 'لا سبيل إلى الأول' and 'لا سبيل إلى الثاني'.
2
Analyze the first refutation
The author argues that void cannot be pure nothing because it admits degrees: the void between two walls is smaller than between two cities. Since increase and decrease are properties of existents, void must exist. Reconstruct this reasoning in your own words.
Check the example: الخلاء بين الجدارين أقل من الخلاء بين المدينتين.
3
Analyze the second refutation
If dimension existed without matter, it would be self-sufficient and not need a subject. But we observe dimension always attached to matter, so its conjunction would be inexplicable. This is a contradiction (خلف). Understand why the author considers this a reductio.
The key premise: لو وجد البعد مجرداً عن الهيولى لكان لذاته غنياً عن المحل.
4
Identify the dialectical concession
The author notes that the whole argument presupposes essential similarity (تماثل) between material and abstract dimension. If the opponent denies this and claims specific difference (تباين نوعي), the refutation fails. Recognise this as a limitation of the argument.
The phrase: هذا موقوف على تماثل البعد المادي والمجرد في الماهية.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحيز
مجردا عن المادة، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأنه يكون خلاء أقل من خلاء، فإن الخلاء بين الجدارين أقل من الخلاء بين المدينتين، وما يقبل الزيادة والنقصان استحال أن يكون لا شيئا محضا ، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأنه لو وجد البعد مجردا عن الهيولى لكان لذاته غنيا عن المحل، فاستحال اقترانه به هذا خلف.
مجردا إنما قال الإشراقيون بتجرد البعد المكاني عن المادة؛ إذ لو كان ماديا لكان جسما، وللزم من حصول الجسم المتمكن فيه تداخل الجسمين، وهو محال بالبداهة. لأنه يكون خلاء حاصله أن المكان على تقدير كونه عبارة عن الخلاء لا يمكن أن يكون لا شيئا محضا كما هو مزعوم المتكلمين؛ لأن الخلاء بعضه يكون أقل من بعض كالخلاء بين الجدارين أقل من الخلاء بين المدينتين ولما كان الخلاء موصوفا بالزيادة والنقصان لا يمكن أن يكون لا شيئا محضا؛ لأن الاتصاف بالزيادة والنقصان من أحكام الموجودات وما ليس موجود لا يتصور فيه الزيادة والنقصان. هذا خلف هذا موقوف على تماثل البعد المادي والمجرد في الماهية، حتى يلزم من افتقار البعد المادي إلى المادة افتقار البعد المجرد إلى المادة؛ لامتناع تخلف مقتضى الماهية الواحدة في أفرادها، ولعل الخصم لا يسلم التماثل، ويقول بالتباين النوعي بينهما، فلا يلزم الخلف.
Chapter on Place. [Dimension] abstracted from matter: the first [way] is not possible because then there would be a void smaller than another void, for the void between two walls is smaller than the void between two cities, and what admits increase and decrease cannot be pure nothing. The second [way] is not possible because if dimension existed abstracted from hyle, it would be in itself independent of a subject, so its conjunction with it would be impossible; this is a contradiction. [Explanation:] The Ishraiqiyyun claimed the immateriality of spatial dimension from matter, for if it were material it would be a body, and then the occupation of a body in it would entail the interpenetration of two bodies, which is obviously impossible. Because the outcome is that space, if it is considered to be void, cannot be pure nothing as the mutakallimun claim, for some void is smaller than other void, like the void between two walls is smaller than the void between two cities. And since void is described by increase and decrease, it cannot be pure nothing, because being described by increase and decrease is a property of existents, and what is non-existent cannot be conceived of increase and decrease. This is a contradiction. This [argument] depends on the similarity of material and abstract dimension in essence, so that from the dependence of material dimension on matter, the dependence of abstract dimension on matter follows, because it is impossible for the requirement of a single essence to differ among its individuals. Perhaps the opponent does not concede the similarity and asserts a specific difference between them, so the contradiction does not follow.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the Ishraiqiyyun (Illuminationist) position that spatial dimension exists independently of matter. The author presents two possible ways to conceive of dimension abstracted from matter: either it is a pure void (nothing), or it is an abstract entity. He argues against both. For the first: if dimension were pure nothing, it could not admit degrees of increase and decrease, yet we observe that voids can be smaller or larger (e.g., between two walls vs. two cities). Therefore it cannot be non-existent. For the second: if dimension existed without matter, it would be self-sufficient and not require a subject, but in reality dimension is always found in matter; thus its conjunction with matter would be inexplicable, leading to contradiction. The author then notes that this argument relies on the assumption that material and abstract dimension share the same essence (so that what applies to one applies to the other). The opponent might deny this essential similarity, claiming a specific difference (tabayun naw'i), which would block the conclusion. This shows the author's awareness of the dialectical context.

Key Terms
البعد
الخلاء
الهيولى
المجرد
التماثل
التباين النوعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that spatial dimension is pure nothing?
A) Because nothing cannot be a subject of change.
B) Because void can be greater or lesser, so it must be existent.
C) Because the Qur'an denies void.
D) Because dimensions are always material.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of immaterial dimension hinges on void having degrees and the assumed essential similarity with material dimension; the opponent may deny the latter and block the argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Reconstruct the author's first refutation using a contemporary example: Suppose someone claims that the emptiness in a cup is pure nothing. Use the author's reasoning to argue that emptiness has degrees (e.g., half-empty vs. fully empty) and therefore cannot be nothing. Write a short dialogue.
?
Task 2: In the kalam argument for the existence of a Creator, one premise is that bodies are temporally originated. If an opponent denies that bodies share the same essence regarding origination (tabayun naw'i), how would you respond? Use the author's concession about essential similarity to structure your reply.

Proving the Existence of Time: The Common Measure Argument
Author proves time exists by comparing two motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument for the existence of time from two motions of different speeds and identify the logical principle that distinguishes the common from the particular.

Core Idea
Time is not motion, body, or distance – it is the common measure that makes comparison possible.

This lesson unpacks a classic proof for the existence of time from the Hidayat al-Hikma. The author asks us to imagine two motions starting and ending together, one fast and one slow. By analyzing what they share and what they do not, we arrive at a separate reality: time. This argument is foundational for discussions of time in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Set up the thought experiment
Imagine two motions beginning and ending together, one faster and one slower. The faster covers more distance, the slower less.
These motions are assumed to be continuous and simultaneous in start and stop.
2
Identify what is common
Between the start and stop, there is an interval that measures both motions. This interval is the same for both.
This common interval is not dependent on the specifics of either motion.
3
Rule out motion, body, and distance
The fast and slow motions differ in distance covered, the moving bodies are different, and the motions themselves are different. The common interval cannot be any of these because they are all particular to each motion.
The principle: 'what is common is other than what distinguishes' (ما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف).
4
Apply the principle of common vs. distinguishing
Since the common interval is not the distance, body, or motion, it must be something else. That something else is time.
This is an immediate inference, not a syllogism.
5
State the conclusion
Therefore, time exists as a separate reality that measures motion.
The author goes on to discuss the quiddity and eternity of time, but the existence is proven here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الزمان إذا فرضنا حركة واقعة في مسافة على مقدار من السرعة، وابتدأت معها حركة أخرى أبطأ منها، واتفقتا في الأخذ والترك، وجدت البطيئة قاطعة لمسافة أقل من مسافة السريعة والسريعة قاطعة لمسافة أكثر منها، وإذا كان كذلك كان بين أخذ السريعة. مستفادة فيه إشارة إلى أن القاسر ليس فاعلا للحركة في الحركة القسرية، كما يتوهم في بادي الرأي في الحجر المرمي إلى فوق أن الرامي هو الفاعل للحركة القسرية، بل هو معد الطبعه والفاعل طبيعة بمشاركة القاسر . فصل المقصود في هذا الفصل إثبات أمور ثلاثة الأول وجود الزمان، والثاني: بيان حقيقته وماهيته والثالث بيان أزليته وأبديته وأثبت وجوده أولا بأن يفرض حركتان مختلفتان في السرعة والبطء، ومتفقتان في الأخذ والترك، فكانت السريعة قاطعة لمسافة أكثر والبطيئة لأقل، فبين أخذ تينك الحركتين وتركهما أمر واسع لقطع المسافتين يتقدر به الحركتان، وذلك الأمر الواسع سواء فيهما، وليس ذلك الأمر نفس المسافة؛ لاختلافهما، ولا عين المتحركين لاختلافهما أيضا، ولا نفس الحركتين لاختلافهما، ومن المعلوم أن ما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف، فتعين أن ما سوى الحركات والمتحركات والمسافات أمر آخر يسع لتلك الأمور، وهو الزمان.
Section on time: If we suppose a motion occurring in a distance at a certain speed, and another slower motion begins with it, and they agree in starting and stopping, then the slower is found to cover a distance less than the distance of the faster, and the faster covers more distance. When this is so, between the start... It contains an indication that the coercer is not the agent of motion in forced motion, as one might initially think in the stone thrown upward that the thrower is the agent of forced motion, but rather he is the preparer of its nature, and the agent is nature with the participation of the coercer. Section: The purpose of this section is to establish three matters: first, the existence of time; second, clarifying its reality and quiddity; third, clarifying its eternity and everlastingness. And [the author] first proved its existence by supposing two motions differing in speed and agreeing in starting and stopping; then the faster covered more distance and the slower less. So between the start of those two motions and their stop there is something extensive for covering the two distances, by which the two motions are measured. That extensive thing is common to both. It is not the distance itself because the distances are different; nor the moving bodies themselves because they are also different; nor the motions themselves because they are different. And it is known that what is common is other than what distinguishes. So it becomes certain that besides motions, moving bodies, and distances, there is another thing that encompasses those matters, and that is time.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of time, presented in the context of a physics section. The author first states the purpose: to prove three things about time: its existence, its reality/quiddity, and its eternity/everlastingness. The proof begins with a thought experiment: suppose two motions starting and ending together but with different speeds (one fast, one slow). The fast motion covers more distance, the slow less. Between the start and stop, there is a continuum that measures both motions. This continuum is common to both motions. It cannot be the distance itself because the distances are different; nor the moving bodies because they are different; nor the motions because they are different. Since the common element is not any of these, it must be something else, which is time. The argument uses the principle that what is common is distinct from what distinguishes (ما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف). This is a logical argument (immediate inference from comparison of motions). The passage also includes an aside (possibly interpolated) reiterating that in forced motion, the coercer is not the agent but the preparer. However, the main focus is the proof. Examples are from the Islamic sciences (motions of bodies, distances).

Key Terms
زمان (zamān): Time; the common measure that encompasses two motions of different speeds, distinct from motions, bodies, and distances. [Note: Standard definition in Islamic philosophy; consistent with Aristotelian physics.]
أخذ وترك (akhdh wa tark): Start and stop (of motion); used to define the temporal interval being measured. [Note: Common expression in kinematics; no significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that time is not the same as motion, moving body, or distance?
A) Because they are all different for fast and slow motions
B) Because time is eternal
C) Because time is unextended
D) Because the motions are forced

Key Takeaway
Time is a real, common measure that underlies all motions, distinct from the specifics of each motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider two moving objects: a bird flying and a person walking, both moving from point A to point B but at different speeds and starting/stopping together. According to the argument in this passage, what common reality measures both motions? Identify the steps of the argument in this scenario.
?
Read the following statement from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa: 'Time is the measure of motion in respect of priority and posteriority.' Compare this definition with the argument from Hidayat al-Hikma. Does Ibn Sina's definition resonate with the argument's conclusion? Explain.

Conditional Proof that Time Has No Beginning or End
Author proves time's infinitude via conditional proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional proof (qiyas khalf) for time's beginninglessness and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Time cannot have a beginning because that would imply time before time.

The author employs a proof by contradiction (khalf) to demonstrate that time has no beginning and no end. This is a classic conditional argument: if time had a beginning, then there would be time before time, which is impossible. By tracing the logical steps, you will see how this type of argument works in an Islamic philosophical context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional assumption
Assume time has a beginning. Then its non-existence before its existence is a 'priority' (qabliyya) that does not coexist with posteriority.
2
Classify the type of priority
The author states that any priority that does not coexist with posteriority is temporal. So this priority would itself be a time.
3
Derive the contradiction
That would mean there is time before time, which is a contradiction (khalf).
4
Conclude the negation
Since the assumption leads to a contradiction, the assumption must be false. Therefore, time has no beginning.
The same reasoning applies to the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الزمان لا بداية له ولا نهاية له؛ لأنه لو كان له بداية لكان عدمه قبل وجوده قبلية لا توجد مع البعدية، وكل قبلية لا توجد مع البعدية فهي زمانية، فيكون قبل الزمان زمان هذا خلف ولو كان له نهاية لكان عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية لا توجد مع القبلية، فتكون زمانية، فيكون بعد الزمان زمان هذا خلف. وقد يظن من أزلية الزمان وأبديته أن الزمان واجب الوجود، وهذا الظن فاسد؛ لأن الواجب ما يمتنع عليه جميع أنحاء العدم بالنظر إلى ذاته لا ما يمتنع عليه النحو الخاص من العدم والزمان إنما يمتنع عليه العدم السابق على الوجود اللاحق، ولا يلزم من امتناع العدم المقيد بالقبلية والبعدية امتناع مطلق العدم حتى يلزم كونه واجب الوجود، والزمان من أضعف الموجودات حيث لا قرار له، فكيف يكون واجب الوجود الذي هو أقوى الموجودات بقاء وثباتا؟
Indeed, time has no beginning and no end. Because if it had a beginning, then its non-existence before its existence would be a priority that does not coexist with posteriority, and every priority that does not coexist with posteriority is temporal. So there would be time before time, which is a contradiction. And if it had an end, then its non-existence after its existence would be a posteriority that does not coexist with priority, and it would be temporal. So there would be time after time, which is a contradiction. And one might suppose from the beginninglessness and everlastingness of time that time is necessary existence. But this supposition is false, because the necessary being is that for which all modes of non-existence are impossible with respect to its essence, not that for which a particular mode of non-existence is impossible. Time only has the impossibility of non-existence before subsequent existence, and the impossibility of non-existence restricted by priority and posteriority does not entail the impossibility of absolute non-existence such that it would be necessary existence. And time is among the weakest existents since it has no permanence, so how could it be the necessary being which is the strongest of existents in permanence and constancy?
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage provides a proof by contradiction (qiyas khalf) that time has no beginning or end. The author first assumes time has a beginning: then its non-existence before its existence would be a 'priority' (qabliyya) that does not coexist with 'posteriority' (ba'diyya). Such a priority is temporal, so there would be time before time, a contradiction. The same reasoning applies to an end. Then the author addresses a potential misconception: one might think that because time is beginningless and endless, it is necessary being (wajib al-wujud). This is refuted by distinguishing between absolute impossibility of non-existence (which defines the Necessary Being) and mere impossibility of a specific non-existence (e.g., non-existence before existence). Time only lacks non-existence in that specific sense, not absolutely. Moreover, time is weak and impermanent, unlike the Necessary Being which is most permanent. The example from kalam: the world's temporal origination implies it is not necessary being; time's eternalness does not make it necessary.

Key Terms
قبلية
بعدية
خلف
زمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about a 'priority' (qabliyya) that does not coexist with posteriority?
A) It is impossible.
B) It is temporal.
C) It is eternal.
D) It is necessary.

Key Takeaway
Time's lack of beginning is proven by showing that a beginning would imply time before time, a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid conditional proof (qiyas khalf) to show that time has no end, using the same structure as the proof for no beginning.
?
Identify the antecedent and consequent in the part of the passage proving no end.

Distinguishing Eternal from Necessary: The Case of Time
Author refutes the notion that time's eternity implies necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the impossibility of a specific mode of non-existence and absolute impossibility of non-existence, as applied to the concept of Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Eternal duration does not make time necessary; only absolute impossibility of non-existence qualifies.

After proving time's beginninglessness and endlessness, the author anticipates a confusion: since time is eternal, one might think it is necessary being (wajib al-wujud). The author refutes this by clarifying the definition of necessary being: it is that for which all modes of non-existence are impossible. Time only lacks non-existence before its existence and after its existence, but it could still not exist in some other sense. Moreover, time is weak and impermanent, unlike the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misconception
The misconception is that eternal time is necessary being. The text says: 'One might suppose from the beginninglessness and everlastingness of time that time is necessary existence.'
2
Restate the definition of necessary being
The author defines necessary being as that for which all modes of non-existence (جميع أنحاء العدم) are impossible with respect to its essence.
3
Analyze which non-existence is impossible for time
Time only has the impossibility of non-existence before existence (i.e., it cannot be non-existent before it exists) and after existence (it cannot be non-existent after it exists). Other modes of non-existence are not impossible.
4
Contrast with the Necessary Being
The Necessary Being's non-existence is impossible absolutely, not just a particular mode. Also, time is weak and impermanent, unlike the Necessary Being which is most permanent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الزمان لا بداية له ولا نهاية له؛ لأنه لو كان له بداية لكان عدمه قبل وجوده قبلية لا توجد مع البعدية، وكل قبلية لا توجد مع البعدية فهي زمانية، فيكون قبل الزمان زمان هذا خلف ولو كان له نهاية لكان عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية لا توجد مع القبلية، فتكون زمانية، فيكون بعد الزمان زمان هذا خلف. وقد يظن من أزلية الزمان وأبديته أن الزمان واجب الوجود، وهذا الظن فاسد؛ لأن الواجب ما يمتنع عليه جميع أنحاء العدم بالنظر إلى ذاته لا ما يمتنع عليه النحو الخاص من العدم والزمان إنما يمتنع عليه العدم السابق على الوجود اللاحق، ولا يلزم من امتناع العدم المقيد بالقبلية والبعدية امتناع مطلق العدم حتى يلزم كونه واجب الوجود، والزمان من أضعف الموجودات حيث لا قرار له، فكيف يكون واجب الوجود الذي هو أقوى الموجودات بقاء وثباتا؟
Indeed, time has no beginning and no end. And time is among the weakest existents since it has no permanence, so how could it be the necessary being which is the strongest of existents in permanence and constancy?
Source pages p. 35

This passage provides a proof by contradiction (qiyas khalf) that time has no beginning or end. The author first assumes time has a beginning: then its non-existence before its existence would be a 'priority' (qabliyya) that does not coexist with 'posteriority' (ba'diyya). Such a priority is temporal, so there would be time before time, a contradiction. The same reasoning applies to an end. Then the author addresses a potential misconception: one might think that because time is beginningless and endless, it is necessary being (wajib al-wujud). Time only lacks non-existence in that specific sense, not absolutely. Moreover, time is weak and impermanent, unlike the Necessary Being which is most permanent. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proof that the Celestial Sphere is Circular
Author proves the celestial sphere is circular

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that the celestial sphere is circular by analysing the properties of the fixed directions and the indivisibility argument.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere's circularity follows from the fixed, indivisible nature of the two absolute directions.

This passage presents a philosophical demonstration that the celestial sphere (falak) must be circular. The author builds on earlier lessons that established the existence of absolute directions (above and below). Here, he uses the properties of these directions—existence, position, and indivisibility—to deduce the shape of the heavenly body. Follow the steps to see how logic ties to cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'circular body'
The passage first defines a circular body as one with a center point such that all lines from it to the surface are equal. This definition sets the target: we must show the celestial sphere fits this.
This geometric definition is crucial for the conclusion.
2
Identify the two fixed directions
The author asserts there are two unchanging directions: above and below. Each is existent (mawjuda) and has a position (dhat wad'), otherwise pointing or moving toward them would be impossible.
These directions are not relative; they are absolute as established previously.
3
Analyze the indivisibility claim
Each direction must be indivisible (ghayr munqasima). The author reasons: if a direction were divisible, a mover reaching the nearer part would either move from it (contradicting the farther part being the origin) or to it (contradicting the nearer part being the goal). This reductio forces indivisibility.
The argument uses the definition of direction as the origin or goal of motion.
4
Connect directions to spherical shape
With two fixed, point-like directions, any body moving in relation to them must be spherical. The directions define an axis, and motion must be uniform around a center, yielding a sphere.
The author concludes: 'Whenever it is so, the celestial sphere is circular.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات كون الفلك مستديرا. مستديرا: الجسم المستدير هو الجسم الذي يفرض في وسطه نقطة، ويكون جميع الخطوط الخارجة منها متساوية ويقال لتلك النقطة مركز، ولسطح ذلك الجسم محيط، وللجسم كرة، هكذا الفلك. بيانه أن ههنا جهتين لا تتبدلان إحداهما فوق والأخرى تحت، وكل واحدة منهما موجودة ذات وضع غير منقسمة في امتداد مأخذ الحركة، ومتى كان كذلك كان الفلك مستديرا. وإنما قلنا: إن الجهة موجودة ذات وضع؛ لأنها لو لم تكن كذلك لما أمكنت الإشارة إليها ولما أمكن اتجاه المتحرك إليها، وإنما قلنا: إنها غير منقسمة؛ لأنها لو انقسمت ووصل المتحرك إلى أقرب الجزئين من الجهة وتحرك، فإما أن يتحرك من المقصد أو إلى المقصد، فإن تحرك من المقصد لم يكن أبعد الجزئين من الجهة، وإن تحرك إلى المقصد لم يكن أقرب الجزئين من الجهة. اتجاه المتحرك لأن القرب والوصول إلى المعدوم لا يجوز؛ لأن المعدوم ليس بشيء، والقرب والوصول يستدعيان الوجود. غير منقسمة: أي الجهة غير منقسمة لا يقع فيها الحركة؛ لأن الجهة ما منه حركة أو ما إليه الحركة، فلو كانت منقسمة في ذلك الجانب فمتى وصل المتحرك إلى أقرب الجزئين من الجهة، فلا يخلو إما يرجع أو يذهب، فإن رجع فلا يكون أبعد الجزئين من الجهة؛ لأن أبعد الجزئين ليس بشيء منهما، وإن ذهب فلا يكون أقرب الجزئين من الجهة؛ لأنه ليس مما منه الحركة بل صار داخلا في المسافة هذه.
Chapter on Proving that the Celestial Sphere is Circular. 'Circular': a circular body is a body in the middle of which a point is assumed, such that all lines emerging from it are equal; that point is called the center, the surface of that body is called the circumference, and the body is called a sphere; thus is the celestial sphere. Its proof: here there are two directions that do not change: one above and the other below. Each of them is existent, having a position, and indivisible in the extension of the direction of motion. Whenever it is so, the celestial sphere is circular. We only said that the direction is existent and having a position because if it were not so, then pointing to it would not be possible, nor would the direction of the mover towards it be possible. And we only said that it is indivisible because if it were divided, and the mover reached the nearer of the two parts of the direction and moved, then either it moves from the goal or to the goal. If it moves from the goal, then it is not the farther of the two parts from the direction; and if it moves to the goal, then it is not the nearer of the two parts from the direction. The direction of the mover [is necessary] because proximity and reaching to non-existence are not permissible, since non-existence is nothing, and proximity and reaching require existence. 'Indivisible': meaning that the direction is indivisible, and motion does not occur in it because the direction is that from which motion begins or to which motion goes. So if it were divisible in that side, then when the mover reaches the nearer of the two parts of the direction, it inevitably either returns or goes on. If it returns, then it is not the farther of the two parts from the direction because the farther part is not something from them; and if it goes on, then it is not the nearer of the two parts from the direction because it is not that from which motion begins, but rather it enters into this distance.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (burhan) that the celestial sphere (falak) is circular (mustadir). The chapter heading states the claim. Immediately, the commentator defines 'circular body' as one with a center and equal radii, identifying the celestial sphere as such. Then the proof is given: there are two fixed directions, above and below. Each direction is existent (mawjuda), has a position (dhat wad'), and is indivisible (ghayr munqasima) in the direction of motion. The commentator explains that if a direction were not existent and positional, it could not be pointed to or be a goal of motion. The indivisibility claim is defended with a reductio: if the direction were divisible, a mover approaching it would face a contradiction—either it moves from the nearer part (then it is not moving from the farther) or to the nearer part (then it is not moving from the nearer? Actually the argument is: if the mover reaches the nearer part, then if it moves from that nearer part as the starting point, it cannot be moving from the farther part; if it moves towards the nearer part as the goal, then it cannot be moving towards the farther part. This shows that the direction must be a point-like extremity, not extended. The commentator also notes that direction is defined as the origin or goal of motion, so it cannot be divisible because motion would then occur within it. The conclusion: given these properties of the two fixed directions, the celestial sphere (which is the body that moves in relation to these directions) must be circular. This is because the fixed directions define the axis, and the motion must be uniform around a center.

مستدير
جهة
غير منقسمة

A) Because if they were divisible, the mover would have to choose between two sub-directions, leading to a contradiction in motion.
B) Because the direction is a three-dimensional region that cannot be divided.
C) Because the direction is defined as the midpoint of the sphere.
D) Because the mover cannot reach a divided direction without splitting into two movers.

Key Takeaway
The proof relies on the fact that direction is a point-like terminus, not an extended region, which forces the celestial body to be spherical.

Reconstructing the Celestial Sphere Syllogism
Syllogistic proof of celestial sphere's incorruptibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the philosophers' categorical syllogism proving the celestial sphere's immunity to generation and corruption, and identify its major, minor, and middle terms.

Core Idea
A syllogism's validity depends on its form, not its conformity to Islamic creed.

This lesson examines a classic philosophical proof used to argue that the celestial sphere is eternal and unchanging. The argument is structured as a categorical syllogism, which we will dissect term by term. After understanding the logical structure, we will note how Muslim theologians objected on scriptural grounds, highlighting the tension between Greek logic and Islamic revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (natija)
Find the claim being proved: 'the celestial sphere does not accept generation and corruption.' This is the conclusion of the syllogism.
The conclusion is stated at the very beginning.
2
Locate the minor premise (sughra)
The minor premise is a statement about the celestial sphere: 'it is a determiner of directions.' This is the subject of the conclusion.
The author says 'the minor premise, its establishment has passed.'
3
Locate the major premise (kubra)
The major premise is a general rule: 'nothing that is a determiner of directions accepts generation and corruption.' This is the predicate of the conclusion.
The author provides a detailed justification for this premise.
4
Identify the middle term (hadd al-awsat)
The middle term connects the two premises: 'determiner of directions' appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
The middle term is 'muhaddid al-jihat'.
5
Determine figure and mood
This syllogism follows the first figure (subject-middle, middle-predicate). The mood is Celarent (universal negative major, universal affirmative minor, universal negative conclusion).
First figure requires the middle term to be subject of the major and predicate of the minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك لا يقبل الكون والفساد والخرق والالتيام أما أنه لا يقبل الكون والفساد فلأنه محدد الجهات، ولا شيء من محدد الجهات يقبل الكون والفساد، أما الصغرى، فقد مر تقريرها، وأما الكبرى فلأن كل ما يقبل الكون والفساد فلصورته الحادثة حيز طبعي ولصورته الفاسدة حيز آخر طبعي؛ لما بينا أن كل جسم فله حيز طبعي، وكل ما هذا شأنه فهو قابل للحركة المستقيمة؛ لأن الصورة
لا يقبل إلخ اعلم أن من الأجسام ما يقبل الكون والفساد كالعناصر، وسيجيء في العنصريات أن العناصر بعضها ينقلب بعضا بأن يفسد صورته ويحدث صورة أخرى، ومنها ما لا يقبل الكون والفساد والأفلاك عند الفلاسفة مما لا يقبل الكون والفساد وعندنا أهل الإسلام كل جسم سواء كان فلكا أو عنصرا قابل للكون والفساد والخرق والالتيام وقد نطق الكتاب بخرق السماء يوم القيامة، وأما اليوم، فما لها من فطور كما دل عليه الكتاب أيضا.
فله حيز لأن الحيز الطبعي ما يقتضيه صورته النوعية ولما كانت الصورتان مختلفتين، كان حيزاهما مختلفين أيضا؛ لامتناع اجتماع جسمين في حيز واحد، وإلا لزم التداخل. هذا شأنه كل جسم يتوارد عليه الصورتان فذلك الجسم قابل للحركة المستقيمة؛ لأن الصورة الكائنة لا تخلو إما أن تكون في حيز طبعي أو في حيز غريب أي غير طبعي، فإن كانت في حيز غريب كانت تقتضي أن تذهب بالحركة المستقيمة إلى حيزها الطبعي؛ لأن الحركة المستديرة لا تقتضي بقاء الجسم في ذلك الحيز، فيلزم كون الفلك قابلا للحركة المستقيمة وهو باطل، وإن كانت في حيز طبعي فالصورة الفاسدة كانت حين وجودها في حيز غريب فكانت تقتضي حين وجودها أن تخرج ذلك الحيز الغريب وتروح إلى الحيز الطبعي وما ذلك إلا بالحركة المستقيمة، فيلزم أيضا كون الفلك قابلا للحركة المستقيمة، وهو كما ترى.
وأما أنه لا يقبل الخرق والالتيام فلأن ذلك أيضا إنما يحصل بالحركة المستقيمة، والفلك لا يقبل الحركة المستقيمة، فلا يقبل الخرق والالتيام.
Chapter on that the celestial sphere does not accept generation, corruption, tearing, and stitching. As for that it does not accept generation and corruption, it is because it is a determiner of directions, and nothing that is a determiner of directions accepts generation and corruption. As for the minor premise, its establishment has passed; and as for the major premise, it is because everything that accepts generation and corruption has for its generated form a natural place and for its corrupted form another natural place; because we have shown that every body has a natural place, and everything with this property is susceptible to rectilinear motion; because the form... 'etc.' Know that among bodies there are those that accept generation and corruption, such as the elements, and it will come in the elemental [section] that some elements transform into others by corrupting their form and generating another form. And among them are those that do not accept generation and corruption, and the celestial spheres according to the philosophers are among those that do not accept generation and corruption. And according to us, the people of Islam, every body, whether celestial sphere or element, is susceptible to generation, corruption, tearing, and stitching. The Book [Qur'an] has spoken of the tearing of the sky on the Day of Resurrection; and as for today, it has no cracks, as the Book also indicates. So it has a place because the natural place is what its specific form necessitates. And when the two forms are different, their two places are also different due to the impossibility of two bodies occupying one place; otherwise overlapping would be necessary. This property: every body upon which the two forms alternate is susceptible to rectilinear motion. Because the generated form is either in a natural place or in an alien place, i.e., non-natural. If it is in an alien place, it would necessitate going by rectilinear motion to its natural place, because circular motion does not necessitate the body remaining in that place. Therefore it follows that the celestial sphere is susceptible to rectilinear motion, which is false. And if it is in a natural place, then the corrupted form at the time of its existence was in an alien place, so at that time it necessitated exiting that alien place and going to the natural place, and that is only by rectilinear motion. Therefore it also follows that the celestial sphere is susceptible to rectilinear motion, and that is as you see. And as for that it does not accept tearing and stitching, it is because that also only occurs by rectilinear motion, and the celestial sphere does not accept rectilinear motion, so it does not accept tearing and stitching.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic proof that the celestial sphere (al-falak) does not undergo generation/corruption (kawn wa fasad) or tearing/stitching (kharaq wa iltiyam). The author begins by stating the claim and then provides a categorical syllogism: The sphere is a determiner of directions (muhaddid al-jihat), and nothing that determines directions accepts generation/corruption. The minor premise (that the sphere determines directions) is said to have been established earlier. The major premise is justified: anything that accepts generation/corruption has different natural places (hayz tabi'i) for its generated and corrupted forms, which necessitates rectilinear motion—but the sphere does not move rectilinearly. The commentary (اعلم) then distinguishes bodies that accept change (elements) from those that don't (spheres according to philosophers). The author inserts an important critical note: in Islamic belief, all bodies—including the spheres—are created and subject to change, citing the Quranic verses about the sky being torn on Judgment Day and having no cracks now. The detailed proof at the end shows that if the sphere had two different forms, it would require rectilinear motion, which is impossible for the sphere (as it moves circularly). Therefore, it cannot have generation/corruption. Similarly, tearing and stitching require rectilinear motion, so they are also impossible for the sphere. The argument is complete and self-contained.

كون وفساد
حيز طبعي
حركة مستقيمة

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism proves its conclusion if the premises are true; philosophers used this to argue for the celestial sphere's incorruptibility, but Muslim theologians challenged the premises using revelation.

Disjunctive Syllogism: Proving the Celestial Sphere's Perpetual Motion
Author proves the sphere's motion is perpetual circular motion using a disjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and validate a disjunctive syllogism from a kalami falsafa argument and identify its premise structure.

Core Idea
Time demands a perpetual motion; only circular motion can be eternal.

In this passage, the author of Hidayat al-Hikma harnesses a disjunctive argument to prove that the celestial sphere moves in perpetual circular motion. The argument hinges on the nature of time (zaman) as eternal and the impossibility of infinite rectilinear motion. We will dissect the logical structure and see how it exemplifies the use of the disjunctive syllogism in Islamic cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author aims to prove that the celestial sphere moves circularly perpetually. Find the conclusion stated in the Arabic: that the sphere is actually moving with circular motion perpetually.
2
Extract the disjunctive premise
The argument begins: 'the perpetual motion that preserves time is either rectilinear or circular.' This is the major disjunctive premise. Identify both disjuncts in the text.
Disjuncts: 'haraka mustaqima' and 'haraka mustadira'
3
Analyze the elimination of rectilinear motion
The author eliminates rectilinear motion via a dilemma: it either goes to infinity (requires infinite distance, impossible) or returns (requires cessation and restart, not perpetual). Verify that both horns of the dilemma are impossible according to Aristotelian physics.
Infinite distance is impossible because all dimensions are finite (as established earlier).
4
Conclude the remaining disjunct
With rectilinear motion eliminated, the only remaining possibility is circular motion. Therefore, the motion preserving time must be circular and perpetual. This is a valid disjunctive syllogism (modus tollendo ponens).
The conclusion is stated implicitly; the passage cuts off before stating it explicitly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك يتحرك على الاستدارة دائما
لأن الحركة الحافظة للزمان إما أن تكون مستقيمة أو مستديرة، لا جائز
ذلك لأن الخرق لا يكون بدون تباعد الأجزاء عن أحيازها، وذلك يستلزم الحركة المستقيمة، والالتيام لا يكون بدون تقارب الأجزاء في أحيازها بعد تباعدها، والتقارب بعد التباعد يستلزم الحركة المستقيمة. فصل إلخ قد ثبت في الفصل السابق كون الفلك قابلا للحركة المستديرة، وههنا يثبت كونه متحركا بالفعل بالحركة المستديرة دائما تقريره: أنك قد علمت في بحث الزمان أنه مقدار الحركة، وأنه أزلي وأبدي، فلا بد له من حركة أزلية وأبدية؛ لكونه قائما بها ومقدارا لها، وإن لم توجد حركة دائمة انعدم الزمان لانعدام محله فالحركة الدائمة الحافظة لوجود الزمان لا تخلو إما أن تكون مستقيمة أو مستديرة، لا جائز أن تكون مستقيمة؛ لأنها إما أن تذهب إلى غير النهاية أو ترجع، والأول محال وإلا لزم وجود بعد غير متناه؛ لأن الحركة لا بد لها من مسافة، والمسافة بعد قائم بالجسم، فلو ذهبت الحركة إلى غير النهاية للزم وجود مسافة غير متناهية، وهو محال لوجوب تناهي الأبعاد كما مر والثاني أيضا محال؛ لأنها لو رجعت لكانت منتهية إلى حد، فتكون الحركة الذاهبة منعدمة، ثم تحدث الحركة الراجعة؛ لأن بين –
Chapter on that the celestial sphere moves circularly perpetually. Because the motion that preserves time is either rectilinear or circular, not possible... That is because tearing does not occur without separation of parts from their places, and that necessitates rectilinear motion, and stitching does not occur without approximation of parts in their places after separation, and approximation after separation necessitates rectilinear motion. Chapter etc. It was established in the previous chapter that the celestial sphere is capable of circular motion, and here it is established that it is actually moving with circular motion perpetually. Its presentation: you have learned in the discussion of time that it is the measure of motion, and that it is eternal and everlasting, so there must be an eternal and everlasting motion for it, because it is subsistent by it and a measure for it. And if a perpetual motion did not exist, time would cease due to the cessation of its subject. So the perpetual motion that preserves the existence of time is either rectilinear or circular. It is not possible that it be rectilinear, because it either goes to infinity or returns. The first is impossible; otherwise there would be an infinite distance, because motion must have a distance, and distance is a dimension subsisting in the body. If motion went to infinity, an infinite distance would be necessary, and that is impossible because distances must be finite, as has passed. The second is also impossible, because if it returned, it would terminate at a limit, so the going motion would cease, and then the returning motion would occur; because between –
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the celestial sphere moves in perpetual circular motion. The argument is disjunctive: the motion that preserves time must be either rectilinear or circular; it cannot be rectilinear, so it must be circular. The author first restates that the sphere is capable of circular motion (from the previous chapter) and now shows it is actual. The link to time (zaman) is crucial: time is the measure (miqdar) of motion and is eternal (azali) and everlasting (abadi), so there must be an eternal motion to sustain it. If there were no perpetual motion, time would cease. The author then eliminates rectilinear motion via a dilemma: if rectilinear, it either goes to infinity or returns. Going to infinity would entail an infinite distance, which is impossible (as finite bodies can only have finite dimensions). Returning would mean the motion ends and restarts, which cannot be perpetual because the going motion ceases. Therefore, only circular motion remains. The argument is cut off before completing the conclusion, but the inference is clear: circular motion is the only option. The passage includes a brief interjection about tearing and stitching, likely a remnant from the previous chapter, but it does not disrupt the main argument.

Key Terms
زمان (zaman): Time; defined as the measure (miqdar) of motion, and argued to be eternal and everlasting, thus requiring a perpetual motion. [Note: In Aristotelian physics, time is the measure of motion; Islamic philosophers (falasifa) often hold its eternity, while theologians (mutakallimun) reject it.]
حركة مستديرة (haraka mustadira): Circular motion; the only type of motion that can be perpetual without change of place, typical of celestial spheres. [Note: Standard; contrasted with rectilinear motion as imperfect.]
أزلي وأبدي (azali wa abadi): Eternal and everlasting; time is described as having no beginning (azali) and no end (abadi). [Note: In falsafa, the world and time are eternal; in kalam, only God is eternal.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the perpetual motion preserving time cannot be rectilinear?
A) Because rectilinear motion is only possible for elements, not for celestial spheres.
B) Because rectilinear motion would require either an infinite distance or a return, both of which are impossible.
C) Because the Quran states that the heavens move in circles.
D) Because time would stop if the motion were rectilinear.

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive syllogism, when combined with the eternity of time, forces the conclusion that celestial motion must be circular and perpetual.

?

Circular Motion and the Continuity of Time
Proves that only circular motion can preserve time continuity, so the celestial sphere moves in a circle perpetually

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical structure of the proof that the celestial sphere moves circularly by showing that straight motion would lead to atomic time, and reconstruct the argument as a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Only circular motion prevents time from disintegrating into atoms.

This lesson unpacks a key argument in Islamic cosmology that the celestial sphere must move in a circle continuously. The author argues that if the motion were straight, time would be composed of indivisible instants, which is absurd. Therefore, the motion that preserves time must be circular and perpetual. We will follow the logical steps of this reductio ad absurdum and see how it establishes the nature of celestial motion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting assumption
The passage begins by noting that between two instants there must be a time of rest if motion were straight. This is based on earlier arguments about rectilinear motion requiring rest.
2
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
If motion were straight, then between two instants there would be no intervening motion, so instants would follow each other directly, making time consisted of indivisible parts (atoms). This is considered absurd.
The absurdity is that time would be atomic, contradicting its continuous nature.
3
Use the correspondence principle
Since time corresponds to motion via distance (al-inṭibāq), atomic time would imply atomic distance, which is also impossible. Thus the supposition leads to multiple absurdities.
The correspondence (inṭibāq) is key: distance maps to time through motion.
4
Conclude the negation and the positive claim
Therefore, the motion that preserves time cannot be straight; it must be circular. Moreover, this circuit motion must be uninterrupted, otherwise time would be interrupted.
The final conclusion is that the celestial sphere moves circularly always.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك يتحرك منهما آنيا وجب أن يكون بين الآنين زمان لا يتحرك فيه الجسم، وإلا لزم تعاقب الآنين، فيكون الزمان مركبا من أجزاء لا تتجزى، ويلزم منه تركب المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزى؛ لانطباقها على الحركة، هذا خلف، فعلم أن الحركة الحافظة للزمان ليست مستقيمة، فتكون مستديرة، وهذه الحركة غير منقطعة وإلا لزم انقطاع الزمان، فإذن يكون الفلك يتحرك على الاستدارة دائما، وهو المطلوب.
Chapter that the celestial sphere moves. Since each of them is instantaneous, there must be between the two instants a time in which the body does not move; otherwise the succession of instants would be required, then time would be composed of indivisible parts, and it would follow that distance is composed of indivisible parts because it corresponds to motion; this is a contradiction. So it is known that the motion preserving time is not straight, thus it is circular. And this motion is uninterrupted, otherwise time would be interrupted. Therefore the celestial sphere moves circularly perpetually, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the celestial sphere must move in a circle continuously. It builds on the previous argument that between opposite straight motions there is a time of rest. If motion were straight, it would require rest and thus time would be composed of indivisible parts (atoms of time), which is absurd. Therefore the motion that preserves the continuity of time cannot be straight; it must be circular. Moreover, this circular motion must be perpetual, because if it stopped, time would be interrupted. Hence the celestial sphere is in continuous circular motion.
