The Grain and Mountain Objection: A Dialectical Analysis
Dialectical defense of a physical principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical structure of the grain and mountain objection, identifying the three-part exchange (objection, answer, critique) and evaluating its logical force.

Core Idea
A valid physical principle must survive even absurd-seeming objections.

This passage presents a classic dialectical move in Islamic physics: an objection is raised against a principle, an answer is offered, and a commentator refines the answer. The example of a thrown grain meeting a descending mountain tests the principle that rest must occur between any two rectilinear motions. By tracing the structure, you learn how kalām scholars defend their principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle under attack
Locate the statement that 'rest must occur between two rectilinear motions'. This is the claim the objector tries to refute.
The principle is stated at the beginning: 'وجوب السكون بين الحركتين المستقيمتين'.
2
Reconstruct the objection (mu'tarid)
Find the example: a grain thrown upward meets a descending mountain. According to the principle, the grain must rest at the meeting point. If the grain rests, so must the mountain – which is absurd. The objector uses this to deny the principle.
The objector's argument is: if rest is necessary between upward and downward motion, then at the meeting point both must rest; but the mountain does not rest, so the principle is false.
3
Analyze the author's answer (jawāb)
The author (al-muṣannif) distinguishes between essential and accidental motion: the grain is essentially at rest but accidentally moves with the mountain. Thus, no temporal rest occurs, only an instantaneous cessation.
The answer uses the analogy of a passenger on a ship: essentially still, accidentally moving.
4
Examine the commentator's critique (naqd)
The commentator (the present text) argues that the author's answer is insufficient because the objection implies temporal rest, not momentary. He then refines: 'momentary rest' is metaphorical; the grain never rests for a duration, so the mountain's motion is unaffected.
The commentator insists that rest in an instant is not true rest – it's merely the cessation of motion at that instant.
5
Determine the outcome of the dialectic
The commentator ultimately upholds the original principle by showing that the objection fails: no temporal rest occurs that would impede the mountain. The principle stands refined.
The outcome is that the principle is preserved, but the understanding of 'rest' is clarified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هداية: أوردها لدفع اشتباه يرد على وجوب السكون بين الحركتين المستقيمتين، لو وجب السكون بين حركتين مستقيمتين لزم أن يقف الجبل الهابط من علو حين ملاقاة حبة مرمية إلى فوق في جو السماء؛ لأن حركتها الصاعدة تنتهي بالسكون حين لاقت الجبل، فتقف الحبة المرمية زمانا بناء على وجوب السكون بين حركتها الصاعدة والهابطة فيلزم من وقوفها وقوف الجبل، وكل عاقل يعلم أن الجبل لا يقف في الهواء بإمساك الحبة، والجواب عنه أنه لا يلزم من سكون الحبة سكون الجبل؛ لأن الحبة المرمية وإن كانت ساكنة بالذات؛ لانتفاء ميل الرجوع فيها ولكنها متحركة بالعرض بواسطة الجبل في زمان لم يحدث فيه ميل الرجوع فيها، ولا منافاة بين السكون بالذات والتحرك بالعرض كجالس السفينة، فإنه ساكن بالذات ومتحرك بالعرض، ثم بعد زمان يحدث فيها ميل الرجوع فتحرك حينئذ بالذات، فلا تنتقض قاعدة وجوب السكون ولا يلزم سكون الجبل، وأما جواب المصنف محليه فليس بصاف؛ لأن بناء الإشكال على وجوب السكون زمانا بين حركتين، ولا شك أن بين وجوب السكون للحبة زمانا وبين حركة الجبل في ذلك الزمان ممانعة ظاهرة، إلا أن يقال : سكونها بالذات زمانا لا ينافي تحركها بالعرض في ذلك الزمان فلا يمانع حركة الجبل، فحينئذ هذا الجواب إلى ما قلنا من قبل، ويلغو ذكر يرجع سكون آني للحبة وعدم تنافيه تحركة زمانية للجبل. سكونها : فإن قيل: إن بإزاء سكون الحبة الآني حركة الجبل الآنية، فيكون بين سكونها الآني وحركته الآنية ممانعة، فيلزم سكون الجبل، فما يغني هذا الجواب، قلت: لا يتصور وجود الحركة في الآن؛ لأن معناه الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على سبيل التدريج، وكذلك السكون المقابل للحركة ولذا لا يقف الجسم في الآن بالحركة والسكون وأما قوله : 'سكونها آني' فمعناه انقطاع الحركة حين التلاقي، وسماه مجازا بالسكون. آني حادث في آن الملاقاة، ولا يستمر سكونها زمانا حتى يلزم سكون الجبل، بل تتحرك بالعرض بواسطة الجبل. نعم لو سكنت زمانا و لم تتحرك بالعرض في زمان حركة الجبل، لزم سكون الجبل.
Guidance: He brought it to remove a confusion that occurs regarding the necessity of rest between two rectilinear motions. If rest were obligatory between two rectilinear motions, it would necessitate that the descending mountain stops at the moment of meeting a grain thrown upward in the sky; because its ascending motion ends with rest when it meets the mountain. So the thrown grain stops for a time based on the necessity of rest between its ascending and descending motions, and from its stopping follows the stopping of the mountain. Every rational person knows that the mountain does not stop in the air by being held by the grain. The answer to this is that the stopping of the grain does not necessitate the stopping of the mountain, because the thrown grain, although it is stationary in itself (sākin bi-l-dhāt) due to the absence of a tendency for return, yet it moves accidentally (mutaharrik bi-l-'araḍ) by means of the mountain in a time in which no tendency for return has occurred. There is no incompatibility between being stationary in itself and moving accidentally, like the passenger of a ship: he is stationary in himself and moving accidentally. Then after a time, a tendency for return occurs in it, so it then moves in itself. Thus the rule of necessity of rest is not violated, nor does the stopping of the mountain follow. As for the author's answer on his part, it is not sound because the construction of the objection is based on the necessity of rest for a time between two motions. There is no doubt that between the necessity of rest for the grain for a time and the motion of the mountain in that time there is an apparent opposition. However, it could be said: its being stationary in itself for a time does not prevent its moving accidentally in that time, so it does not hinder the motion of the mountain. Then this answer is like what we said before, and the mention of the momentary rest of the grain and its non-incompatibility with the temporal motion of the mountain becomes void. If it is said: 'The momentary rest of the grain corresponds to the momentary motion of the mountain, so there is opposition between its momentary rest and its momentary motion, thus the stopping of the mountain follows. How does this answer help?' I say: 'Motion cannot be conceived to exist in an instant, because its meaning is exiting from potentiality to actuality gradually, and likewise rest opposite motion; therefore the body does not stop in an instant by motion and rest.' As for his statement 'its rest is momentary', it means the cessation of motion at the moment of meeting, and he metaphorically called it rest. It is a momentary occurrence in the instant of meeting, and its rest does not continue for a time such that the mountain's stopping would follow; rather it moves accidentally by means of the mountain. Yes, if it rested for a time and did not move accidentally in the time of the mountain's motion, then the mountain's stopping would follow.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) addressing an objection against the principle that rest must occur between two rectilinear motions. The objection uses the example of a grain thrown upward that meets a descending mountain at its apex. According to the principle, the grain must rest before descending, which would imply the mountain also stops—an obvious absurdity. The author's answer (jawāb) distinguishes between essential (bi-l-dhāt) and accidental (bi-l-'araḍ) motion: the grain is essentially stationary at the instant of turning, but accidentally moves with the mountain. This is likened to a passenger on a ship. The commentator (the present text) then critiques the author's response, arguing that the objection is based on a temporal rest, not an instantaneous one. He clarifies that 'momentary rest' is metaphorical—merely the cessation of motion at an instant—and that there is no actual rest in time. Therefore, the mountain's continuous motion is unaffected. Ultimately, the commentator upholds the principle of necessary rest between rectilinear motions, showing that the example does not refute it.

A dialectical objection can be answered by distinguishing essential from accidental motion, and a commentator may further refine the answer by clarifying the temporal status of rest.

Essential vs. Accidental Motion: A Conceptual Tool in Kalām Physics
Conceptual tool for resolving physical paradoxes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (bi-l-dhāt) and accidental (bi-l-'araḍ) motion and use this distinction to resolve apparent contradictions in physical arguments.

Core Idea
A body can be essentially at rest while accidentally in motion – like a passenger on a ship.

In kalām physics, the distinction between essential and accidental motion is crucial for resolving paradoxes. The grain-and-mountain debate hinges on this distinction: the grain is essentially at rest (because it lacks an internal tendency to return) but accidentally moves with the mountain. This lesson unpacks this conceptual tool and shows how it saves the principle of necessary rest.

Guided Path
1
Define essential motion (ḥaraka bi-l-dhāt)
Essential motion is motion that originates from the body's own intrinsic tendency (mayl). For example, a stone falling due to gravity has essential downward motion.
In the passage, the grain lacks a tendency for return, so its own motion is not essential after the peak.
2
Define accidental motion (ḥaraka bi-l-'araḍ)
Accidental motion occurs when a body is carried by another moving entity. The grain, though essentially at rest, is carried by the mountain during their contact.
The passenger on a ship is the classic analogy: he is essentially stationary but accidentally moves with the ship.
3
Apply the distinction to the grain scenario
At the moment of meeting, the grain's essential upward tendency ceases, so it is essentially at rest. But it is still moving accidentally with the mountain. Thus, no temporal rest occurs.
This resolves the objection: the mountain does not stop because the grain never truly rests for a duration.
4
Understand the refinement: rest at an instant
The commentator emphasizes that 'rest' at an instant is only metaphorical. Motion and rest cannot exist in an instant because they involve gradual change. The grain's 'rest' is merely the cessation of its essential motion at that instant.
This is a deeper physical insight: motion requires time, so an instant cannot contain motion or rest in the real sense.
5
Test the distinction with another example
Consider a person sitting in a moving car: he is essentially at rest on the seat but accidentally moving with the car. If the car crashes, his accidental motion changes abruptly.
This helps solidify the tool.

Source Text

Proving the Celestial Mover is Non-Corporeal
Author proves the moving force of the celestial sphere is a separate soul, not a corporeal force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves the celestial sphere's mover is not a corporeal force and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Divisible forces are limited; the celestial sphere's motion is unlimited, thus its mover is a soul, not a body.

This passage presents a philosophical demonstration that the force moving the celestial sphere must be a non-corporeal soul, not a corporeal force. The author uses a reductio argument grounded in the divisibility of corporeal forces. By breaking down the logic, you will see how a categorical syllogism underlies this proof and how it connects to Islamic cosmology, where the celestial spheres are seen as living, rational beings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Read the final sentence of the passage: it states that the moving force is not corporeal but a separate soul. This is the claim being proved.
The conclusion is the thesis of the chapter.
2
Extract the key premises
The author argues: (1) Every corporeal force is divisible and thus each part can only produce finite effects. (2) The whole of a corporeal force, being a sum of finite parts, is itself finite. (3) The celestial sphere's motions are infinite (endless).
Premise (3) is assumed from Aristotelian cosmology where celestial motions are perpetual.
3
Formalize the categorical syllogism
Construct a categorical syllogism in the second figure: Major premise: No corporeal force is capable of infinite effects. Minor premise: The celestial mover is capable of infinite effects. Conclusion: Therefore, the celestial mover is not a corporeal force.
This is mood Cesare (second figure), which is valid if the major premise is universal negative and the minor is affirmative.
4
Check validity and soundness
The syllogism is formally valid. Soundness hinges on whether the premises are true. The author provides a reductio to support the major premise: if a corporeal force had a part capable of infinite effects, it would imply an absurd increase upon an infinite sequence.
The reductio strengthens the universal negative premise by showing its denial leads to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن القوة المتحركة للفلك ليست قوة جسمانية. جسمانية فهي قابلة للتجزي، وكل قوة قابلة للتجزي، فإن الجزء منها يقوى على شيء، والجملة تقوى على مجموع تلك الأشياء، وإلا لكان الجزء مساويا للكل في التأثير، هذا خلف، ومتى كان كذلك فالمجموع لا يقوى على غير المتناهي؛ لأن الجزء منها إما أن يقوى على جملة متناهية من مبدأ معين أو على جملة غير متناهية، والثاني باطل؛ إذ المجموع يقوى على ما هو زائد فيلزم الزيادة على غير المتناهي المتسق النظام، هذا خلف، فعلم أن الجزء يقوى على جملة متناهية، والجزء الآخر مثله، فالمجموع لا يقوى على غير المتناهي؛ لأن انضمام المتناهي إلى المتناهي لا يوجب اللاتناهي. وبيان ذلك: أن القوة الجسمانية إذا كانت قابلة للتجزي، فلها أجزاء، وكل جزء من القوة يقدر على آثار هي أزيد من آثار جزء القوة، فلو كان جزء القوة قادرا على آثار غير متناهية لزمت الزيادة على غير المتناهي المترتب النظام؛ ضرورة أن سلسلة الحركات مترتبة لا يمكن الزيادة على غير المتناهي المترتب النظام في الجانب الذي هو غير متناه؛ لأن زيادة الزائد إنما هي بعد تمام آحاد المزيد عليه، فلما بطل أن يكون جزء القوة قادرا على آثار غير متناهية تعين أن يكون قادرا على آثار متناهية، والجزء الآخر كذلك، فجملة القوة لا تقدر على آثار غير متناهية؛ لأن أجزاء القوة بحسب انقسام خارجي بإزاء انقسام خارجي للجسم متناهية، وآثار كل منها متناهية وانضمام المتناهي إلى المتناهي بمرات متناهية يستلزم التناهي، فآثار جملة القوة الجسمانية الحالة في الجسم متناهية فإن جملة القوة عبارة عن جميع الأجزاء، وآثار جميع الأجزاء متناهية كما مر بيانها فآثار الجملة القوة أيضا متناهية، فثبت أن القوة المحركة للفلك التي تقدر على تحريكات غير متناهية، ليست قوة جسمانية، بل روح مجرد له تعلق بالجسم تعلق التصرف والتدبير، وهو المطلوب. كذلك: أي الجزء يقوى على شيء، والجملة تقوى على مجموع تلك الأشياء. إلى المتناهي لا يوجب اللاتناهي فثبت أن كل ما يقوى عليه القوة الجسمانية فهو متناه.
Chapter on that the moving force of the celestial sphere is not a corporeal force. It is corporeal, so it is divisible, and every divisible force, its part is powerful over something, and the whole is powerful over the sum of those things. Otherwise the part would be equal to the whole in effect, which is absurd. And whenever that is the case, the whole is not powerful over the infinite; because the part either is powerful over a finite sum from a specific beginning or over an infinite sum. The second is false, since the whole is powerful over what is greater, so an increase over the infinite ordered in sequence would be necessary, which is absurd. So it is known that the part is powerful over a finite sum, and the other part is similar. So the whole is not powerful over the infinite, because the joining of the finite to the finite does not necessitate the infinite. The explanation of that: When a corporeal force is divisible, it has parts, and each part of the force is capable of effects that are more than the effects of a part of the force. So if a part of the force were capable of infinite effects, an increase over the infinite ordered in sequence would be necessary; necessarily because the series of movements is ordered, and increase over the infinite ordered in sequence in the direction that is infinite is impossible; because the increase of the increasing occurs only after the completion of the individuals of what is increased upon. So when it is false that a part of the force is capable of infinite effects, it is determined that it is capable of finite effects, and likewise the other part. So the whole force is not capable of infinite effects, because the parts of the force, according to external division corresponding to the external division of the body, are finite, and the effects of each are finite, and the joining of the finite to the finite by finite times necessitates finitude. So the effects of the whole corporeal force residing in the body are finite, for the whole force is all the parts, and the effects of all the parts are finite as previously explained, so the effects of the whole force are also finite. So it is established that the moving force of the celestial sphere, which is capable of infinite motions, is not a corporeal force, but rather a separate soul having connection with the body as connection of disposal and management, which is what is sought. Likewise: that is, the part is powerful over something, and the whole is powerful over the sum of those things. The finite does not necessitate infinity, so it is established that everything that the corporeal force is powerful over is finite.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof that the motive force of the celestial sphere is not a corporeal force but a separate soul (ruh mujarrad). The argument proceeds by reductio ad absurdum, leveraging the nature of corporeal forces. The author begins by stating the thesis: the moving force of the celestial sphere is not corporeal. He then explains that any corporeal force is divisible, and in a divisible force, a part can only produce a part of the effects of the whole; otherwise, the part would equal the whole in effect, which is impossible. From this, he argues that if a corporeal force were divisible, its parts would each be limited to finite effects. The sum of finites is finite, so the whole force would also be finite. But the celestial sphere's motions are infinite in duration, so its mover cannot be a finite corporeal force; it must be a non-corporeal entity (a soul) that can produce infinite effects. The author elaborates with a detailed argument: if a part of a corporeal force could produce infinite effects, that would require an increase upon an already infinite sequence, which is impossible because increase only occurs after completion of the preceding items. Therefore each part is limited, and the whole is limited. Thus the mover of the celestial sphere, which causes infinite motions, is a separate soul that manages the body. The passage concludes by reiterating that anything a corporeal force can affect is finite.

Key Terms
قوة جسمانية (quwwa jismāniyya): A force inherent in a body, characterized by divisibility and producing only finite effects.
روح مجرد (rūḥ mujarrad): A non-corporeal, immaterial entity capable of producing infinite effects, such as the mover of the celestial sphere. [Note: In later Islamic philosophy, this concept is refined into the 'Active Intellect' or 'celestial soul' in meššai tradition.]
غير المتناهي (ghayr al-mutanāhī): The infinite; here referring to the endless sequence of celestial motions.
التجزي (al-tajazzī): Divisibility; a property of corporeal substances and forces.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't the moving force of the celestial sphere be a corporeal force?
A) Because corporeal forces are immaterial.
B) Because corporeal forces are divisible and thus produce only finite effects, but the sphere's motions are infinite.
C) Because the celestial sphere is made of a fifth element.
D) Because corporeal forces require a medium.

Key Takeaway
The central logical move is that a divisible (corporeal) force cannot cause an infinite effect, establishing that the celestial mover must be immaterial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a categorical syllogism in the second figure (Camestres or Cesare) to prove that the force moving the Moon is not a corporeal force, using the same structure as the passage. Identify the major, minor, and conclusion.

The Syllogism: Proximate Mover is Corporeal
Syllogistic proof for corporeal proximate mover

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving that the proximate mover of the celestial sphere is corporeal and verify its validity.

Core Idea
Particular voluntary motion requires particular perception, which only corporeal forces possess.

In this passage, the author presents a logical demonstration that the immediate mover of the celestial sphere must be a corporeal force. The argument hinges on the nature of voluntary movement and the distinction between universal and particular concepts. By breaking down the syllogism, you will see how the author uses the principle of sufficient reason to exclude the possibility of a universal concept causing particular movements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is the claim that the author is proving: 'The mover of particular movements is corporeal' (fa-muḥarrik al-ḥarakāt al-juzʾiyya jismānī). Locate this in the Arabic.
The conclusion appears at the end of the main argument and is repeated for emphasis.
2
Find the major premise
The major premise is a universal statement that links corporeality to having a particular concept. The author states: 'Every thing having a particular concept is corporeal' (wa kull mā lahu taṣawwur juzʾī fahuwa jismānī). This is the general rule.
3
Find the minor premise
The minor premise identifies that the subject (the mover of particular movements) falls under the rule. The author says: 'The mover of particular movements has a particular concept' (fa-muḥarrik al-ḥarakāt al-juzʾiyya lahu taṣawwur juzʾī). This is derived from the earlier argument that particular movements cannot come from a universal concept.
4
Identify the middle term
The middle term is the term common to both premises but absent from the conclusion. Here it is 'having a particular concept' (lahu taṣawwur juzʾī). It connects the subject of the conclusion to the predicate 'corporeal'.
The middle term is essential for the logical link.
5
Determine the figure and mood
In the major premise, the middle term is the subject ('every possessor of a particular concept is corporeal'). In the minor premise, the middle term is the predicate ('the mover has a particular concept'). This makes it the first figure. Both premises are universal affirmatives, so the mood is Barbara (AAA). Check validity.
First figure, Barbara is always valid for universal affirmative conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن المحرك القريب للفلك قوة جسمانية. لأن التحريكات الاختيارية لا تقع إلا عن إرادة إما أن تقع عن تصور كلي أو جزئي، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأن التصور الكلي نسبته إلى جميع الجزئيات على السوية، فلا يقع منه بعض الحركات الجزئية دون بعض وإلا لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح فمبدأ التحريكات الجزئية له تصور جزئي، وكل ما له تصور جزئي فهو جسماني، فمحرك الحركات الجزئية جسماني، وهو المطلوب. ثم اعلم أن للفلك نفسا مجردة عن المادة، تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية بحسب العدة والمدة، وأخرى قوة جسمانية بها تدرك الجزئيات؛ لأن صدور الأفعال الجزئية الاختيارية لا يمكن بدون إدراكها، والنفس المجردة الفلكية لا ترتسم فيها الصورة الجزئية الجسمانية؛ لكونها مجردة والصورة مادية فاحتاجت النفس المجردة الفلكية في إدراكها إلى قوة جسمانية، فنسبة القوة الجسمانية الفلكية إلى نفسها المجردة كنسبة القوى الجسمانية إلى نفوسنا المجردة وليست القوة الجسمانية فاعلة للأفعال الغير المتناهية، حتى يلزم التناقض حيث سلب من قبل كونها فاعلة لتلك الأفعال الغير المتناهية، وأثبت ههنا كونها محركة قريبة، بل الفاعل هي النفس المجردة، ولما احتاجت في صدور الأفعال الجزئية إلى القوة الجسمانية من حيث إدراكها – كما مر تقريره - أطلق على القوة الجسمانية أنها محركة قريبة مجازا لا حقيقة حتى يلزم التناقض. فمبدأ التحريكات: أي لما بطل كون التحريكات الجزئية من تصور كلي تعين وقوعها عن تصور جزئي، فمحرك الحركات الجزئية له تصور جزئي، وكل ما له تصور جزئي فهو جسماني، فمحرك الحركات الجزئية جسماني، وهو المطلوب. حاصله أن الفعل الاختياري إنما يصدر عن فاعل مختار بعد تصوره، فالحركات الجزئية الاختيارية الفلكية إما أن تقع بعد تصور كلي أو جزئي، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛
Chapter on that the proximate mover of the celestial sphere is a corporeal force. Because voluntary movements occur only by will, either occurring from a universal concept or a particular concept. The first is impossible, because the universal concept relates equally to all particulars, so some particular movements would not occur from it without a preponderating factor, which is absurd. So the principle of particular movements has a particular concept, and everything having a particular concept is corporeal. So the mover of particular movements is corporeal, which is what is sought. Then know that the celestial sphere has a soul separate from matter, capable of infinite actions in number and duration, and another corporeal force by which it perceives particulars; because the occurrence of particular voluntary actions is impossible without their perception, and the separate celestial soul does not receive the particular corporeal form because it is separate and the form is material. So the separate celestial soul needed a corporeal force for its perception. The relation of the celestial corporeal force to its separate soul is like the relation of corporeal forces to our separate souls. And the corporeal force is not the agent of infinite actions, such that contradiction would follow where it was previously negated as being the agent of those infinite actions, and here it is affirmed as a proximate mover. Rather, the agent is the separate soul. And when it needed for the occurrence of particular actions the corporeal force in terms of its perception – as previously established – the corporeal force is called a 'proximate mover' metaphorically, not literally, such that contradiction would follow. So the principle of movements: that is, when it is impossible that particular movements are from a universal concept, it is determined that they occur from a particular concept. So the mover of particular movements has a particular concept, and everything having a particular concept is corporeal. So the mover of particular movements is corporeal, which is what is sought. Its summary: that voluntary action only issues from a free agent after its conception. So the particular voluntary celestial movements either occur after a universal concept or a particular concept; the first is impossible.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof that the proximate mover of the celestial sphere is a corporeal force. The author begins by stating the thesis, then provides a logical argument. The key premise is that voluntary movements (al-tahrikat al-ikhtiyariyya) must originate from either a universal concept (tasawwur kulli) or a particular concept (tasawwur juz'i). A universal concept relates equally to all particulars, so it cannot specify one particular movement without a preponderating factor (tarjih bila murajjih), which is impossible. Therefore, particular movements must arise from a particular concept. Since only corporeal entities can have particular concepts (because they involve material, particular images), the mover that produces such movements must be corporeal. The author then clarifies an apparent contradiction: earlier it was established that the moving force of the celestial sphere is a separate soul (non-corporeal), but now he says the proximate mover is corporeal. He resolves this by explaining that the celestial sphere has both a separate soul (capable of infinite actions) and a corporeal force that perceives particulars. The separate soul is the ultimate agent, but it uses the corporeal force as an instrument for perception and particular action. Thus, the corporeal force is called a 'proximate mover' only metaphorically, not literally; the true agent is the soul. The argument concludes by restating the syllogism: particular movements require a particular concept, which implies a corporeal mover. The author summarizes by reiterating that voluntary action requires conception, and celestial particular actions cannot come from a universal concept.

Key Terms
محرك قريب
تصور كلي
تصور جزئي
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the syllogism, why is the universal concept insufficient to cause particular movements?
A) Because universal concepts are not real.
B) Because it would require a preponderating factor, which is absent.
C) Because particular movements can only come from particular concepts.
D) Because the universal concept is immaterial.

Key Takeaway
The first-figure Barbara syllogism is a valid form used to demonstrate that the proximate mover must be corporeal because it requires a particular concept, which only corporeal beings possess.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism of the same form (first figure, Barbara) to prove that the soul is immaterial. Use premises related to simplicity and non-divisibility. Ensure you identify the middle term.
?
Take a contemporary theological claim (e.g., 'The Qur'an is uncreated') and attempt to formulate it as a categorical syllogism. Check if your syllogism meets the conditions of first figure validity.

Resolving the Contradiction: Soul vs. Corporeal Force
Resolution of apparent contradiction regarding the mover

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the author reconciles the claim that the mover is a separate soul with the claim that the proximate mover is corporeal, and identify the metaphorical use of 'proximate mover'.

Core Idea
The soul is the true agent; the corporeal force is its instrument for perceiving particulars.

The previous argument concluded that the proximate mover is corporeal, but earlier in the text it was established that the celestial sphere has a separate, non-corporeal soul as its mover. This seems contradictory. In this part of the passage, the author resolves the tension by distinguishing between the ultimate agent (the soul) and the instrumental proximate mover (a corporeal force). Understanding this distinction is crucial for a coherent cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two movers mentioned
The author explicitly mentions a separate soul (nafs mujarrada) and a corporeal force (quwwa jismāniyya). Note their roles: the soul is capable of infinite actions, while the corporeal force is responsible for perceiving particulars.
The separate soul is the true agent, but it cannot directly perceive material particulars.
2
Understand the need for a corporeal force
The separate soul cannot receive particular corporeal forms because it is immaterial and the forms are material. Therefore, it requires a corporeal faculty to perceive particulars—this is the corporeal force.
The analogy is given: like our separate souls rely on bodily senses.
3
Distinguish literal from metaphorical 'proximate mover'
The author states that the corporeal force is called a 'proximate mover' (muḥarrik qarīb) metaphorically, not literally. The literal agent is the separate soul. The corporeal force is the instrument (āla) through which the soul acts.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
4
Reconcile the two proofs
The earlier proof that the mover is non-corporeal refers to the ultimate agent (the soul). The current proof that the proximate mover is corporeal refers to the instrumental cause. Both are true without contradiction.
This shows the importance of precise terminology in philosophical arguments.

فصل في أن المحرك القريب للفلك قوة جسمانية. لأن التحريكات الاختيارية لا تقع إلا عن إرادة إما أن تقع عن تصور كلي أو جزئي، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأن التصور الكلي نسبته إلى جميع الجزئيات على السوية، فلا يقع منه بعض الحركات الجزئية دون بعض وإلا لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح فمبدأ التحريكات الجزئية له تصور جزئي، وكل ما له تصور جزئي فهو جسماني، فمحرك الحركات الجزئية جسماني، وهو المطلوب. ثم اعلم أن للفلك نفسا مجردة عن المادة، تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية بحسب العدة والمدة، وأخرى قوة جسمانية بها تدرك الجزئيات؛ لأن صدور الأفعال الجزئية الاختيارية لا يمكن بدون إدراكها، والنفس المجردة الفلكية لا ترتسم فيها الصورة الجزئية الجسمانية؛ لكونها مجردة والصورة مادية فاحتاجت النفس المجردة الفلكية في إدراكها إلى قوة جسمانية، فنسبة القوة الجسمانية الفلكية إلى نفسها المجردة كنسبة القوى الجسمانية إلى نفوسنا المجردة وليست القوة الجسمانية فاعلة للأفعال الغير المتناهية، حتى يلزم التناقض حيث سلب من قبل كونها فاعلة لتلك الأفعال الغير المتناهية، وأثبت ههنا كونها محركة قريبة، بل الفاعل هي النفس المجردة، ولما احتاجت في صدور الأفعال الجزئية إلى القوة الجسمانية من حيث إدراكها – كما مر تقريره - أطلق على القوة الجسمانية أنها محركة قريبة مجازا لا حقيقة حتى يلزم التناقض. فمبدأ التحريكات: أي لما بطل كون التحريكات الجزئية من تصور كلي تعين وقوعها عن تصور جزئي، فمحرك الحركات الجزئية له تصور جزئي، وكل ما له تصور جزئي فهو جسماني، فمحرك الحركات الجزئية جسماني، وهو المطلوب. حاصله أن الفعل الاختياري إنما يصدر عن فاعل مختار بعد تصوره، فالحركات الجزئية الاختيارية الفلكية إما أن تقع بعد تصور كلي أو جزئي، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛

?
Apply the same distinction to an Islamic theological debate: The Ash‘ari theory of acquisition (kasb) holds that God creates human actions while humans 'acquire' them. Analyze which aspect is the true agent and which is the proximate cause, using the author's tool of literal vs. metaphorical agency.
?
Write a short paragraph explaining how the author's method of resolving contradictions (by distinguishing ultimate and proximate causes) can be used to harmonize seemingly contradictory verses of the Qur'an.

Proof that the Proximate Mover is Corporeal (via Partial Perception)
Author proves the proximate mover is corporeal via the lemma that partial perception requires a corporeal faculty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves the proximate mover is corporeal and explain the lemma that partial perception requires a corporeal substrate.

Core Idea
Partial perception requires a corporeal substrate; the proximate mover has partial perception, therefore it is corporeal.

In the Hidayat al-Hikma, the author proves that the immediate mover of the celestial spheres must be corporeal. The argument revolves around a key lemma: 'Everything that has partial perception is corporeal.' This lesson unpacks the demonstration step by step, showing how the size variation of partial forms forces a divisible, corporeal substrate, and then applies it to the proximate mover.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall claim
The chapter title states the conclusion: the proximate mover (al-muharrik al-qarib) is corporeal. Read the first and last sentences to confirm this.
2
Extract the lemma
The text states: 'Everything that has partial perception is corporeal.' This is the major premise of the main syllogism. Locate this statement early in the passage.
3
Understand the proof of the lemma
The author argues that partial forms (e.g., small vs. large image) differ in size. The difference cannot be due to the form's essence (both are the same type) nor due to the external object (we can imagine non-existent objects). Therefore, the difference must be in the substrate of impression, which must be divisible, hence corporeal.
The commentator offers a simpler proof: a partial form has position and magnitude, so it cannot inhere in an abstract essence.
4
Apply the lemma to the proximate mover
The proximate mover is the principle of partial movements (mabda' al-tahrikat al-juz'iyya). Since it directs particular motions, it must have partial perception of those motions. Thus, via the lemma, it is corporeal.
5
Reconstruct the categorical syllogism
Major premise: Everything with partial perception is corporeal. Minor premise: The proximate mover has partial perception. Conclusion: Therefore, the proximate mover is corporeal. This is a first-figure Barbara syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني في الفلكيات فصل في أن المحرك القريب جسماني، وكل ما له تصور جزئي فهو جسماني؛ لأن الصورة الجزئية ترتسم وهي أصغر، وترتسم وهي أكبر، فإما أن يكون الاختلاف في الصغر والكبر لاختلاف الصورتين بالحقيقة أو لاختلاف المأخوذ عنه الصورتان بالصغر والكبر، أو لاختلافهما في المحل من المدرك، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأن الكلي نسبته إلى جميع أفراده على السوية، فلو وقع الفعل الجزئي بعد التصور الكلي لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، وهو باطل، فتعين الأول، فلا بد للتصور الجزئي من محل يقوم به الصورة الجزئية، ومحل ارتسام الصورة الجزئية المادية قوة جسمانية لا مجردة؛ لأن الصورة الجزئية ترتسم مرة صغيرة وأخرى كبيرة، فالاختلاف بين الصورتين بالصغر والكبر لا لاختلاف ماهيتهما، أو لاختلاف ما أخذ عنه الصورتان، أو لاختلاف محل ارتسامهما، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأنا أخذنا صورتين من نوع واحد، ذلك الاتحاد النوعي بينهما قد تختلفان بالصغر والكبر، فلا دخل لاختلاف الماهية في الصغر والكبر، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن الصورتين قد تختلفان بالصغر والكبر في التصور من غير أن تؤخذ من شيئين مختلفين بالصغر والكبر في الخارج، كما إذا تصورنا قبة من ياقوت وجبلا من ياقوت من غير أن نأخذ صورتيهما من شيء في الخارج؛ إذ ليس في الخارج قبة ياقوت ولا جبله حتى نأخذ صورتيهما منهما، فتبين أن اختلاف الصورتين بالصغر والكبر لا دخل فيه للمأخوذ عنه، فتبين القسم الثالث فمحل ارتسام الصغيرة غير محل ارتسام الكبيرة، فمحل ارتسامهما مختلف بالصغر والكبر، فينقسم محل ارتسامهما، فهو جسماني؛ لأن الانقسام إلى المقدار الصغير والكبير من خواص الأجسام، لا يمكن ذلك في الذات المجردة. وقد أطال المصنف في إثبات أن ما له تصور جزئي فهو جسماني، وأورد شقوقا وأبطل اثنين منها ببيان لا يخلو عن صعوبة، ويكفي لإثبات هذا المرام أن يقال: إن الصورة الجزئية المادية لكونها ذات وضع ومقدار لا يمكن أن ترتسم في ذات مجردة؛ لأنها لو ارتسمت في ذات مجردة لزم كون ذات مجردة ذات وضع ومقدار، فلا تبقى مجردة، وهذا خلف، فلا بد أن ترتسم في قوة جسمانية، فثبت أن ما له تصور جزئي فهو جسماني، وهو المطلوب. فإذا ثبت أن ما له تصور جزئي فهو جسماني، فمبدأ التحريكات الجزئية جسماني، وهو المطلوب؛ لأن المراد بالمبدأ المحرك القريب.
The Second Art on Celestial Bodies: Chapter that the proximate mover is corporeal. And everything that has partial perception is corporeal; because the partial form is impressed while it is smaller, and is impressed while it is larger. So the difference in smallness and bigness is either due to the difference of the two forms in reality, or due to the difference of that from which the two forms are taken in smallness and bigness, or due to their difference in the substrate of the perceptive faculty. The first is not possible; because the universal has the same relation to all its individuals. So if the partial act occurred after universal perception, an ungrounded preference would follow, which is false. Thus the first [case] is determined. So partial perception necessarily has a substrate in which the partial form subsists. And the substrate of impression of the material partial form is a corporeal power, not an abstract one; because the partial form is impressed once as small and another time as large. The difference between the two forms in smallness and bigness is not due to the difference of their essences, nor due to the difference of that from which the two forms are taken, nor due to the difference of their substrate of impression. The first is not possible; because we take two forms of one type—that specific unity between them—they may differ in smallness and bigness, so the difference of essence has no role in smallness and bigness. The second is not possible; because the two forms may differ in smallness and bigness in perception without being taken from two things that differ in smallness and bigness externally, as when we imagine a dome of ruby and a mountain of ruby without taking their two forms from anything external; since there is no dome of ruby nor its mountain externally from which we take their two forms. Thus it is clear that the difference of the two forms in smallness and bigness has no involvement of that from which they are taken. So the third division becomes clear: the substrate of impression of the small one is not the substrate of impression of the large one. So their substrate of impression differs in smallness and bigness, so the substrate of their impression is divisible; therefore it is corporeal, because divisibility into small and large quantity is a property of bodies, not possible in an abstract essence. And the author goes on at length in proving that that which has partial perception is corporeal, and he presents divisions and invalidates two of them with a demonstration not free of difficulty. And it suffices for proving this objective to say: The material partial form, because it possesses position and magnitude, cannot be impressed in an abstract essence; because if it were impressed in an abstract essence, it would follow that an abstract essence has position and magnitude, so it would not remain abstract, and this is a contradiction [khalf]. So it must necessarily be impressed in a corporeal power. Thus it is established that that which has partial perception is corporeal, and that is the required [conclusion]. So if it is established that that which has partial perception is corporeal, then the principle of partial movements is corporeal, and that is the required [conclusion]; because by 'principle' is meant the proximate mover.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration (burhan) within the second art on celestial bodies. The author aims to prove that the proximate mover (al-muharrik al-qarib) is corporeal (jismani). The argument proceeds through a lemma: 'Everything that has partial perception (tasawwur juz'i) is corporeal.' The proof of this lemma occupies most of the text. The author first notes that a partial form (sura juz'iyya) can be perceived in two different sizes (small and large). The difference in size cannot be due to the form's essence (since both forms are of the same type), nor due to the external object from which the form is abstracted (since we can imagine non-existent objects like a ruby dome and ruby mountain without external referents). Thus, the difference must be due to the substrate in which the form is impressed: the perception of the small form occurs in one part of the substrate, and the large form in a different part. Therefore, the substrate is divisible in terms of small and large, which is a characteristic of bodies, not of abstract entities. Hence, the faculty that receives partial forms is corporeal. After establishing the lemma, the author draws the final conclusion: the principle of partial movements (mabda' al-tahrikat al-juz'iyya) has partial perception of the movements it causes, so it must be corporeal. And this principle is what is meant by the 'proximate mover' (al-muharrik al-qarib). The text also contains a commentator's remark noting that the original author (al-musannif) gave a more elaborate proof with multiple divisions, but a simpler proof suffices: a partial form has position and magnitude, so it cannot be received in an abstract essence (otherwise it would become material, a contradiction). Thus the proof is complete.

Key Terms
تصور جزئي
جسماني
محرك قريب
صورة جزئية
محل ارتسام

Spherical Elements and Refutation of Earth's Rotation
Author refutes earth's rotation using conditional logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional refutation of earth's rotation and evaluate its logical validity.

Core Idea
A falling stone proves the earth does not rotate.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikmah presents a classic refutation of the earth's rotation. The author first establishes the spherical shape and stationary nature of the elements, then addresses a contrary claim. The refutation uses a conditional reasoning pattern that is both simple and powerful. By parsing this argument, you will see how a single observational fact can refute a cosmological hypothesis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the statement that some people claim: that the earth rotates daily towards the east, and that the apparent daily motion is due to this rotation. This is the claim to be refuted.
2
Extract the conditional premise
The author presents a conditional: 'If the earth rotated eastward, then a stone dropped vertically would not land directly below its release point, but rather to the west.' This is because the earth would have moved east during the stone's fall.
This is the 'if-then' structure of the argument.
3
Note the observed consequent
The author appeals to intuition (al-bidāha): the stone does land directly below the release point. This is the second premise: the consequent (the stone landing to the west) is false.
4
Recognize the logical form
The argument follows modus tollens: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P. Here P = 'earth rotates', Q = 'stone lands to the west'. Since Q is false, P is false. This is a valid conditional syllogism.
The author presents this as a decisive refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: إن العناصر كرية الأشكال لبساطتها، والنار فوق العناصر الثلاثة بحيث يماس سطحها الأعلى بمقعر فلك الدنيا، ثم الهواء ثم الماء ثم الأرض تحت الكل بحيث ينطبق مركزها على مركز العالم وهي ساكنة، وزعم قوم أنها متحركة بالاستدارة إلى المشرق، والحركة اليومية منسوبة إليها ويبطله أنه لو كان كذلك لما وقع الحجر الهابط على الموضع الذي حاذاه من الأرض حين هبوطه؛ لأن ذلك الموضع قد تحرك نحو المشرق بتبعية الأرض فراسخ، بل يقع في المغرب من ذلك الموضع، وهذا باطل بالبداهة وقد أبطله
They said: The elements are spherical in shape because of their simplicity. Fire is above the three elements such that its upper surface touches the concavity of the lowest sphere, then air, then water, then earth below all, such that its center coincides with the center of the world, and it is stationary. Some people claimed that it (the earth) moves by rotation towards the east, and the daily motion is attributed to it. But it is refuted that if it were so, then when a stone falls vertically, it would not land on the spot it was aligned with at the start of its fall, because that spot would have moved eastward by following the earth for leagues, rather it would land to the west of that spot. And this is false by intuition, and he refuted it.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents a standard refutation of the rotation of the earth. The author first states the accepted view: the elements are spherical (because of their simplicity), arranged in concentric layers—fire nearest the lunar sphere, then air, water, and earth at the center, which is stationary. Against this, some claim that the earth rotates daily from west to east, which would explain the apparent daily motion of the fixed stars. The refutation uses a conditional syllogism: If the earth rotated eastward, then a stone dropped from a height would land to the west of its starting point (because the earth moved eastward during the fall). But experience shows that the stone lands directly below its release point. Therefore, the earth does not rotate. This argument is based on common sense observation (البداهة). The refutation is presented as decisive.

Argument from Divisibility for the Soul's Immateriality
Author provides two arguments proving that the rational soul is immaterial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio ad absurdum argument for the immateriality of the rational soul and identify its key steps.

Core Idea
A simple intelligible form cannot inhere in a divisible subject—proving the soul is indivisible.

This passage contains a rigorous reductio argument that the rational soul must be immaterial. The argument proceeds by assuming the soul is material (and thus divisible) and then showing that this assumption leads to a contradiction regarding the nature of intelligible forms. Understanding this logical structure is essential for grasping the philosophical proof of the soul's immateriality, a cornerstone of Islamic psychology.

Guided Path
1
Assume the soul is material
Start with the hypothesis that the rational soul is material, which means it has position and is therefore divisible, as established earlier.
2
Derive divisibility
From the assumption, conclude that the soul is divisible because everything with position is divisible.
This step relies on a previously proven principle that all material things are divisible.
3
Consider the two possibilities for intelligible forms
Intelligible forms (the content of intellection) must be either simple or composite. Examine each case.
These are the only two logical possibilities.
4
Show contradiction for simple forms
If a simple form inheres in a divisible soul, it would be divided because the accident follows the division of its subject. This contradicts its simplicity.
Simple entities are indivisible by definition.
5
Show contradiction for composite forms
Composite forms are made of simples; those simples would also be divided by the soul's division, again a contradiction.
The composition regress stops at simples, which must be indivisible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن القوة العاقلة مجردة عن المادة؛ لأنها لو كانت مادية لكانت ذات وضع، فإما أن لا تنقسم أو تنقسم، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأن كل ما له وضع ينقسم، على ما مر في نفي الجزء، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن معقولاتها إن كانت بسيطة يلزم انقسامها؛ لأن الحال في أحد جزئيها غير الحال في الجزء الآخر، وإن كانت مركبة، وكل مركب إنما يتركب من البسائط، فيلزم انقسام تلك البسائط، هذا خلف، ونقول أيضا: إن التعقل ليس بالآلة الجسمانية، وإلا يعرض لها الكلال لضعف البدن، ذات وضع أي قابلة للإشارة الحسية ضرورة أن المادة ذات وضع، فالقائم بها أيضا يكون ذا وضع معقولاتها حاصله أن النفس إن كانت مادية منقسمة وصور المعقولات حالة فيها، فلا تخلو تلك الصور من أن تكون بسيطة أو مركبة، فإن كانت بسيطة لزم انقسام البسيط؛ لأن الوصف الحال في محل منقسم ينقسم لا محالة بواسطة، والنفس على هذا التقدير منقسمة، فالصورة الحالة فيها أيضا منقسمة، وقد كانت بسيطة، هذا خلف. وإن كانت مركبة فكل مركب ينتهي تركيبه إلى بسيط، وإلا لزم تركيبه من أجزاء غير متناهية وذلك باطل بالتطبيق، فذلك البسيط المنتهي إليه المركب حال في النفس أيضا، فيلزم انقسامه بواسطة النفس، وهذا خلف، فلما لزم الخلف على تقدير كون النفس مادية على كلا الشقين بطل كونها مادية، فثبت كونها مجردة، وهو المطلوب. ونقول إلخ: دليل آخر على تجرد النفس، ويمكن أن يكون المراد به أن القوة العاقلة ليست جسمانية، بل صفة قائمة بالنفس وإلا إلخ أي لو كان التعقل بالآلة الجسمانية يعرض لها الكلال عند ضعف البدن؛ لأن القوى الجسمانية قائمة بالبدن، فلما ضعف البدن ضعفت القوى البدنية، كما ترى الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة تكل بعد الأربعين، فلو كانت القوة العاقلة للنفس مادية لكلت أيضا بعد الأربعين كسائر القوى الجسمانية، والتالي باطل؛ لأن القوة العاقلة بعد الأربعين قوية على حالها، بل -
And know that the rational faculty is abstract from matter; for if it were material, it would have position, and then it would either be indivisible or divisible. The first is impossible because everything with position is divisible, as established in the refutation of the atom. The second is also impossible because its intelligibles, if they are simple, entails their division (since the state in one of its parts is not the state in the other part), and if they are composite, every composite is composed of simples, so those simples would be divided; this is a contradiction. We also say: intellection is not by a bodily organ, otherwise fatigue would occur to it due to bodily weakness. (Commentary:) 'Having position' means it is subject to sensory indication, necessarily because matter has position, so what subsists by it also has position. The gist of his statement about its intelligibles: If the soul were material and divisible, and the forms of the intelligibles inhere in it, then those forms are either simple or composite. If simple, then the simple would become divided because the description inherent in a divisible subject is divided necessarily, and the soul on this assumption is divisible, so the form inhering in it is also divided, but it was simple — this is a contradiction. If composite, every composite ends in a simple (otherwise it would be composed of infinite parts, which is false by the principle of congruity), so that simple which is the ultimate constituent of the composite inheres in the soul, and thus it would be divided by the soul — this is also a contradiction. Since contradiction follows on both alternatives of the soul being material, its materiality is refuted, so its immateriality is established; that is the desired conclusion. 'And we also say, etc.': another proof for the immateriality of the soul. It may mean that the rational faculty is not corporeal, but rather an attribute subsisting in the soul, otherwise... i.e., if intellection were by a bodily organ, then fatigue would occur to it when the body weakens, because bodily powers subsist in the body. Therefore, when the body weakens, the bodily powers weaken, as you see the external and internal senses fatigue after forty years. So if the rational faculty of the soul were material, it would also fatigue after forty years like other bodily powers. But the consequent is false because the rational faculty after forty years remains strong, on the contrary...
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents two rigorous arguments for the immateriality of the rational soul, a central thesis in Islamic philosophy. The first argument (from divisibility) proceeds as a reductio ad absurdum. Assume the soul is material; then it has position, and anything with position is divisible. If divisible, then any intelligible forms inhering in it would also be divided. But if the forms are simple (non-composite), their division is impossible because simple entities are not divisible. If they are composite, they are composed of simples, and those simples would also be divided due to the soul's division, again impossible. This contradiction shows the assumption is false. The commentary details this: the soul's divisibility forces the division of its content; since we experience concepts as indivisible, the soul must be immaterial. The second argument (from fatigue) uses a conditional syllogism (modus tollens). If intellection were by a bodily organ, it would tire when the body weakens, just as sense organs tire with age. But the rational faculty does not tire; indeed it often strengthens with age. Therefore, intellection is not by a bodily organ, confirming the soul's immateriality. These arguments are classical in works like Ibn Sina's al-Shifa and are foundational for proving the soul's survival after death.

Key Terms
القوة العاقلة
مجردة عن المادة
ذات وضع
خلف

B) Because the soul is known to be simple from experience.
C) Because the argument assumes the soul is composite.
D) Because the soul's intelligibles are always composite.

Key Takeaway
A reductio ad absurdum shows that assuming the soul is material leads to contradiction, proving its immateriality.

Argument from Fatigue for the Soul's Immateriality
Author provides two arguments proving that the rational soul is immaterial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a modus tollens conditional syllogism and evaluate its validity in the context of the soul's immateriality.

Core Idea
If intellection were bodily, it would tire with age; but it doesn't, so it's not bodily.

The second argument for the immateriality of the rational soul is a crisp conditional syllogism. It takes the form of modus tollens: if intellection were a bodily process, it would fatigue like other bodily powers; but intellection does not fatigue with age (indeed it often strengthens); therefore, intellection is not a bodily process. This argument is accessible yet powerful, relying on observable experience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
The argument starts with an 'if...then' statement: 'If intellection is by a bodily organ, then fatigue would occur to it due to bodily weakness.' This is the major premise.
The antecedent is 'intellection is by a bodily organ'; the consequent is 'fatigue would occur'.
2
Identify the denial of the consequent
The argument observes that intellection does not fatigue; indeed it often becomes stronger with age. That is the negation of the consequent.
The observed fact is that the rational faculty remains strong after forty years, unlike the senses.
3
Apply modus tollens
From 'if P then Q' and 'not Q', we validly conclude 'not P'. So we infer that intellection is not by a bodily organ.
This is a valid logical form: modus tollens.
4
Conclude immateriality
Since intellection is not by a bodily organ, the rational soul (the subject of intellection) is not material; it is immaterial.
The argument assumes that if the soul were material, its intellection would be through a bodily organ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن القوة العاقلة مجردة عن المادة؛ لأنها لو كانت مادية لكانت ذات وضع، فإما أن لا تنقسم أو تنقسم، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأن كل ما له وضع ينقسم، على ما مر في نفي الجزء، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن معقولاتها إن كانت بسيطة يلزم انقسامها؛ لأن الحال في أحد جزئيها غير الحال في الجزء الآخر، وإن كانت مركبة، وكل مركب إنما يتركب من البسائط، فيلزم انقسام تلك البسائط، هذا خلف، ونقول أيضا: إن التعقل ليس بالآلة الجسمانية، وإلا يعرض لها الكلال لضعف البدن، ذات وضع أي قابلة للإشارة الحسية ضرورة أن المادة ذات وضع، فالقائم بها أيضا يكون ذا وضع معقولاتها حاصله أن النفس إن كانت مادية منقسمة وصور المعقولات حالة فيها، فلا تخلو تلك الصور من أن تكون بسيطة أو مركبة، فإن كانت بسيطة لزم انقسام البسيط؛ لأن الوصف الحال في محل منقسم ينقسم لا محالة بواسطة، والنفس على هذا التقدير منقسمة، فالصورة الحالة فيها أيضا منقسمة، وقد كانت بسيطة، هذا خلف. وإن كانت مركبة فكل مركب ينتهي تركيبه إلى بسيط، وإلا لزم تركيبه من أجزاء غير متناهية وذلك باطل بالتطبيق، فذلك البسيط المنتهي إليه المركب حال في النفس أيضا، فيلزم انقسامه بواسطة النفس، وهذا خلف، فلما لزم الخلف على تقدير كون النفس مادية على كلا الشقين بطل كونها مادية، فثبت كونها مجردة، وهو المطلوب. ونقول إلخ: دليل آخر على تجرد النفس، ويمكن أن يكون المراد به أن القوة العاقلة ليست جسمانية، بل صفة قائمة بالنفس وإلا إلخ أي لو كان التعقل بالآلة الجسمانية يعرض لها الكلال عند ضعف البدن؛ لأن القوى الجسمانية قائمة بالبدن، فلما ضعف البدن ضعفت القوى البدنية، كما ترى الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة تكل بعد الأربعين، فلو كانت القوة العاقلة للنفس مادية لكلت أيضا بعد الأربعين كسائر القوى الجسمانية، والتالي باطل؛ لأن القوة العاقلة بعد الأربعين قوية على حالها، بل -
And know that the rational faculty is abstract from matter; for if it were material, it would have position, and then it would either be indivisible or divisible. The first is impossible because everything with position is divisible, as established in the refutation of the atom. The second is also impossible because its intelligibles, if they are simple, entails their division (since the state in one of its parts is not the state in the other part), and if they are composite, every composite is composed of simples, so those simples would be divided; this is a contradiction. We also say: intellection is not by a bodily organ, otherwise fatigue would occur to it due to bodily weakness. (Commentary:) 'Having position' means it is subject to sensory indication, necessarily because matter has position, so what subsists by it also has position. The gist of his statement about its intelligibles: If the soul were material and divisible, and the forms of the intelligibles inhere in it, then those forms are either simple or composite. If simple, then the simple would become divided because the description inherent in a divisible subject is divided necessarily, and the soul on this assumption is divisible, so the form inhering in it is also divided, but it was simple — this is a contradiction. If composite, every composite ends in a simple (otherwise it would be composed of infinite parts, which is false by the principle of congruity), so that simple which is the ultimate constituent of the composite inheres in the soul, and thus it would be divided by the soul — this is also a contradiction. Since contradiction follows on both alternatives of the soul being material, its materiality is refuted, so its immateriality is established; that is the desired conclusion. 'And we also say, etc.': another proof for the immateriality of the soul. It may mean that the rational faculty is not corporeal, but rather an attribute subsisting in the soul, otherwise... i.e., if intellection were by a bodily organ, then fatigue would occur to it when the body weakens, because bodily powers subsist in the body. Therefore, when the body weakens, the bodily powers weaken, as you see the external and internal senses fatigue after forty years. So if the rational faculty of the soul were material, it would also fatigue after forty years like other bodily powers. But the consequent is false because the rational faculty after forty years remains strong, on the contrary...
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents two rigorous arguments for the immateriality of the rational soul, a central thesis in Islamic philosophy. The first argument (from divisibility) proceeds as a reductio ad absurdum. Assume the soul is material; then it has position, and anything with position is divisible. If divisible, then any intelligible forms inhering in it would also be divided. But if the forms are simple (non-composite), their division is impossible because simple entities are not divisible. If they are composite, they are composed of simples, and those simples would also be divided due to the soul's division, again impossible. This contradiction shows the assumption is false. The commentary details this: the soul's divisibility forces the division of its content; since we experience concepts as indivisible, the soul must be immaterial. The second argument (from fatigue) uses a conditional syllogism (modus tollens). If intellection were by a bodily organ, it would tire when the body weakens, just as sense organs tire with age. But the rational faculty does not tire; indeed it often strengthens with age. Therefore, intellection is not by a bodily organ, confirming the soul's immateriality. These arguments are classical in works like Ibn Sina's al-Shifa and are foundational for proving the soul's survival after death.

Key Terms

الكلال
التعقل
الآلة الجسمانية

The empirical observation that rational thought does not decline with age provides a simple yet powerful modus tollens proof of the soul's immateriality.

The Proof of the Soul's Origination as a Conditional Syllogism
Proof that the Rational Soul is Originated with the Body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism proving the rational soul is originated with the body and verify its validity.

Core Idea
If the soul pre-existed, it would differ by essence or accidents; neither works, so it cannot pre-exist.

This passage offers a crisp logical proof for the origination of the rational soul. The author sets up a conditional statement: if souls existed before bodies, then differences among them would have to be due to either their essence or their separable accidents. He then shows both possibilities fail, leading to the necessary conclusion that souls are originated with the body. In this lesson, we will break down this conditional syllogism and see how the refutations are constructed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the conditional in the Arabic: "if they were existent before the body, then difference... would either be by essence and its concomitants or by separable accidents." This is the major premise of the argument.
2
Refute the first disjunct: difference by essence
The author argues that essence is common to all souls, so it cannot be the basis for difference. What is common cannot differentiate.
This refutes the first part of the consequent.
3
Refute the second disjunct: difference by separable accidents
Separable accidents require a subject with varying receptivity, which is only the body. Without a body, no such accidents can attach.
This refutes the second part of the consequent.
4
Apply modus tollens
Since both disjuncts of the consequent are false, the antecedent (pre-existence) must be false. Therefore, souls are originated with the body.
This is the logical rule of modus tollens applied to a disjunctive consequent.
5
Formalize the syllogism
Write the argument: If P (pre-existence) then (Q or R) (difference by essence or accidents). Not Q and not R. Therefore not P.
Verify validity: a valid conditional syllogism of type modus tollens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول أيضا : إن النفوس الناطقة حادثة؛ لأنها لو كانت موجودة قبل البدن، فالاختلاف بينها إما أن يكون بالماهية ولوازمها أو بعوارضها المفارقة. لا جائز أن يكون بالماهية ولوازمها؛ لأنها مشتركة، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز. وأما بطلان الاختلاف باللوازم فلما مر من اتحاد الماهية؛ لأن اتحاد الملزوم يستلزم اتحاد اللازم. وأما بطلان الاختلاف بالعوارض المفارقة فلأن اختلاف العوارض إنما يكون باختلاف استعداد المادة؛ لأن تلك العوارض لا تقتضي الماهية لذاتها، وإلا لزم أن تكون تلك العوارض لازمة لها، وهذا خلف، فلا بد هناك من قابل العوارض مختلفة باستعداد مختلف وما هو إلا البدن، وإذا فرض هناك وجودها قبل البدن لم يكن اختلافها بالعوارض المفارقة اللاحقة من جهة البدن، فلما أنحاء الاختلاف قبل البدن، وكان الاختلاف لازما لها؛ ضرورة اختلاف بطل جميع التشخصات، بطل وجودها قبل البدن. ولا جائز أن يكون بالعوارض المفارقة؛ لأن العوارض المفارقة إنما تلحق الشيء بسبب القوابل؛ لأن الماهية لا تستحق العوارض لذاتها، وإلا لكان العارض لازما، والقابل للنفس إنما هو البدن، فمتى لم تكن الأبدان موجودة لم تكن النفوس موجودة، فتكون حادثة ضرورة. للنفس: أي للعوارض المفارقة التي بسببها تختلف تشخصاتها.
And we also say: The rational souls are originated, because if they were existent before the body, then difference among them would either be by essence and its concomitants or by separable accidents. It is not possible that it be by essence and its concomitants, because they are common, and that by which they are common is other than that by which they are distinguished. As for the invalidity of difference by concomitants, it is due to what has passed regarding the unity of essence, because unity of the substrate necessitates unity of the concomitant. As for the invalidity of difference by separable accidents, it is because difference in accidents only occurs due to difference in the preparedness of the matter, since those accidents are not required by the essence in itself, otherwise they would be necessary to it, which is contrary to fact. Hence there must be a receiver for accidents that is different with different preparedness, and that is none other than the body. And if their existence before the body is supposed, then their difference would not be by separable accidents that attach from the body. Since all modes of difference before the body are impossible, and difference is necessary for them due to the necessity of difference in all individuations, their existence before the body is invalid. And it is not possible that difference be by separable accidents, because separable accidents only attach to a thing due to receivers, because the essence does not deserve accidents in itself, otherwise the accident would be necessary. And the receiver for the soul is only the body. So when bodies are not existent, souls are not existent, thus they are necessarily originated. Explanation: 'for the soul' means the separable accidents by which its individuations differ.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof (burhan) for the origination (huduth) of the rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa) with the body. The author, following the Peripatetic school, argues that souls cannot pre-exist bodies. The argument is a categorical syllogism by elimination: If souls existed before bodies, then differences among them would have to be due to either their essence (mahiyya) with its concomitants (lawazim) or separable accidents (a'rad mufariqa). The author refutes each possibility in turn. First, difference by essence is impossible because all souls share the same definition; what is shared cannot be what distinguishes them. Second, difference by concomitants is impossible because the essence is one, so its necessary concomitants must also be one. Third, difference by separable accidents is impossible because accidents that are not essential require a subject with varying dispositions; the only such subject for the soul is the body. If the soul existed before the body, there would be no body to provide different dispositions, so no difference could arise. Yet souls are clearly different in their individuations, so they cannot pre-exist. Thus, they must be originated at the time of the body. The proof uses the principle that a common essence cannot be the source of difference, and that separable accidents depend on a receptive matter. This is a standard argument in Islamic philosophy (falsafa), particularly in the tradition of Ibn Sina. The commentary notes at the end clarifies that 'the soul' here refers to the separable accidents causing individuation.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
حادثة
الماهية
اللوازم
العوارض المفارقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because separable accidents are essential to the soul.
B) Because separable accidents require a subject with varying dispositions, which is the body.
C) Because all souls have the same separable accidents.
D) Because separable accidents are impossible in any substance.

Key Takeaway
The rational soul's origination is proven by eliminating its pre-existence through a conditional syllogism that refutes both possible sources of differentiation.

Reconstruct the argument in formal categorical terms: identify the major and minor premises and the middle term. Check if it can be expressed as a categorical syllogism in the second figure.

Essential and Accidental Attributes: The Conceptual Framework Behind the Proof
Proof that the Rational Soul is Originated with the Body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify attributes of an essence into necessary concomitants and separable accidents, using the criteria from this passage.

Core Idea
Essence unites, but accidents require a body to differentiate.

The proof for the soul's origination depends on a subtle distinction between what belongs to an essence necessarily (its concomitants) and what attaches to it accidentally through external factors. In this lesson, we will unpack these terms and see how they function in the argument. Understanding this distinction is crucial for many debates in Islamic metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Define 'essence' (al-mahiyya)
The text says essence is common to all souls. It is what makes a soul a soul. Identify the term in the Arabic: الماهية.
2
Identify necessary concomitants (al-lawazim)
These follow from the essence necessarily. If the essence is one, its concomitants must be one as well. Look for the phrase 'اتحاد الملزوم يستلزم اتحاد اللازم'.
3
Define separable accidents (al-a'rad al-mufariqa)
These are not required by the essence; they depend on an external receiver with varying dispositions. Note the contrast with lawazim.
4
See the argument's logic: differentiate by separating the categories
The author shows that difference cannot arise from what is common (essence and its necessary concomitants), and cannot arise from separable accidents without a body. Thus, souls cannot be different before bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Reductio Proof for the Necessary Being
Author proves the existence of a Necessary Being by reductio

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio argument for the Necessary Being and explain why the assumption of no Necessary Being leads to impossibility.

Core Idea
If everything were contingent, the whole would need an external cause—but that cause must be necessary.

In this passage, the author presents a concise yet powerful demonstration of the existence of the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud). The proof is a reductio argument that begins by assuming the contrary—that no being exists necessarily—and shows that this leads to an impossibility. Understanding this argument is foundational for Islamic philosophy and kalam discussions on the existence of God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assumption
The argument starts by assuming the opposite: 'If there were no existent necessarily existing in itself...' This is the supposition to be reduced to absurdity.
2
Derive the consequence
Under that assumption, all existents are possible in themselves (mumkin li-dhatihi). Since each possible requires an external cause, the entire set of existents would also need an external cause.
3
Recognize the impossibility
That external cause cannot itself be contingent (otherwise it would need another cause, leading to infinite regress). Therefore, it must be necessary. But the assumption denied any necessary being. Hence a contradiction: the assumption leads to its own negation.
4
Conclude existence
Since the assumption is impossible, its negation is necessary: there exists a Necessary Being. The author says 'Fawajibu wujūdihī wājib' – his existence is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات الواجب لذاته أن نقول: إن لم يكن في الوجود موجود واجب لذاته يلزم منه المحال؛ لأن الموجودات بأسرها حينئذ تكون جملة مركبة من آحاد كل واحد منها ممكن لذاته، فتحتاج إلى علة خارجية، والعلم به بديهي، والموجود الخارج عن جميع الممكنات واجب لذاته فيلزم وجود واجب الوجود على تقدير عدمه، وهو محال، فوجوده واجب.
Section on proving the Necessary Being in itself. We say: If there were no existent necessarily existing in itself, then the impossible would follow; because all existents would then be a composite of individuals each of which is possible in itself, so they would need an external cause. And the knowledge of this is self-evident. And the existent outside all possibles is necessary in itself. So the existence of the Necessary Being would be necessary on the assumption of its non-existence, which is impossible. Therefore its existence is necessary.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) for the existence of a Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud) using a reductio ad absurdum. The author begins by stating the conditional: if there is no existent that is necessary in itself, then an impossibility follows. The reasoning is that all existents would then be a composite of individual things each of which is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhatihi). Since each possible requires an external cause for its existence, the entire set would also require an external cause—but this would lead to an infinite regress or circularity, which is impossible. The author notes that this knowledge is self-evident (badihi). Then he argues that the existent that is outside all possibles (i.e., not needing a cause) must be necessary in itself. Therefore, if we suppose its non-existence, that would entail its existence (since the necessary cannot be absent), which is contradictory. Hence, its existence is necessary. This is a concise demonstration typical of Islamic philosophy, akin to the argument that the contingent world requires a necessary cause.

Key Terms
واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The universe would be eternal.
D) The proof would be invalid.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of all beings being contingent necessitates the existence of a Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Write out the assumption and its consequence in your own words, identifying the key terms wājib li-dhātihi and mumkin li-dhātihi.
?
Construct a parallel reductio argument in the context of usul al-fiqh: show that if all legal rulings were based purely on human reason (without divine revelation), then an infinite regress of justification would arise, necessitating a divine lawgiver.
