Two Meanings of Existence: Verbal vs. Real
Author distinguishes meanings of existence before presenting the argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the verbal concept of existence (al-ma'na al-masdari) and the real source of existentness (munsha' al-wujud) as used in Islamic metaphysics.

Core Idea
Existence has two meanings; only the real source is debated in theology.

Before diving into the argument, the author clarifies that the term 'existence' (wujud) is used in different ways. One is the self-evident verbal concept, another is the underlying reality that makes things existent. Philosophers and theologians agree on the verbal meaning but disagree on whether the real source of existence is identical to or additional to the essence of the Necessary Being. Understanding this distinction is essential to following the logical proof that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three uses of 'wujud'
Read the passage and pick out the three distinct meanings the author lists: the verbal concept (ma'na masdari), the verbal 'being' (kawn masdari), and the real source (munsha').
2
Distinguish verbal from real existence
Note that the verbal meaning is self-evident in conception and cannot be identical to any essence. The real source is the locus of existentness and the basis for judgments and effects.
The verbal meaning is not the subject of the philosophers' argument.
3
Locate the disagreement
Identify that philosophers say the real source is identical to the essence; theologians say it is additional. Both sides accept the verbal meaning.
4
Explain why the verbal meaning cannot be identical to an essence
The author says the verbal concept 'does not befit being identical to any reality'. Think about why a verbal concept (like 'running') cannot be the same as the thing it describes.
This prepares for the argument that only the real existence is in question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن وجود واجب الوجود نفس حقيقته لأن وجوده لو كان زائدا على حقيقته لكان عارضا لها، ولو كان عارضا الوجود اعلم أن الوجود قد يطلق ويراد به المعنى المصدري البديهي التصور، ويراد به الكون المصدري، ومعناه بالفارسية: هستن و بودن، وقد يطلق ويراد به منشأ اختراع هذا المعنى المصدري ،ومنبعه وهو مناط الموجودية وترتب الأحكام والآثار، وهو الوجود الأصلي والحقيقي، فاختلف الحكماء والمتكلمون في ذلك المنشأ، فالحكماء قالوا: إنه عين ذاته والمتكلمون حكموا بأنه زائد على ذاته، وأما المعنى المصدري فلا يصلح أن يكون عين حقيقة من الحقائق، فضلا أن يكون عين الواجب، استدل الحكماء على قولهم بأنه لو كان زائدا على ذاته لكان عارضا لحقيقته؛ لامتناع كونه جزءا، ولو كان عارضا لها لكان ممكنا لذاته؛ لأن كل عارض محتاج في وجوده إلى محل يقوم به، وكل محتاج في وجوده إلى الغير ممكن، والممكن لا بد له من جاعل ومؤثر يوجده ويخرجه من العدم، فذلك المؤثر إن كان من نفس تلك الحقيقة الواجبة لذاتها يلزم أن تكون موجودة قبل وجودها العارض لها الممكن المحتاج إليها؛ لأن العلة الموجدة للشيء يجب تقدمها على معلولها بالوجود فيلزم أن تكون تلك الحقيقة الواجبة لذاتها موجودة قبل وجودها؛ لأن الوجود العارض المعلول المتأخر هو وجودها فإذا كان المؤثر هو نفس الحقيقة يلزم أن تكون الحقيقة موجودة قبل وجودها، هذا خلف، وإن كان غير الحقيقة لها كان الوجود من حيث هو مفتقرا إلى الغير، فيكون ممكنا لذاته، فلا بد له من مؤثر، وذلك المؤثر إن كان نفس تلك الحقيقة يلزم أن تكون موجودة قبل الوجود؛ لأن العلة الموجدة للشيء يجب تقدمها على المعلول بالوجود، فيكون الشيء موجودا قبل نفسه، هذا خلف، وإن كان غير تلك الماهية يلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته محتاجا إلى الغير في الوجود، وهذا محال.
Section on that the existence of the Necessary Being is identical to His essence. Because if His existence were additional to His essence, it would be an accident to it. And if it were an accident... Know that 'existence' may be used to mean the verbal concept that is self-evident in conception, and it may be used to mean the verbal 'being' (in Persian: hastan and budan). And it may be used to mean the source of invention of this verbal meaning, and its origin, which is the locus of existentness and the arrangement of judgments and effects, and it is the original and real existence. The philosophers and theologians differed regarding this source: the philosophers said it is identical to the essence, while the theologians ruled that it is additional to the essence. As for the verbal meaning, it is not suitable to be identical to any reality, let alone to be identical to the Necessary. The philosophers argued for their statement: if it were additional to the essence, it would be an accident to its reality, because it cannot be a part. And if it were an accident to it, it would be possible in itself, because every accident needs a substrate in which it subsists, and everything that needs another in its existence is possible. The possible must have a maker and agent that brings it into existence from non-existence. That agent, if it were from the same necessary essence, it would entail that the essence existed before the existence that is an accident to it, because the cause that brings something into existence must precede its effect in existence. So it would entail that the necessary essence existed before its existence, because the accidental existence is its existence, thus the thing would be prior to itself, which is absurd. And if the agent were other than the essence, then existence, as such, would be dependent on another, so it would be possible in itself, and thus must have an agent. If that agent were the same essence, again the same circularity. If the agent were other than that quiddity, then the Necessary Being in itself would need another in its existence, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative section where the author, following the philosophers, establishes that the existence of the Necessary Being is identical to His essence, not an additional attribute. The author begins by stating the conclusion: existence cannot be additional (za'id) to the essence, because if it were, it would be an accident (arad). Then there is a digression explaining the different meanings of 'existence' (al-wujud): the verbal concept (ma'na masdari) which is self-evident, the verbal 'being' (kawn masdari), and the real source (munsha') which is the basis of existentness and effects. It is this last meaning that philosophers and theologians disagree on. The philosophers say it is identical to essence; theologians say it is additional. The author then presents the philosophers' proof: assume existence is additional. It would then be an accident, which is contingent (mumkin) because it needs a substrate. Every contingent requires a cause. If that cause is the essence itself, then the essence would have to exist before its own existence (since the cause precedes the effect in existence), which is circular. If the cause is something else, then the Necessary Being would depend on another, contradicting its necessity. Therefore, existence must be identical to the essence.

Key Terms
وجود (wujūd): Here, in the context of the Necessary Being, it refers to the real existence that is the source of existentness; the author distinguishes this from the verbal concept. [Note: In later logical texts, wujud may be treated as a predicate; here it is a metaphysical notion.]
عارض (ʿāriḍ): An accident that inheres in a substrate; contingent and dependent.
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa): The true essence or reality of a thing, especially of the Necessary Being.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the 'real source' of existence (munsha' al-wujud)?
A) The verbal concept that is self-evident in conception.
B) The origin of the verbal meaning, the locus of existentness and the basis for judgments.
C) The Persian word 'hastan' meaning 'to be'.
D) The essence of the Necessary Being only.

Key Takeaway
Existence (wujud) has two distinct meanings: the verbal concept and the real source; only the latter is debated in metaphysics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a fiqh text, the term 'wajib' is used in two senses: the linguistic meaning (any binding command) and the technical meaning (what the Lawgiver requires definitively). Identify which sense is used in the statement: 'Wajib is what the Lawgiver requires.' Explain why the other sense would not work.

The Philosophers' Proof: Existence Must Be Identical to Essence
Author presents philosophical proof that existence cannot be additional to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the philosophers' compound reductio argument that existence of the Necessary Being is identical to His essence, and identify the key steps that rule out existence as an additional attribute.

Core Idea
If existence were additional to the Necessary Being, it would either be self-caused or caused by another—both impossible.

Now that the distinction between verbal and real existence is clear, the author presents the philosophers' proof. They assume, for the sake of argument, that existence is additional to the essence. Using a step-by-step reductio, they show that this assumption leads to absurdities. This lesson will walk you through each logical step of that proof, which is a compound syllogism combining categorical and conditional reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial assumption
The argument begins by assuming the opposite of what the philosophers believe: that existence is additional (za'id) to the essence of the Necessary Being.
This is a classic reductio assumption.
2
Derive the first consequence: existence would be an accident
If existence is additional, it cannot be a part of the essence, so it must be an accident (arad) that inheres in the essence.
Because the only two options for an additional attribute are part or accident.
3
Establish that every accident is contingent
An accident needs a substrate to exist. Anything that needs another is contingent (mumkin), and contingent things require a cause (ja'il) to bring them into existence.
4
Pursue the two causal possibilities
The cause of this accidental existence must be either the essence itself or something other than the essence. Each branch leads to a contradiction.
5
First branch: cause is the essence itself
If the essence causes its own existence, then the essence would have to exist before its existence (because the cause precedes the effect). Since the accidental existence is the very existence of the essence, this means the essence exists before itself—a circularity.
This is explicitly called 'khalaf' (absurd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Proving That Necessity of Existence Is Identical to the Essence
Author argues that necessity of existence and determination are not additional to the essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that the necessity of existence is identical to the essence of the Necessary Being, and identify the circularity and infinite regress avoided by this identification.

Core Idea
If necessity were an added attribute, it would cause circularity or infinite regress—so it must be identical to the essence.

In Kalam and Falsafa, a key debate is whether divine attributes are identical to or distinct from the essence. This passage from al-Hidayat al-Hikma presents a rigorous proof that the necessity of existence cannot be an added attribute. By working through the argument step by step, you will see how the philosophers establish that wujub al-wujud is identical to the dhat, avoiding logical absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author asserts that the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) and determination (ta'ayyun) are identical to the essence, not additional. Locate this claim in the opening sentence.
The Arabic says: 'فصل في أن وجوب الوجود وتعينه عين ذاته'
2
Understand the first reductio
Assume necessity is additional. Then it would be an effect (ma'lul) of the essence. A cause must itself be necessary to produce an effect, so the necessity of the cause must be essential. This would place necessity prior to the necessary being, making the essence dependent on another—contradicting its necessity.
This leads to the conclusion that necessity must be identical to essence to avoid contradiction.
3
Analyze the second proof
The author recasts: if necessity were not identical, it would be an accident, which is contingent and requires a cause. This cause's necessity must itself be necessary. If the cause's necessity is identical to the effect's necessity, the thing precedes itself. If different, infinite regress. The only escape is a necessity that is identical to the essence.
This is a more formal argument showing the impossibility of composition in the Necessary Being.
4
Apply the terminological clarification
The author notes that terms like 'necessity' can refer to the mental concept (concept) or the real source (munsha'). The philosophers mean the real source, so the argument deals with reality, not mere concepts.
This prevents misunderstanding that they are talking about abstract ideas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن وجوب الوجود وتعينه عين ذاته أما الأول فإن وجوب الوجود لو كان زائدا على حقيقته لكان معلولا لذاته، والعلة ما لم يجب وجودها استحال أن يوجد المعلول، وذلك به الوجوب هو الوجوب بالذات ضرورة، فيكون وجوب الوجود قبل الواجبة، ويلزم افتقار الواجب لذاته في وجوده إلى الغير فلا يكون واجبا، وهذا خلف، فلاح أن وجوده عين حقيقته وهو المطلوب. وجوب الوجود: اعلم أن وجوب الوجود وكذا تعينه قد يطلق ويراد به المفهوم المصدري وقد يراد به المنشأ، وحيث حكموا بصيغة وجوب الوجود وتعينه" أرادوا المنشأ لا المفهوم المصدري والدليل على الأول: أن وجوب الوجود لو لم يكن عين حقيقة لكان عارضا لها؛ لامتناع الجزئية، وممكنا؛ لاحتياج العارض إلى المعروض، ومعلولا لذاته؛ لامتناع احتياج الواجب لذاته في وجوبه إلى الغير، والعلة ما لم يجب وجودها في نفسها لم يكن أن توجد المعلول ضرورة أن وجوب وجود المعلول متفرع على وجوب وجود العلة، فوجوب وجود العلة إن كان عين وجوب وجود المعلول يلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، وإن كان غيره لزم التسلسل أو ينتهي إلى وجوب هو عينه، وهو المطلوب.
Section on that the necessity of existence and its determination are identical to His essence. As for the first: if the necessity of existence were additional to His essence, it would be an effect of His essence. And the cause, unless its existence is necessary, it is impossible for the effect to exist. And that necessity is the necessity by essence, necessarily. So the necessity of existence would be prior to the necessary thing, and it would entail the dependence of the Necessary Being in its existence on another, so it would not be necessary. This is a contradiction. Thus it becomes clear that His existence is identical to His essence, and that is what is sought. Necessity of existence: Know that the necessity of existence and likewise its determination may be used to mean the verbal concept, and may be used to mean the source (munsha'). And when they rule using the expression 'necessity of existence and its determination', they mean the source, not the verbal concept. The proof for the first: if the necessity of existence were not identical to the essence, it would be an accident to it (because it cannot be a part), and possible (because the accident needs the substrate), and an effect of the essence (because the necessary cannot need another for its necessity). The cause, unless its existence is necessary in itself, cannot bring about the effect, necessarily, because the necessity of the effect's existence is branched from the necessity of the cause's existence. So if the necessity of the cause's existence is identical to the necessity of the effect's existence, then the thing would be prior to itself. If it is different, then an infinite regress would follow, or it ends with a necessity that is identical to it, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage argues that the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) and determination (ta'ayyun) are identical to the essence of the Necessary Being, not attributes added to it. The author begins by stating that if necessity of existence were additional, it would be an effect (ma'lul) of the essence. But a cause must itself be necessary to produce an effect; so the necessity of the cause must be essential. This leads to circularity: the necessity of existence would then be prior to the necessary being itself, making the essence dependent on another, contradicting its necessity. Hence, necessity must be identical to the essence. The author then clarifies a terminological point: like 'existence', 'necessity' and 'determination' can refer to either the verbal concept or the real source. The philosophers mean the source. The proof is then recast: if necessity were not identical to essence, it would be an accident, which is contingent and would need an additional cause. This leads to either a vicious circle (if the cause's necessity is the same as the effect's) or an infinite regress (if different). So it must end with a necessity that is identical to the essence, which is what is proved.

Key Terms
وجوب الوجود
تعين
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the necessity of the cause is different from the necessity of the effect in the proof?
A) The cause would be prior to itself.
B) An infinite regress of necessities would follow.
C) The effect would be unnecessary.
D) The proof would be invalid.

Key Takeaway
Necessity of existence must be identical to the essence to avoid an infinite regress of necessities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the proof using the same logical structure but apply it to a different supposed attribute, e.g., if the attribute of 'being eternal' were distinct from the essence, what absurdities would follow? Write a short reductio.
?
Identify whether the following statement commits circularity or infinite regress: 'The Necessary Being is necessary because of its attribute of necessity.' Explain using the reasoning from this lesson.

The Determination Argument for Oneness
First part of the proof: determination and regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio argument showing that if determination were additional to essence, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
The necessary being's determination cannot be separate from its essence, or it would be contingent.

The author presents a logical proof that there can only be one necessary being. He assumes two such beings and examines the basis of their distinction. If that basis is not the entire essence, it must be an additional determination, which leads to a causal chain ending in either circularity or infinite regress. Let's walk through each step and see why both outcomes are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assumption and the distinction
The argument starts with a hypothetical: suppose there are two necessary existents. They share the attribute of necessary existence but must differ in something (the determination, ta'ayyun). Locate the phrases 'لو فرضنا موجودين واجبي الوجود' and 'ما به الامتياز' and recognize that the determination is the basis of their difference.
2
Consider the second case: determination is not the entire essence
The author first rules out that determination is the entire essence (that would make necessary existence external). So focus on the remaining case: determination is additional to the essence. The text says: 'والثاني: والدليل على الثاني...'.
3
Follow the causal chain
If determination is additional, it is an accident ('arad) and must be caused. The necessary being per se cannot rely on another for its determination. So the determination is caused by something else (a cause), and that cause must itself be determined to exist. Trace the argument: 'العلة ما لم تكن متعينة لم توجد'.
4
Examine the two possibilities for the cause's determination
The determination of the cause is either identical to the effect's determination or different. If identical, then the thing precedes itself (circularity). If different, then we need another cause, leading to infinite regress. Both are impossible for the necessary being. Conclude that the additional determination case is absurd.
5
Conclude that the assumption is false
Since both possibilities for the basis of distinction lead to contradiction, the original assumption of two necessary beings must be false. Therefore, only one necessary being exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في توحيد واجب الوجود لأنا لو فرضنا موجودين واجبي الوجود لكانا مشتركين في وجوب الوجود متمايزين بأمر من الأمور، وما به الامتياز إما أن يكون تمام الحقيقة أو لا يكون، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأن الامتياز لو كان بتمام الحقيقة لكان وجوب الوجود لاشتراكه خارجا عن حقيقة كل واحد، والثاني: والدليل على الثاني: أن التعين لو كان زائدا على حقيقته لكان عارضا لها معلولا لها؛ لامتناع افتقار الواجب لذاته في تعينه إلى الغير، والعلة ما لم تكن متعينة لم توجد؛ لامتناع وجود المبهم في الخارج، فالتعين الذي هو ثابت للعلة قبل المعلول إن كان عين التعين الذي هو معلول يلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، وإن كان غيره يلزم التسلسل أو ينتهي إلى تعين هو عينه، وهو المطلوب. ثم قال: أن مفهوم واجب الوجود لا يصدق في الخارج إلا على ذات واحدة، ويمتنع أن يصدق على غيرها، وهذه المسألة متفق عليها بين الفلاسفة والمتكلمين، لا يخالف فيها إلا الثنوية - خذلهم الله تعالى. واستدل الحكماء على التوحيد بأنا لو فرضنا موجودين واجبي الوجود لكان معنى وجوب الوجود مشتركا بينهما، والتعين غير مشترك بينهما؛ لامتناع الامتياز بدون الاختلاف، فكل منهما مشتمل على مفهوم مشترك، وهو مفهوم مميز، فذلك المفهوم المميز إما يكون تمام الحقيقة أو لا، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأن المفهوم المميز لو كان تمام الحقيقة لكان المفهوم المشترك أعني وجوب الوجود خارجا عن حقيقة كل منهما وهو محال؛ لما ثبت من قبل أن وجوب الوجود عين حقيقة الواجب لذاته.
Chapter on the Oneness of Necessary Being: For if we assume two existents that are necessary of existence, they would share in the necessity of existence and be distinguished by some matter. The basis of distinction is either the entire essence or not. The first is impossible because if distinction were by the entire essence, then the necessity of existence, due to its being shared, would be outside the essence of each. The second: and the proof of the second: that if the determination were additional to its essence, it would be an accident of it and caused by it; because it is impossible for the necessary being per se to be in need in its determination of something else, and a cause if it is not determined does not exist; because it is impossible for an indeterminate to exist in the external world. So the determination that is fixed for the cause before the effect: if it is identical with the determination that is the effect, then the priority of the thing over itself follows; and if it is different, then infinite regress follows or it ends in a determination that is identical to it, which is the desired [result]. Then he said: The concept of necessary of existence is not true in the external world except of one essence, and it is impossible for it to be true of any other. This issue is agreed upon between the philosophers and the mutakallimun; none oppose it except the dualists - may Allah forsake them. And the sages have argued for oneness as follows: If we assume two existents that are necessary of existence, then the meaning of necessity of existence would be shared between them, and the determination is not shared between them; because distinction without difference is impossible. So each of them comprises a common concept and a distinguishing concept. That distinguishing concept is either the entire essence or not. The first is impossible because if the distinguishing concept were the entire essence, then the common concept - namely the necessity of existence - would be outside the essence of each, which is impossible; because it has been established earlier that the necessity of existence is identical to the essence of the necessary being per se.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage provides a logical proof for the oneness of God (tawhid) using a reductio ad absurdum. The argument assumes two necessary beings (wajib al-wujud) and shows this leads to contradiction. The author first states the premise: if there were two, they would share the attribute of being necessary but differ in some way. The basis of distinction (ma bihi al-imtiyaz) is examined: either it is the entire essence (tamam al-haqiqa) or not. The first case fails because then the common attribute (wajib al-wujud) would be external to each essence, but it has been established that wajib al-wujud is identical to the essence. The second case (distinction is not the entire essence) leads to the distinguishing factor being an accident or a part. The author provides a detailed proof: if the determination (ta'ayyun) were additional to the essence, it would be an accident and caused, but the necessary being cannot be caused in its determination. Further, the cause must be determined before effect, leading to either circularity or infinite regress, both impossible. Thus, the only coherent conclusion is that there is only one necessary being. The proof concludes by noting the consensus of philosophers and mutakallimun, except the dualists. An alternative, clearer formulation is then given: two necessary beings would share the common concept of necessity but have different determinations; the distinguishing concept either is the whole essence (making necessity external) or not (making each composite and thus contingent). The argument uses categorical reasoning: the middle term is the distinction between common and distinguishing concepts. The Islamic example of wine (khamr) being forbidden due to intoxication is analogous in structure but the content here is purely metaphysical.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
تعين
تمام الحقيقة
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the determination argument, what problem arises if the cause's determination is identical to the effect's determination?
A) The cause would be prior to itself without any regress.
B) The thing would precede itself (circularity), which is absurd.
C) The cause would be identical to the effect, making them one.
D) The effect would be uncaused.

Key Takeaway
The determination argument shows that any additional determination on a necessary being leads to an impossible causal chain, so there can be only one necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar reductio argument for the oneness of the Necessary Being by considering the attribute of 'existence' as a common concept and show that two such beings would require a differentiating factor that leads to composition or regress.
?
Identify where in the argument the principle 'the cause must be determined' is used, and explain why an indeterminate cause cannot exist in the external world.

The Common Concept Proof Against Dualism
Second part: proof using common and distinguishing concepts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two possibilities for the distinguishing concept in a pair of necessary beings and explain why each leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
A shared attribute cannot be the entire essence of each individual.

In this second formulation, the author reframes the proof using the logical tools of common concept (mafhum mushtarak) and distinguishing concept (mafhum mumayyiz). Two necessary beings would share the concept of necessary existence but differ by some distinguishing concept. This distinguishing concept is either the entire essence or not. Both cases are examined and shown to be impossible. Let's dissect this logical dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common and distinguishing concepts
Assume two necessary beings. They share the common concept 'necessity of existence' (wajib al-wujud). They differ by some distinguishing concept (mafhum mumayyiz). Locate the phrase 'فكل منهما مشتمل على مفهوم مشترك، وهو مفهوم مميز'.
2
Case 1: The distinguishing concept is the entire essence
If the distinguishing concept constitutes the entire essence of each being, then the common concept (necessity) would be external to the essence of each. But it has been established that for a necessary being, necessity is identical to the essence. Therefore, this case is impossible. Check the text: 'لأن المفهوم المميز لو كان تمام الحقيقة لكان المفهوم المشترك أعني وجوب الوجود خارجا عن حقيقة كل منهما وهو محال'.
3
Case 2: The distinguishing concept is not the entire essence
If the distinguishing concept is only a part of the essence, then each being would be composed of the common concept (necessity) and the distinguishing concept. Composition implies contingency, because each part depends on the other. But a necessary being cannot be composite. Thus, this case is also impossible.
4
Conclude that two necessary beings cannot exist
Since both cases lead to contradiction, the initial assumption is false. Therefore, the concept of necessary existence can apply to only one being in the external world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Necessary Being's Attributes Are Necessary: A Conditional Proof
Author proves God's attributes are necessary via conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism proving that God's attributes are necessary concomitants of His essence.

Core Idea
If God's attributes depended on another, God would be contingent; they don't, so they are necessary.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma proves that the necessary being's attributes are necessary, not accidental. The author uses a conditional syllogism (modus tollens) to refute the hypothesis that attributes depend on an external cause. By understanding this proof, you will see how Islamic philosophers defend the self-sufficiency of the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being proved
The claim is that the necessary being's attributes are necessary, meaning they are not dependent on any external cause.
2
Locate the conditional premise
The author says: if the essence were not sufficient for its attributes (if they come from another), then that other would cause the attribute's existence and non-existence.
3
Trace the contradiction
The author shows that if attributes depended on another, God's existence would be either with the attribute (contradiction: attribute caused by another but God's existence uncaused) or without the attribute (contradiction: attribute caused by another but absent), leading to impossibility.
4
Formalize the argument
This is modus tollens: If P (attributes depend on another) then Q (God becomes contingent). Not Q (God is necessary). Therefore not P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته واجب من جميع جهاته أي ليس له حالة منتظرة؛ لأن ذاته كافية فيما له من الصفات، فيكون ... إلى الثاني: أي إلى أن لا يكون المفهوم المميز عين حقيقة كل واحد منهما؛ لأنه يلزم حينئذ أن يكون كل واحد منهما مركبا من المفهوم المشترك والمميز، وكل مركب محتاج إلى غيره - وهو جزؤه - فيكون ممكنا لذاته، وقد كان واجبا لذاته، هذا خلف، والأولى أن يقول : لو لم يكن تعين كل واحد منهما عين حقيقته لزم أن يكون عارضا، وقد تبين بطلانه من قبل؛ لما أن مر تعين الواجب عين ذاته، أو يكون جزءا فيلزم التركيب الملزوم ،للإمكان وهو مناف للوجوب، وقد كان واجبا، هذا خلف. الواجب إلخ أي الواجب الوجود لازم لذاته جميع صفاته ليس له في صفاته افتقار إلى الغير؛ لأن ذاته علة تامة لصفاته والدليل على ذلك أنه لو لم تكن ذاته كافية صفاته من غيره، فيكون وجود ذلك الغير علة لوجود تلك الصفة، وعدمه علة لعدمها، ولو كان كذلك لزم أن لا يبقى الواجب لذاته واجبا، واللازم باطل، فكذا الملزوم، وإنما قلنا لزم أن لا يبقى الواجب واجبا؛ لأن وجوبه إما أن يكون مع وجود الصفة أو عدمها، فإن كان مع وجود الصفة لم يكن وجود الصفة معلولا للغير، وقد فرضناه معلولا للغير هذا خلف، وإن كان مع عدم الصفة لم يكن الصفة معلولا للغير لحصوله بذات الواجب بدون اعتبار الغير، وقد فرضنا كونه معلولا للغير هذا خلف ولما لم يكن سبيل إلى الشقين للزوم الخلف لم يكن سبيل إلى وجوبه، هذا حاصل كلام المصنف له.
Chapter on that the necessary being per se is necessary from all its aspects, i.e., it has no potential state; because its essence is sufficient for the attributes it has... To the second: i.e., that the distinguishing concept is not identical to the essence of each of them; because then each of them would be composed of the common concept and the distinguishing concept, and every composite is in need of something else - i.e., its part - so it would be contingent per se, whereas it was necessary per se, which is a contradiction. It is better to say: If the determination of each of them were not identical to its essence, it would be an accident, and its falsity has been shown previously; because the determination of the necessary is identical to its essence, or it would be a part, necessitating composition which implies contingency, which contradicts necessity, and it was necessary, this is a contradiction. 'The necessary etc.' means the necessary of existence is such that all its attributes are necessary for its essence; it has no need in its attributes for another; because its essence is the complete cause of its attributes. The proof of that: if its essence were not sufficient for its attributes [and they came] from another, then the existence of that other would be the cause of the existence of that attribute, and its non-existence would be the cause of its non-existence. If that were the case, then the necessary being per se would not remain necessary. This is a contradiction. And we say it would not remain necessary because its existence is either with the existence of the attribute or without it. If it is with the existence of the attribute, then the existence of the attribute is not caused by another, whereas we assumed it is caused by another - contradiction. If it is without the attribute, then the attribute is not caused by another because it occurs by the essence of the necessary without consideration of another, whereas we assumed it is caused by another - contradiction. Since there is no way to either side due to the resulting contradiction, there is no way to its necessity. This is the gist of the author's statement.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage argues that the necessary being (God) is necessary from all aspects, meaning He has no potentiality and His attributes are necessary. The author first restates part of the previous argument about the distinguishing concept not being identical to the essence, leading to composition and contingency. He then presents the main proof: if the necessary being's essence were not sufficient for its attributes, they would depend on something else. That something else would be a cause of the attribute's existence or non-existence. This dependency would make the necessary being's existence conditional on the attribute: either the attribute exists (then God's existence is with it, but the attribute is caused by another, while God's existence is uncaused - contradiction) or the attribute does not exist (then God's existence is without it, but the attribute is supposed to be caused by another, yet it is not present - contradiction). Thus the assumption fails, proving that God's essence is the complete cause of all attributes. The argument is a conditional syllogism: if attributes depend on another, then God's necessity is compromised; the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. The commentary clarifies that 'complete cause' ('illa tamma) means the essence suffices without external condition. This is a key doctrine in Islamic theology: God's attributes are not accidental but necessary concomitants of His essence. An Islamic example: just as the attribute of 'life' in God is necessary, not derived from elsewhere.

Key Terms
علة تامة
صفات
واجب لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical form does the proof that God's attributes are necessary follow?
A) Categorical syllogism in Barbara
B) Modus ponens
C) Modus tollens
D) Disjunctive syllogism

Key Takeaway
The necessary being's attributes are necessary because any dependency would make His existence conditional, contradicting His self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional syllogism (modus tollens) proving that the Quran is uncreated based on the necessary being's attributes being necessary. Use the premise: If the Quran were created, then God would be contingent (since it is His speech). Then conclude it is not created.

A Trilemma for the Necessary Existent's Immateriality
Author argues through a trilemma that the Necessary Existent must be immaterial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism that proves the Necessary Existent is necessarily immaterial and identify which disjuncts are eliminated.

Core Idea
The Necessary Existent must be immaterial; a trilemma proves it by excluding the only alternatives.

In this passage, the author uses a logical trilemma to demonstrate an essential attribute of God: immateriality. By examining all possible scenarios regarding immateriality and materiality, he shows that only one is consistent with the nature of the Necessary Existent. This argument is a classic example of a disjunctive syllogism in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three disjuncts
The author presents three exhaustive possibilities: (1) immateriality is necessary for the Necessary Existent, (2) materiality is necessary, or (3) neither is necessary.
2
Eliminate the second disjunct
If materiality were necessary, the Necessary Existent would be material, which contradicts His transcendence and is thus absurd.
3
Eliminate the third disjunct
If neither property is necessary, then both are possible, implying that the Necessary Existent depends on a cause for its attributes, contradicting its self-sufficiency.
4
Conclude the first disjunct
Since the other two disjuncts are impossible, the only remaining possibility is that immateriality is necessary for the Necessary Existent. The apparent objection that this would make all contingent beings immaterial is not valid because the Necessary Existent's attributes are unique to It.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته هو إما أن يجب له التجرد أو اللاتجرد أو لا يجب شيء منهما، فإن التجرد وجب أن يكون وجود الممكنات بأسرها مجردا غير عارض للماهيات، وهو محال؛ لأنا نعقل المسبع مع الشك في وجوده الخارجي، فلو كان وجوده نفس حقيقته لكان الشيء الواحد معلوما و مشكوكا في حالة واحدة، وهو محال، وإن وجب له اللاتجرد لما كان وجود الباري تعالى مجردا ، هذا خلف، وإن لم يجب له شيء منهما كان كل واحد منهما ممكنا له فيكون معلولا لعلة فيلزم افتقار واجب الوجود في تجرده إلى غيره، فلا تكون ذاته كافية فيما له من الصفات هذا خلف.
Chapter on that the Necessary Existent: either immateriality is necessary for it, or materiality is necessary for it, or neither is necessary for it. If immateriality is necessary, then the existence of all contingent beings would be immaterial, not accidental to quiddities, which is absurd; for we conceive a heptagon while doubting its external existence, so if its existence were its essence, the same thing would be known and doubted in one state, which is absurd. And if materiality is necessary for it, then the existence of the Exalted Creator would not be immaterial, which is a contradiction. And if neither is necessary for it, then each would be possible for it, so it would be an effect of a cause, necessitating the dependence of the Necessary Existent in its immateriality upon another, so its essence would not be sufficient for its attributes, which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents a logical trilemma to prove that the Necessary Existent (God) is necessarily immaterial. The author examines three possibilities: immateriality is necessary, materiality is necessary, or neither is necessary. The first leads to the absurdity that all contingent beings would have immaterial existence, contradicting our ability to conceive a heptagon without its external existence. The second would make God material, contradicting His nature. The third would make both properties possible for God, implying dependence on a cause and thus contradicting His self-sufficiency. Therefore, immateriality is necessary for the Necessary Existent.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Existent must be immaterial; the trilemma shows that materiality or neutrality would violate its necessary attributes.

Self-Knowledge and the Logical Error of Forcing Duality
Author refutes the objection that self-knowledge implies duality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical fallacy of conflating general and specific in objections to self-knowledge, and reconstruct the author's dialectical refutation.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge proves that knowledge does not require two distinct entities.

The objection raised against divine self-knowledge is based on a mistaken assumption: that all knowledge requires a relation between knower and known. The author shows this assumption is false by appealing to a simple example and a logical principle about general and specific. This lesson walks you through the dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (mu'tarid)
Find the objector's claim in the passage: knowledge is a relation between knower and known, so it necessitates difference (taghayur). Since difference between a thing and itself is impossible, self-knowledge is impossible.
2
Extract the author's answer (jawab)
The author argues that self-intellection does not require difference. He defines knowledge as the presence of the reality of a thing abstracted, which is more general than presence of something else.
3
Analyze the logical tool: general vs. specific
The author uses the principle that the falsity of the specific (presence of something else) does not entail the falsity of the general (presence to itself). This blocks the objection.
4
Check the example: human self-knowledge
The author gives an example: each person intellects their own self by their own self. If this were impossible, we would need two souls, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته هداية تعقل الشيء لذاته لا يقتضي التغاير بين العاقل والمعقول؛ لأن العلم هو حضور حقيقة الشيء مجردة، وهذا أعم من حضور حقيقة الشيء المغاير، ولا يلزم من كذب الأخص كذب الأعم؛ لأن كل واحد من الناس يعقل ذاته بذاته، وإلا لكان له نفسان: إحداهما عاقلة، والأخرى معقولة، هذا خلف. هداية: أوردها لدفع اشتباه، وهو أن العلم نسبة بين العالم والمعلوم، فتقتضي التغاير ولا يعقل التغاير بين الشيء ونفسه، فكيف يكون عالما بنفسه؟ فأجاب عنه بأن تعقل الشيء لذاته لا يقتضي التغاير إلخ.
Chapter on that the Necessary Existent — guidance: the intellection of a thing by itself does not necessitate difference between the intellect and the intelligible; because knowledge is the presence of the reality of a thing abstracted, and this is more general than the presence of the reality of something else, and the falsity of the specific does not entail the falsity of the more general; because each person intellects his own self by his own self, otherwise he would have two souls: one intellecting and the other intellected, which is a contradiction. Guidance: It was mentioned to remove a confusion, which is that knowledge is a relation between knower and known, so it necessitates difference, and difference between a thing and itself is inconceivable, so how can it know itself? So he answered that the intellection of a thing by itself does not necessitate difference, etc.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection to God's self-knowledge: knowledge seems to require a relation between two distinct entities, but God is one. The author argues that self-intellection does not require such difference. He uses a logical point: knowledge is the presence of the reality of a thing, which is more general than the presence of something else. The falsity of the specific case (presence of something else) does not imply the falsity of the general case (presence of a thing to itself). He gives an example: each human intellection of themselves is a case of self-knowledge; otherwise we would need two souls. The commentary (هداية) clarifies the objection and answer.

Key Takeaway
Self-knowledge is a counterexample to the claim that all knowledge requires duality, and the logical tool of general vs. specific guards against overgeneralization.

The Necessary Existent Knows Universals: A Categorical Syllogism
Author proves that the Necessary Existent knows universals because it is immaterial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving that the Necessary Existent knows universals and verify its validity.

Core Idea
Immateriality grounds knowledge of universals.

This passage presents a concise proof from immateriality to knowledge of universals. The author uses a categorical syllogism where the major premise is considered self-evident. We will break down the syllogism, identify its components, and check its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Find the statement that is being proven. The conclusion is 'al-wājibu li-dhātihi ‘ālimun bi-l-kulliyyāt' (the Necessary Existent knows universals).
The conclusion is stated at the beginning of the passage.
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise is the subject of the conclusion linked to the middle term. Here it is 'al-wājibu li-dhātihi mujarradun ‘ani l-mādda' (the Necessary Existent is immaterial). The author says it has been established earlier.
The minor premise is not argued here but referenced from a previous chapter.
3
Identify the major premise
The major premise is the general rule: 'kullu mujarradin ‘ani l-mādda yajibu an yakūna ‘āliman bi-l-kulliyyāt' (everything immaterial must know universals). The author calls it self-evident (badīhī).
The major premise links immateriality to knowledge of universals.
4
Determine the figure and mood
The middle term is 'immaterial' which appears in both premises. The major premise is universal affirmative (A), the minor premise is universal affirmative (A), and the conclusion is universal affirmative (A). This is first figure, mood Barbara (AAA-1).
First figure requires the middle term to be subject of major and predicate of minor.
5
Verify validity
Barbara is a valid mood in the first figure. The conclusion follows necessarily from the premises. Thus, if the premises are true, the conclusion is certain.
The author trusts the major premise as self-evident, so the proof stands on that foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته عالم بالكليات لأنه مجرد عن المادة ولواحقها، وكل مجرد عن المادة ولواحقها يجب أن يكون عالما بالكليات، أما الصغرى فقد مر ذكرها، وأما الكبرى فلأن كل مجرد يمكن بالإمكان العام أن يعقل، وهذا بديهي لا خفاء فيه.
Chapter on that the Necessary Existent is knowing of universals because it is immaterial and free from matter and its concomitants, and everything that is immaterial and free from matter and its concomitants must be knowing of universals. As for the minor premise, its mention has passed; as for the major premise, it is because every immaterial being can, by general possibility, intellect, and this is self-evident, no concealment in it.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents a syllogism proving that the Necessary Existent knows universals. The minor premise (the Necessary Existent is immaterial) is already established in a previous chapter. The major premise is that all immaterial beings are capable of intellection of universals. The author asserts this major premise as self-evident: immateriality entails the ability to know universals. Thus, the conclusion follows: the Necessary Existent knows universals.

Key Takeaway

Analogical Proof of God's Knowledge from Design
Author presents a teleological argument for God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the teleological argument for God's knowledge as an analogical inference and identify its premises.

Core Idea
The design of the world points to its Maker's knowledge with rational necessity.

In this lesson, we examine a concise teleological argument presented by the author. The argument uses the observable order and purpose in the world to prove that God has knowledge of all things before they exist. This is a classic example of analogical reasoning (tamthil) in Islamic theology, where the world is compared to a human artifact, and the conclusion follows from the similarity in design.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original case (asl)
Find the familiar case that is used as the basis of the analogy. In this argument, the asl is a 'beautiful building' that you observe.
The building is a human artifact with clear design.
2
Identify the ruling (hukm) of the original case
Determine what is attributed to the asl. Here, upon seeing the beautiful building, you know that its maker is wise and knowledgeable.
The hukm is 'the maker is wise and knowing'.
3
Identify the effective cause ('illah)
Find the common attribute that links the original and the new case. The 'illah is the presence of intricate order, benefits, and wisdom in the design.
The 'illah is the observable order and purpose.
4
Apply the analogy to the new case (far')
The new case is the entire world with its wonderful pattern and benefits. Since it shares the 'illah of intricate design, the same hukm applies: the Creator is wise and knowing, and this includes knowledge of all things before creation.
The conclusion goes beyond mere existence to attribute knowledge.
5
Assess the strength of the analogy
The author claims this is a rational necessity (darurah 'aqliyyah). Evaluate whether the analogy is strong by checking if the 'illah is present in both cases and if there are no relevant differences that would break the analogy.
Islamic theologians often argue this is a certain proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل على كونه تعالى عالما بالأشياء كلها قبل حدوثها بأن نظام العالم على هذا النمط البديع مع ما يشتمل عليه من المصالح والحكم لا يمكن بدون صانع عليم حكيم بالضرورة العقلية، كما إذا رأيت بنيانا حسنا علمت أن صانعه عليم حكيم.
And it is also inferred that He Almighty is knowledgeable of all things before their creation from the fact that the order of the world in this wonderful pattern, along with what it contains of benefits and wisdoms, cannot be without a wise knowing Creator by rational necessity, just as when you see a beautiful building you know that its maker is wise and knowing.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a concise teleological argument (dalil al-nizam) for God's comprehensive knowledge. The author states that the world's order, with its intricate design and evident benefits and wisdoms, necessitates a Creator who is both knowledgeable ('alim) and wise (hakim). This is a matter of rational necessity (darurah 'aqliyyah). The analogy of a beautiful building is given: when one sees a well-constructed building, one immediately knows that its builder is wise and knowledgeable. Similarly, the cosmos itself points to a wise and all-knowing Creator. This argument is often used in Islamic theology to prove God's knowledge of all particulars, including future events, as the design implies knowledge of ends and means before creation. The argument is analogical (tamthil) in form, comparing the world to a human artifact.

Key Terms

C) The impossibility of accidents without a substance.
D) The universality of knowledge in immaterial beings.

Key Takeaway
The teleological argument from design, when valid, yields rational certainty about God's knowledge of all things before creation.

The Possibility Argument for God's Knowledge of Universals
Compound demonstration of God's knowledge from possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound syllogism that proves the Necessary Being can know universals, using the principle of general possibility.

Core Idea
If God can be conceived alongside other concepts, He can know all things.

This lesson unpacks the author's main argument for God's knowledge of universals. The reasoning proceeds through a chain of inferences from the possibility of the Necessary Being being intelligible to the necessity that He actually knows all universals. We will break down each step of this compound syllogism and apply it to Islamic theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first premise
The author states: 'Whatever can be understood by itself can be understood with each of the intelligibles.' This is a universal statement linking solitary conceivability to concomitant conceivability.
This premise relies on the nature of conceptualization.
2
Apply to the Necessary Being
The Necessary Being itself is something that can be understood as a concept. Therefore, it can be understood alongside all other intelligibles. This yields the intermediate conclusion: the Necessary Being can be accompanied by all intelligibles in the mind.
This step is a simple instantiation of the universal premise.
3
Bridge mental possibility to absolute possibility
The author invokes the principle that 'general possibility precedes specific possibility.' If something is possible in the mind, it is possible absolutely. Thus, the accompaniment of intelligibles with the Necessary Being is possible not only in the mind but also in external reality.
Imkan 'amm (general possibility) is a key metaphysical principle in Islamic philosophy.
4
From possibility to actuality
Since the Necessary Being has no potential—it is necessary from all aspects—any attribute that is possible for it must be actual. Therefore, the Necessary Being actually knows all universals.
This step completes the demonstration that God's knowledge is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني في العلم بالصنائع وصفاته
1.2
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته
ما يمكن أن يعقل وحده يمكن أن يعقل مع كل واحد من المعقولات لا محالة، فيمكن أن يقارنه سائر المعقولات في النفس، فإن الإدراك والتعقل هو حضور صورة المعقول في العقل مجردة عن المادة ولواحقها وكل ما يمكن أن يقارنه سائر المعقولات في العقل يمكن أن يقارنه
=
كما ثبت من قبل، والشيء المتشخص يستحيل حصوله بنفسه في الذهن، إنما الحصول الذهني للطبائع الكلية نعم يتصور بالوجه وهذا لا يفيد في هذا المقام؛ لأن مناط الاستدلال على اقتران الواجب لذاته بالصور المعقولة في الذهن، ولما امتنع حصوله في الذهن امتنع الاقتران وقد يستدل على كونه تعالى عالما بالأشياء كلها قبل حدوثها بأن نظام العالم على هذا النمط البديع مع ما يشتمل عليه من المصالح والحكم لا يمكن بدون صانع عليم حكيم بالضرورة العقلية، كما إذا رأيت بنيانا حسنا علمت أن صانعه عليم حكيم.
ما يمكن حاصل الاستدلال أن الواجب لذاته يمكن أن يكون معقولا، وكل ما يمكن أن يكون معقولا وحده يمكن أن يكون معقولا مع سائر المعقولات؛ لأنه لا حجر في التصور، فينتج أن الواجب لذاته يمكن أن يكون معقولا مع سائر المعقولات، فيمكن أن يقارن الواجب لذاته سائر المعقولات في الذهن، وكل ما يمكن لذات الواجب لذاته في الذهن يمكنه مطلقا؛ لأن إمكان العام مقدم على إمكان الخاص، وقد ثبت إمكان اقتران المعقولات بالواجب لذاته في الذهن، فقد ثبت إمكان اقترانها مطلقا بدون الذهن فقد ظهر إمكان اقتران المعقولات بالواجب لذاته في الخارج، ولا يمكن اقتران المعقولات بالواجب لذاته في الخارج إلا بأن يكون الواجب محلا لها؛ لامتناع حلول الواجب في شيء، وكل ما يمكن للواجب من الصفات فهو حاصل له بالفعل؛ لما مر من أن الواجب لذاته واجب من جميع جهاته، ليس له كمال منتظر.

الفن الثاني في العلم بالصنائع وصفاته
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته
سائر المعقولات لذاته، وكل ما يمكن لواجب الوجود بالإمكان العام يجب وجوده له وإلا لكان له حالة منتظرة، هذا خلف، فإن قيل: لو كان الباري تعالى عالما بشيء لكان فاعلا لتلك الصورة وقابلا لها، وهو محال؛ لأن القابل هو الذي يستعد للشيء، والفاعل هو الذي يفعل الشيء، والأول غير الثاني فيلزم التركيب. قلنا: لم لا يجوز أن يكون الشيء الواحد مستعدا للشيء التصوري ومفيدا له، وهذا لأن معنى كونه مستعدا للشيء أنه لا يمتنع لذاته أن يتصوره، ومعنى كونه فاعلا أنه مقدم بالعلية على ذلك التصور فلم قلتم : إنهما متنافيان؟ ومن اعتقد
أن علمه تعالى بالأشياء نفس ذاته اعتقد نفي العلم بالحقيقة.
لذاته والنتيجة: فيمكن أن يقارن المجرد سائر المعقولات لذاته، فيمكن أن يكون عالما بالكليات فنضم هذه النتيجة إلى قولنا وكل ما يمكن للمجرد بالإمكان العام يجب وجوده بالفعل، وإلا لكان بالقوة فيلزم كونه ماديا وقد كان مجردا، هذا خلف وعلى هذا كان على المصنف محلله أن يقول مكان الواجب الوجود: المجرد، حتى يكون هذا الكلام كبرى للسابق وينتظم كما لا يخفى على المتأمل. القابل حاصل الاعتراض: أن الباري لو كان عالما بالكليات لكان صورها مرتسمة فيه، فيكون قابلا لها وفاعلا لها؛ لامتناع احتياج الواجب في صفاته إلى الغير، والشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا إلا من جهتين فيلزم التركيب في ذاته، وهو مناف للوجوب، وحاصل الجواب: أن القابلية والفاعلية إضافيتان عارضتان للذات بالنسبة إلى الصورة ليستا داخلتين حتى يلزم التركيب. ذاته وهو مسلك المحققين اختاروه للتصفي عن الشبهات التي ترد على القول بالصورة، وهو لزوم الجهل قبل إيجادها، والاضطرار والإيجاب في صدورها.
The second art on knowledge of the crafts and their attributes. 1.2 Section on that the Necessary Being. Whatever can be understood by itself can necessarily be understood with each of the intelligibles, so the rest of the intelligibles can accompany it in the soul, for perception and intellection is the presence of the form of the intelligible in the mind stripped of matter and its accidents. And everything that can be accompanied by the rest of the intelligibles in the mind can be accompanied by them, as has been established before. But a particular thing is impossible to occur by itself in the mind; only universal natures occur in the mind. Yes, it is conceived by a face, but this does not benefit in this context; because the basis of the inference is on the accompaniment of the Necessary Being with intelligible forms in the mind. And since its occurrence in the mind is impossible, accompaniment is impossible. It may also be inferred that He, exalted, is Knower of all things before their occurrence by the fact that the system of the world in this wonderful pattern, along with what it contains of benefits and wisdoms, cannot be without a wise, knowing Creator by rational necessity, just as when you see a beautiful building, you know that its builder is wise and knowing. The gist of the inference is that the Necessary Being can be intelligible, and everything that can be intelligible by itself can be intelligible with the rest of the intelligibles, because there is no impediment in conception. So it results that the Necessary Being can be intelligible with the rest of the intelligibles. Thus the Necessary Being can be accompanied by the rest of the intelligibles in the mind. And whatever is possible for the essence of the Necessary Being in the mind is possible for it absolutely, because general possibility precedes specific possibility. And the possibility of the accompaniment of the intelligibles with the Necessary Being in the mind has been established, so the possibility of their accompaniment absolutely without the mind has been established. So the possibility of accompaniment of the intelligibles with the Necessary Being in the external world has become apparent. And accompaniment of the intelligibles with the Necessary Being in the external world is impossible unless the Necessary Being is a locus for them, due to the impossibility of the Necessary Being's inherence in something. And every attribute possible for the Necessary Being is actually present for it; because of what has passed that the Necessary Being is necessary from all its aspects, having no potential perfection. The second art on knowledge of the crafts and their attributes. Section on that the Necessary Being... the rest of the intelligibles for itself. And everything that is possible for the Necessary Being by general possibility must exist for it, otherwise it would have a potential state—this is absurd. For if it is said: If the Exalted Creator were knower of something, He would be the agent of that form and its receptacle, which is impossible; because the receptacle is that which is prepared for the thing, and the agent is that which does the thing, and the first is not the second, so composition follows. We say: Why is it not permissible that one single thing be prepared for a conceptual thing and also cause it? This is because the meaning of its being prepared for a thing is that it is not impossible in itself to conceive it, and the meaning of its being agent is that it is prior by causality to that conception. So why do you say they are mutually exclusive? And whoever believes that His knowledge of things is His very essence, believes in negation of knowledge in reality. For itself and the result: So the abstract can be accompanied by the rest of the intelligibles for itself, so it can be knower of universals. We then attach this result to our statement: Everything that is possible for the abstract by general possibility must exist in actuality, otherwise it would be potential, leading to its being material while it was abstract—this is absurd. And based on this, the author's analyst should say 'the abstract' instead of 'the Necessary Being', so that this statement becomes a major premise for the previous one and is properly ordered, as is not hidden from the contemplative. The receptacle: the gist of the objection is that if the Creator were knower of universals, their forms would be imprinted in Him, so He would be receptacle and agent for them—because of the impossibility of the Necessary Being needing others for His attributes—and one thing cannot be receptacle and agent except from two aspects, so composition follows in His essence, which contradicts necessity. And the gist of the answer is that receptivity and agency are relative attributes that are accidents to the essence with respect to the form, not intrinsic so that composition would follow. His essence... and this is the path of the verifiers, which they chose to clear away the doubts that occur against the position of forms, namely the necessity of ignorance before their creation, and compulsion and obligation in their emanation.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a demonstrative argument for the Necessary Being's knowledge of universals, followed by an objection and its answer. The author begins with the second art on logic (or sciences). He then presents a section title. The first argument: anything that can be understood by itself can also be understood alongside other concepts. The Necessary Being is something that can be understood (as a concept), so it can be understood together with other intelligibles. This implies that the Necessary Being can be accompanied by intelligibles in the mind. Since possibility in the mind entails absolute possibility (because general possibility precedes specific), the Necessary Being can have intelligibles accompanying it externally, meaning it is a locus for them. This leads to the conclusion that the Necessary Being must actually possess all possible attributes, including knowledge of universals. The author then provides an alternative argument from the order and wisdom in the world, which necessarily implies a wise and knowing creator. He reformulates the argument syllogistically. Then, on page 106, an objection is raised: If God knows something, He would be both the agent (giving existence to the form) and the receptacle (receiving the form). But one thing cannot be both agent and patient simultaneously without composition (tarkib) in the essence, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. The answer: being prepared (qabiliyya) and being agent (fa'iliyya) are relative attributes (idafiyyat) that supervene on the essence in relation to the form; they are not intrinsic parts. Therefore, no composition follows. The author also notes a terminological correction: the earlier argument about possibility should refer to 'the abstract' (al-mujarrad) rather than 'the Necessary Being', so that the reasoning is consistent. The passage ends by noting that this path is chosen by the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) to avoid doubts about forms, such as ignorance before creation and compulsion in emanation.

معقول
إمكان عام

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being's knowledge of universals is demonstrated through a chain of reasoning from conceivability to actuality, using the principle of general possibility.

intermediate
8 min
Objection and Answer: God as Agent and Receptacle of Knowledge
Dialectical objection and author's response regarding composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the objection of composition (tarkib) against God's knowledge and explain the response that agent and receptacle are relational attributes.

Core Idea
God's knowledge does not entail composition because 'agent' and 'receptacle' are relational, not intrinsic.

A common objection to the claim that God knows universals is that He would then be both agent (giving existence to the form) and receptacle (receiving the form), which are distinct roles and would imply composition in His essence. This lesson examines the objection and the author's response, which distinguishes between intrinsic and relational attributes.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
If God knows a universal, then the form of that universal is imprinted in Him. He would be the agent of that form (since He causes it) and its receptacle (since He contains it). These are two different roles, so God would have composition in His essence.
The objection is based on the principle that one thing cannot be both agent and patient of the same thing in the same respect.
2
Identify the underlying assumption
The assumption is that 'being agent' and 'being receptacle' are intrinsic attributes that would partition the essence. The objector sees them as mutually exclusive and distinct parts.
This assumption leads to the charge of tarkib (composition).
3
Analyze the response
The author answers that receptivity (qabiliyya) and agency (fa'iliyya) are relative attributes (idafiyyat) that accrue to the essence in relation to the form. They are not intrinsic parts but accidental relations. Therefore, no composition follows.
The response preserves divine simplicity by denying that these attributes are real distinctions within the essence.

Source Text

الفن الثاني في العلم بالصنائع وصفاته
1.2
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته
ما يمكن أن يعقل وحده يمكن أن يعقل مع كل واحد من المعقولات لا محالة، فيمكن أن يقارنه سائر المعقولات في النفس، فإن الإدراك والتعقل هو حضور صورة المعقول في العقل مجردة عن المادة ولواحقها وكل ما يمكن أن يقارنه سائر المعقولات في العقل يمكن أن يقارنه
=
كما ثبت من قبل، والشيء المتشخص يستحيل حصوله بنفسه في الذهن، إنما الحصول الذهني للطبائع الكلية نعم يتصور بالوجه وهذا لا يفيد في هذا المقام؛ لأن مناط الاستدلال على اقتران الواجب لذاته بالصور المعقولة في الذهن، ولما امتنع حصوله في الذهن امتنع الاقتران وقد يستدل على كونه تعالى عالما بالأشياء كلها قبل حدوثها بأن نظام العالم على هذا النمط البديع مع ما يشتمل عليه من المصالح والحكم لا يمكن بدون صانع عليم حكيم بالضرورة العقلية، كما إذا رأيت بنيانا حسنا علمت أن صانعه عليم حكيم.
ما يمكن حاصل الاستدلال أن الواجب لذاته يمكن أن يكون معقولا، وكل ما يمكن أن يكون معقولا وحده يمكن أن يكون معقولا مع سائر المعقولات؛ لأنه لا حجر في التصور، فينتج أن الواجب لذاته يمكن أن يكون معقولا مع سائر المعقولات، فيمكن أن يقارن الواجب لذاته سائر المعقولات في الذهن، وكل ما يمكن لذات الواجب لذاته في الذهن يمكنه مطلقا؛ لأن إمكان العام مقدم على إمكان الخاص، وقد ثبت إمكان اقتران المعقولات بالواجب لذاته في الذهن، فقد ثبت إمكان اقترانها مطلقا بدون الذهن فقد ظهر إمكان اقتران المعقولات بالواجب لذاته في الخارج، ولا يمكن اقتران المعقولات بالواجب لذاته في الخارج إلا بأن يكون الواجب محلا لها؛ لامتناع حلول الواجب في شيء، وكل ما يمكن للواجب من الصفات فهو حاصل له بالفعل؛ لما مر من أن الواجب لذاته واجب من جميع جهاته، ليس له كمال منتظر.

الفن الثاني في العلم بالصنائع وصفاته
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته
سائر المعقولات لذاته، وكل ما يمكن لواجب الوجود بالإمكان العام يجب وجوده له وإلا لكان له حالة منتظرة، هذا خلف، فإن قيل: لو كان الباري تعالى عالما بشيء لكان فاعلا لتلك الصورة وقابلا لها، وهو محال؛ لأن القابل هو الذي يستعد للشيء، والفاعل هو الذي يفعل الشيء، والأول غير الثاني فيلزم التركيب. قلنا: لم لا يجوز أن يكون الشيء الواحد مستعدا للشيء التصوري ومفيدا له، وهذا لأن معنى كونه مستعدا للشيء أنه لا يمتنع لذاته أن يتصوره، ومعنى كونه فاعلا أنه مقدم بالعلية على ذلك التصور فلم قلتم : إنهما متنافيان؟ ومن اعتقد
أن علمه تعالى بالأشياء نفس ذاته اعتقد نفي العلم بالحقيقة.
لذاته والنتيجة: فيمكن أن يقارن المجرد سائر المعقولات لذاته، فيمكن أن يكون عالما بالكليات فنضم هذه النتيجة إلى قولنا وكل ما يمكن للمجرد بالإمكان العام يجب وجوده بالفعل، وإلا لكان بالقوة فيلزم كونه ماديا وقد كان مجردا، هذا خلف وعلى هذا كان على المصنف محلله أن يقول مكان الواجب الوجود: المجرد، حتى يكون هذا الكلام كبرى للسابق وينتظم كما لا يخفى على المتأمل. القابل حاصل الاعتراض: أن الباري لو كان عالما بالكليات لكان صورها مرتسمة فيه، فيكون قابلا لها وفاعلا لها؛ لامتناع احتياج الواجب في صفاته إلى الغير، والشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا إلا من جهتين فيلزم التركيب في ذاته، وهو مناف للوجوب، وحاصل الجواب: أن القابلية والفاعلية إضافيتان عارضتان للذات بالنسبة إلى الصورة ليستا داخلتين حتى يلزم التركيب. ذاته وهو مسلك المحققين اختاروه للتصفي عن الشبهات التي ترد على القول بالصورة، وهو لزوم الجهل قبل إيجادها، والاضطرار والإيجاب في صدورها.
The second art on knowledge of the crafts and their attributes. 1.2 Section on that the Necessary Being. Whatever can be understood by itself can necessarily be understood with each of the intelligibles, so the rest of the intelligibles can accompany it in the soul, for perception and intellection is the presence of the form of the intelligible in the mind stripped of matter and its accidents. And everything that can be accompanied by the rest of the intelligibles in the mind can be accompanied by them, as has been established before. But a particular thing is impossible to occur by itself in the mind; only universal natures occur in the mind. Yes, it is conceived by a face, but this does not benefit in this context; because the basis of the inference is on the accompaniment of the Necessary Being with intelligible forms in the mind. And since its occurrence in the mind is impossible, accompaniment is impossible. It may also be inferred that He, exalted, is Knower of all things before their occurrence by the fact that the system of the world in this wonderful pattern, along with what it contains of benefits and wisdoms, cannot be without a wise, knowing Creator by rational necessity, just as when you see a beautiful building, you know that its builder is wise and knowing. The gist of the inference is that the Necessary Being can be intelligible, and everything that can be intelligible by itself can be intelligible with the rest of the intelligibles, because there is no impediment in conception. So it results that the Necessary Being can be intelligible with the rest of the intelligibles. Thus the Necessary Being can be accompanied by the rest of the intelligibles in the mind. And whatever is possible for the essence of the Necessary Being in the mind is possible for it absolutely, because general possibility precedes specific possibility. And the possibility of the accompaniment of the intelligibles with the Necessary Being in the mind has been established, so the possibility of their accompaniment absolutely without the mind has been established. So the possibility of accompaniment of the intelligibles with the Necessary Being in the external world has become apparent. And accompaniment of the intelligibles with the Necessary Being in the external world is impossible unless the Necessary Being is a locus for them, due to the impossibility of the Necessary Being's inherence in something. And every attribute possible for the Necessary Being is actually present for it; because of what has passed that the Necessary Being is necessary from all its aspects, having no potential perfection. For itself and the result: So the abstract can be accompanied by the rest of the intelligibles for itself, so it can be knower of universals. And based on this, the author's analyst should say 'the abstract' instead of 'the Necessary Being', so that this statement becomes a major premise for the previous one and is properly ordered, as is not hidden from the contemplative. The Terminological Refinement: 'Abstract' instead of 'Necessary Being'
Author's correction of syllogistic term for consistency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the author recommends replacing 'واجب لذاته' with 'مجرد' in the syllogism to ensure a consistent middle term.

Core Idea
Using 'mujarrad' (abstract) instead of 'wajib li-dhatihi' keeps the middle term identical in both premises.

The author notes a subtle flaw in the formulation of the earlier syllogism: using 'Necessary Being' in one premise and 'abstract' in another breaks the syllogistic form. He corrects this by replacing 'wajib' with 'mujarrad' so that the major and minor premises share a common middle term. This lesson examines why precision in terms is crucial for logical validity.

Guided Path
1
Observe the original syllogism
The author had used 'واجب لذاته' in the minor premise (the Necessary Being can be accompanied) and 'مجرد' in the major premise (everything possible for the abstract must be actual). The middle term is not identical.
This mismatch would invalidate a categorical syllogism.
2
Identify the correction
The author says to replace 'واجب الوجود' with 'المجرد' so that the major premise reads: 'Everything possible for the abstract by general possibility must exist.' This makes 'abstract' the middle term in both premises.
The corrected syllogism is: Minor premise: 'The abstract can be accompanied by intelligibles.' Major premise: 'Everything possible for the abstract must be actual.' Conclusion: 'Therefore the abstract actually has those intelligibles.'
3
Understand the reason
The term 'abstract' is broader and directly refers to any immaterial being, which includes the Necessary Being. Using it ensures the middle term is uniform, making the syllogism formally valid.
This refinement shows the author's logical rigor.
