الفن الثاني في العلم بالصنائع وصفاته
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته
سائر المعقولات لذاته، وكل ما يمكن لواجب الوجود بالإمكان العام يجب وجوده له وإلا لكان له حالة منتظرة، هذا خلف، فإن قيل: لو كان الباري تعالى عالما بشيء لكان فاعلا لتلك الصورة وقابلا لها، وهو محال؛ لأن القابل هو الذي يستعد للشيء، والفاعل هو الذي يفعل الشيء، والأول غير الثاني فيلزم التركيب. قلنا: لم لا يجوز أن يكون الشيء الواحد مستعدا للشيء التصوري ومفيدا له، وهذا لأن معنى كونه مستعدا للشيء أنه لا يمتنع لذاته أن يتصوره، ومعنى كونه فاعلا أنه مقدم بالعلية على ذلك التصور فلم قلتم : إنهما متنافيان؟ ومن اعتقد
أن علمه تعالى بالأشياء نفس ذاته اعتقد نفي العلم بالحقيقة.
لذاته والنتيجة: فيمكن أن يقارن المجرد سائر المعقولات لذاته، فيمكن أن يكون عالما بالكليات فنضم هذه النتيجة إلى قولنا وكل ما يمكن للمجرد بالإمكان العام يجب وجوده بالفعل، وإلا لكان بالقوة فيلزم كونه ماديا وقد كان مجردا، هذا خلف وعلى هذا كان على المصنف محلله أن يقول مكان الواجب الوجود: المجرد، حتى يكون هذا الكلام كبرى للسابق وينتظم كما لا يخفى على المتأمل. القابل حاصل الاعتراض: أن الباري لو كان عالما بالكليات لكان صورها مرتسمة فيه، فيكون قابلا لها وفاعلا لها؛ لامتناع احتياج الواجب في صفاته إلى الغير، والشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا إلا من جهتين فيلزم التركيب في ذاته، وهو مناف للوجوب، وحاصل الجواب: أن القابلية والفاعلية إضافيتان عارضتان للذات بالنسبة إلى الصورة ليستا داخلتين حتى يلزم التركيب. ذاته وهو مسلك المحققين اختاروه للتصفي عن الشبهات التي ترد على القول بالصورة، وهو لزوم الجهل قبل إيجادها، والاضطرار والإيجاب في صدورها.
The second art on knowledge of the crafts and their attributes. 1.2 Section on that the Necessary Being. Whatever can be understood by itself can necessarily be understood with each of the intelligibles, so the rest of the intelligibles can accompany it in the soul, for perception and intellection is the presence of the form of the intelligible in the mind stripped of matter and its accidents. And everything that can be accompanied by the rest of the intelligibles in the mind can be accompanied by them, as has been established before. But a particular thing is impossible to occur by itself in the mind; only universal natures occur in the mind. Yes, it is conceived by a face, but this does not benefit in this context; because the basis of the inference is on the accompaniment of the Necessary Being with intelligible forms in the mind. And since its occurrence in the mind is impossible, accompaniment is impossible. It may also be inferred that He, exalted, is Knower of all things before their occurrence by the fact that the system of the world in this wonderful pattern, along with what it contains of benefits and wisdoms, cannot be without a wise, knowing Creator by rational necessity, just as when you see a beautiful building, you know that its builder is wise and knowing. The gist of the inference is that the Necessary Being can be intelligible, and everything that can be intelligible by itself can be intelligible with the rest of the intelligibles, because there is no impediment in conception. So it results that the Necessary Being can be intelligible with the rest of the intelligibles. Thus the Necessary Being can be accompanied by the rest of the intelligibles in the mind. And whatever is possible for the essence of the Necessary Being in the mind is possible for it absolutely, because general possibility precedes specific possibility. And the possibility of the accompaniment of the intelligibles with the Necessary Being in the mind has been established, so the possibility of their accompaniment absolutely without the mind has been established. So the possibility of accompaniment of the intelligibles with the Necessary Being in the external world has become apparent. And accompaniment of the intelligibles with the Necessary Being in the external world is impossible unless the Necessary Being is a locus for them, due to the impossibility of the Necessary Being's inherence in something. And every attribute possible for the Necessary Being is actually present for it; because of what has passed that the Necessary Being is necessary from all its aspects, having no potential perfection. The second art on knowledge of the crafts and their attributes. Section on that the Necessary Being... the rest of the intelligibles for itself. And everything that is possible for the Necessary Being by general possibility must exist for it, otherwise it would have a potential state—this is absurd. For if it is said: If the Exalted Creator were knower of something, He would be the agent of that form and its receptacle, which is impossible; because the receptacle is that which is prepared for the thing, and the agent is that which does the thing, and the first is not the second, so composition follows. We say: Why is it not permissible that one single thing be prepared for a conceptual thing and also cause it? This is because the meaning of its being prepared for a thing is that it is not impossible in itself to conceive it, and the meaning of its being agent is that it is prior by causality to that conception. So why do you say they are mutually exclusive? And whoever believes that His knowledge of things is His very essence, believes in negation of knowledge in reality. For itself and the result: So the abstract can be accompanied by the rest of the intelligibles for itself, so it can be knower of universals. We then attach this result to our statement: Everything that is possible for the abstract by general possibility must exist in actuality, otherwise it would be potential, leading to its being material while it was abstract—this is absurd. And based on this, the author's analyst should say 'the abstract' instead of 'the Necessary Being', so that this statement becomes a major premise for the previous one and is properly ordered, as is not hidden from the contemplative. The receptacle: the gist of the objection is that if the Creator were knower of universals, their forms would be imprinted in Him, so He would be receptacle and agent for them—because of the impossibility of the Necessary Being needing others for His attributes—and one thing cannot be receptacle and agent except from two aspects, so composition follows in His essence, which contradicts necessity. And the gist of the answer is that receptivity and agency are relative attributes that are accidents to the essence with respect to the form, not intrinsic so that composition would follow. His essence... and this is the path of the verifiers, which they chose to clear away the doubts that occur against the position of forms, namely the necessity of ignorance before their creation, and compulsion and obligation in their emanation.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a demonstrative argument for the Necessary Being's knowledge of universals, followed by an objection and its answer. The author begins with the second art on logic (or sciences). He then presents a section title. The first argument: anything that can be understood by itself can also be understood alongside other concepts. The Necessary Being is something that can be understood (as a concept), so it can be understood together with other intelligibles. This implies that the Necessary Being can be accompanied by intelligibles in the mind. Since possibility in the mind entails absolute possibility (because general possibility precedes specific), the Necessary Being can have intelligibles accompanying it externally, meaning it is a locus for them. This leads to the conclusion that the Necessary Being must actually possess all possible attributes, including knowledge of universals. The author then provides an alternative argument from the order and wisdom in the world, which necessarily implies a wise and knowing creator. He reformulates the argument syllogistically. Then, on page 106, an objection is raised: If God knows something, He would be both the agent (giving existence to the form) and the receptacle (receiving the form). But one thing cannot be both agent and patient simultaneously without composition (tarkib) in the essence, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. The answer: being prepared (qabiliyya) and being agent (fa'iliyya) are relative attributes (idafiyyat) that supervene on the essence in relation to the form; they are not intrinsic parts. Therefore, no composition follows. The author also notes a terminological correction: the earlier argument about possibility should refer to 'the abstract' (al-mujarrad) rather than 'the Necessary Being', so that the reasoning is consistent. The passage ends by noting that this path is chosen by the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) to avoid doubts about forms, such as ignorance before creation and compulsion in emanation.

Proof that God Knows Universals
Demonstration that God knows universals with refutation of composition objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving God's knowledge of universals and explain why the objection about composition fails.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is proven by a syllogism; composition is avoided through relational attributes.

This passage demonstrates that the Necessary Existent (God) knows universals using a categorical syllogism. It then addresses an objection that such knowledge would imply composition in God, which is impossible. By understanding the logical structure and the verifiers' response, you will see how divine simplicity and knowledge are reconciled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main conclusion
The author aims to prove that the Necessary Existent knows universals (al-'ilm bi al-kulliyat). This is the final conclusion of the argument.
2
Extract the two premises
The argument has two premises: (1) The immaterial (al-mujarrad) can accompany all intelligibles essentially, so it can know universals. (2) Everything possible for the immaterial with general possibility must exist actually, otherwise it would be potential. These are combined to yield the conclusion that the immaterial actually knows universals.
The author suggests replacing 'Necessary Existent' with 'immaterial' to clarify the syllogism.
3
Formalize as a categorical syllogism
Let M = immaterial, P = exists actually, S = knows universals. Major premise (Barbara): Everything possible for M is P (i.e., all M-possible things are actual). Minor premise: S is possible for M (i.e., S is M-possible). Conclusion: S is P (the immaterial actually knows universals). This is first figure, form Barbara.
The middle term is 'possible for the immaterial'.
4
Analyze the objection
The objector argues: If God knows a thing, He would be both agent (producing the form) and recipient (receiving the form). A single thing cannot be both agent and recipient of the same form—that would imply composition (tarkib) in God, which is impossible for the Necessary Existent.
The objection assumes that knowledge requires the form to be imprinted in the knower.
5
Examine the response
The author replies that a single thing can be both prepared for a conceptual form and bring it about. 'Preparedness' means no intrinsic impossibility to conceive it, and 'agency' means causal priority. These are relational attributes (idafiyyat) that accrue to the essence without entering into it, so no composition results. Thus the objection is refuted.
This is the path of the verifiers (muhassiqun), who avoid doubts about ignorance before creation or compulsion.

Source Text

فصل في أن الواجب لذاته عالم بالكليات. سائر المعقولات لذاته، وكل ما يمكن لواجب الوجود بالإمكان العام يجب وجوده، له وإلا لكان له حالة منتظرة، هذا خلف، فإن قيل: لو كان الباري تعالى عالما بشيء لكان فاعلا لتلك الصورة وقابلا لها، وهو محال؛ لأن القابل هو الذي يستعد للشيء، والفاعل هو الذي يفعل الشيء، والأول غير الثاني فيلزم التركيب. قلنا: لم لا يجوز أن يكون الشيء الواحد مستعدا للشيء التصوري ومفيدا له، وهذا لأن معنى كونه مستعدا للشيء أنه لا يمتنع لذاته أن يتصوره، ومعنى كونه فاعلا أنه مقدم بالعلية على ذلك التصور فلم قلتم : إنهما متنافيان؟ ومن اعتقد أن علمه تعالى بالأشياء نفس ذاته اعتقد نفي العلم بالحقيقة. لذاته والنتيجة: فيمكن أن يقارن المجرد سائر المعقولات لذاته، فيمكن أن يكون عالما بالكليات فنضم هذه النتيجة إلى قولنا وكل ما يمكن للمجرد بالإمكان العام يجب وجوده بالفعل، وإلا لكان بالقوة فيلزم كونه ماديا وقد كان مجردا، هذا خلف، وعلى هذا كان على المصنف محلله أن يقول مكان الواجب الوجود: المجرد، حتى يكون هذا الكلام كبرى للسابق وينتظم كما لا يخفى على المتأمل. القابل حاصل الاعتراض: أن الباري لو كان عالما بالكليات لكان صورها مرتسمة فيه، فيكون قابلا لها وفاعلا لها؛ لامتناع احتياج الواجب في صفاته إلى الغير، والشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا إلا من جهتين فيلزم التركيب في ذاته، وهو مناف للوجوب، وحاصل الجواب: أن القابلية والفاعلية إضافيتان عارضتان للذات بالنسبة إلى الصورة ليستا داخلتين حتى يلزم التركيب. ذاته وهو مسلك المحققين اختاروه للتصفي عن الشبهات التي ترد على القول بالصورة، وهو لزوم الجهل قبل إيجادها، والاضطرار والإيجاب في صدورها.
Section on that the Necessary Existent knows universals. All intelligibles are essential to Him, and everything possible for the Necessary Existence with general possibility must exist for Him, otherwise He would have a pending state, this is a contradiction. If it is said: If the Creator were knowledgeable of a thing, He would be an agent of that form and a recipient of it, which is impossible; because the recipient is that which is prepared for the thing, and the agent is that which does the thing, and the first is not the second, thus composition follows. We say: Why is it not permissible that a single thing be prepared for the conceptual thing and also bring it about? This is because the meaning of its being prepared for the thing is that it is not impossible in itself to conceive it, and the meaning of its being an agent is that it is prior by causation to that conception; so why do you say they are contradictory? And whoever believes that His knowledge of things is identical to His essence believes in the negation of knowledge in reality. The result: So the immaterial can accompany all intelligibles essentially, so it can know universals. We then combine this result with our statement: Everything possible for the immaterial with general possibility must exist actually, otherwise it would be potential, necessitating its being material while it was immaterial, this is a contradiction. And therefore, the author's analyst should say 'the immaterial' instead of 'the Necessary Existence', so that this statement becomes the major premise of the previous [syllogism] and it becomes coherent, as is not hidden from the contemplator. The objection summarized: If the Creator knew universals, their forms would be imprinted in Him, so He would be both recipient and agent for them, due to the impossibility of the Necessary needing another in His attributes, and a single thing cannot be recipient and agent except from two aspects, thus composition follows in His essence, which contradicts necessity. The answer summarized: Receptivity and agency are relational attributes accidental to the essence with respect to the form, not inherent, so composition does not follow. And [this] is the path of the verifiers, which they adopted to clear away doubts that arise from the assertion of form, namely the necessity of ignorance before its creation and compulsion and determinism in its emanation.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a philosophic-theological demonstration that the Necessary Existent (God) knows universals, structured as a proof with an objection and response. The author begins by stating that all intelligibles are essential to the Necessary Existent. Then he presents a syllogism: everything possible for the Necessary Existent with general possibility must exist actually, otherwise there would be potentiality, which is incompatible with necessity. An objection is raised: if God knows a thing, He would be both agent (producing the form) and recipient (receiving the form), but a single thing cannot be both; this would imply composition (tarkib) in God's essence, which is impossible. The author replies that a single thing can be both prepared for a conceptual form and bring it about, because 'preparedness' means no intrinsic impossibility to conceive it, and 'agency' means causal priority in relation to that conception. These are not contradictory; they are relational attributes (idafiyyat) that accrue to the essence without entering into it, thus no composition results. The author also discusses an alternative approach: the Necessary Existent could be considered as 'the immaterial' (al-mujarrad) to avoid the objection. The verifiers adopt this path to refute doubts about knowledge requiring forms, such as the claim that ignorance precedes knowledge or that knowledge necessitates compulsion. Throughout, the argument uses categorical reasoning (qiyas iqtirani) with premises about possibility and actuality, and the objection-response style reflects dialectical (jadal) influence. An example from Kalam: if God knows that Zayd will exist, this knowledge is eternal and does not change—He knows it without being affected by time.

Knowledge of Changing Particulars Through Causes
Author's argument for God's knowledge of changing particulars through causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving that God knows changing particulars from His perfect knowledge of their causes.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect, so an unchanging cause knows changing effects universally.

In this passage, the author argues that the Necessary Existent (God) knows changing particulars because He knows their causes perfectly. Since the cause is known, its effects are necessarily known. But how can knowledge of particulars be reconciled with God's unchanging essence? The key is that He knows them 'in a universal manner' (ala wajh kulli), not as temporally changing. This lesson walks you through the logical structure of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Find the main claim the author is trying to prove. It is: 'The Necessary Existent knows changing particulars in a universal manner.' This is the conclusion of the syllogism.
2
Extract the major premise
The author states: 'Whoever knows the cause with perfect knowledge must know what follows from it essentially.' This is the major premise linking cause to effect.
This is a categorical statement: All who know the cause perfectly know its effects.
3
Extract the minor premise
The minor premise is: 'He (God) knows their causes with perfect knowledge.' This is affirmed from prior proofs that God knows universals and causes.
The causes are four: matter, form, end, and natural powers.
4
Formalize the syllogism
Using the major and minor premises, the conclusion follows: Therefore, God knows the changing particulars (the effects). This is a first-figure categorical syllogism (Barbara).
5
Understand the universal manner
The author adds that God knows them 'in a universal manner' (ala wajh kulli), meaning through a universal concept that applies to particulars but is not subject to temporal change. This avoids attributing change to God's knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته عالم بالجزئيات المتغيرة على وجه كلي. لأنه يعلم أسبابها علما تاما، فوجب أن يكون عالما بها؛ لأن من يعلم العلة علما تاما، وجب أن يعلم ما يلزم عنها لذاتها، وإلا لما كان عالما بها، لكن لا يدركها مع تغيرها، وإلا لكان يدرك منها تارة أنها موجودة. وجه كلي أي بواسطة مفهوم كلي منطبق على الجزئيات المتغيرة، متخصص بقيودات منحصر في الخارج في فرد واحد وإن كان في نفسه صالحا لكثرة، وليست الجزئيات المتغيرة من حيث إنها متغيرة صورها مرتسمة في ذات الواجب لذاته. أسبابها : أي أسباب الجزئيات المتغيرة، وأسبابها الكلية أمور أربعة: المادة والصورة والغاية والقوى الطبعية وهي كلها معلومة له تعالى كما ثبت من قبل أنه تعالى عالم بالكليات صادرة عنه تعالى بالإرادة فيعلم ما يلزم من تلك الأسباب من الجزئيات المتغيرة؛ لأن العلم بالعلة من حيث إنها علة يستلزم العلم بالمعلول. لكن هذا استدراك على الكلام السابق، أي الواجب لذاته يعلم الجزئيات المتغيرة، ولكن لا يعلمها من حيث إنها متغيرة؛ للزوم التغير في العلم. وبين الملازمة بقوله: " وإلا لكان يدرك" إلخ. لا يدركها قيل عليه إنه لا ريب أن الجزئيات المتغيرة معلولة له تعالى وإن كانت معلولة له بواسطة وقد قالوا إن العلم بالعلة علما تاما يوجب العلم بالمعلول، فأول الكلام يثبت العلم بالجزئيات المتغيرة من حيث إنها متغيرة وآخره ينفيه، فهذا تناقض، وعندي أول الكلام حق وآخره باطل، وما قالوا من لزوم التغير في الذات فباطل؛ لأن التغير التجدد في حدوث الجزئيات وأما علمه تعالى فمتعلق بالوجود والعدم المحدودين بحد الموقتين بوقت في الأزل على نحو واحد من غير تجدد وتعاقب، يعني يعلم سبحانه تعالى في الأزل أن زيدا يوجد في وقت كذا، ويموت في وقت كذا.
Section on that the Necessary Existent knows changing particulars in a universal manner. Because He knows their causes with perfect knowledge, so it is necessary that He knows them; for whoever knows the cause with perfect knowledge must know what follows from it essentially, otherwise he would not know it. But He does not perceive them with their change, otherwise He would perceive them sometimes as existent. A universal manner means through a universal concept applying to changing particulars, specialized by restrictions, confined externally to a single individual although in itself capable of multiplicity. And the forms of changing particulars insofar as they are changing are not imprinted in the essence of the Necessary Existent. Their causes: that is, the causes of changing particulars, and their universal causes are four: matter, form, end, and natural powers. All these are known to Him, as was previously established that He knows universals, issuing from Him by will, so He knows what follows from those causes of changing particulars; because knowledge of the cause insofar as it is a cause necessitates knowledge of the effect. But this is a qualification of the previous statement, i.e., the Necessary Existent knows changing particulars, but does not know them insofar as they are changing, due to the necessitation of change in knowledge. And he explains the implication by his saying: 'otherwise He would perceive' etc. He does not perceive them. It was objected to him: There is no doubt that changing particulars are caused by Him, even if through intermediaries, and they said that knowledge of the cause with perfect knowledge necessitates knowledge of the effect. So the first part of the statement affirms knowledge of changing particulars insofar as they are changing, and the latter denies it; this is a contradiction. In my view, the first part is true and the latter is false, and what they said about the necessitation of change in the essence is false; because change is renewal in the occurrence of particulars, while His knowledge is related to existence and non-existence delimited by the boundary of the two times at a moment in eternity in a single manner without renewal and succession. That is, He knows—glorified be He—in eternity that Zayd will exist at a certain time and die at a certain time.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical explanation of how the Necessary Existent (God) knows changing particulars (al-juz'iyyat al-mutaghayyira) without change in His essence. The author begins by stating that God knows the causes of these particulars with perfect knowledge, and since knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect, He must know the particulars. However, He does not perceive them as changing (i.e., with temporal succession), otherwise His knowledge would change. The phrase 'in a universal manner' (ala wajh kulli) is explained: it means through a universal concept that applies to particulars, confined externally to one individual but conceptually universal. The forms of changing particulars as changing are not imprinted in God. The four causes (matter, form, end, natural powers) are known to Him, and He knows what follows from them by His will. An objection is raised: the first part of the discussion affirms knowledge of changing particulars as changing, but the latter denies it, leading to contradiction. The author resolves this by stating that the first part is true—God knows particulars—but the denial is about knowing them as changing, which is false; change involves renewal in the particulars themselves, whereas God's knowledge is eternal, relating to existence and non-existence at specific times without succession. For example, God eternally knows that Zayd will exist at a certain time and die at a certain time, without His knowledge changing. The argument uses categorical reasoning about causality and knowledge, and the objection-answer structure reflects dialectical method. An Islamic example: God knows when the Day of Judgment will occur, but this knowledge does not change as time passes.

Resolving the Contradiction: God's Knowledge Without Change
Objection and resolution regarding God's knowledge of changing particulars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the apparent contradiction in the previous argument and explain the author's resolution using the distinction between knowledge of change and changing knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is eternal and relates to particulars through universals, so no temporal change enters His essence.

The initial argument claims that God knows changing particulars because He knows their causes. But an objection arises: if God knows particulars as changing, His knowledge would change with them. The author responds by clarifying that God does not know them 'as changing' but 'in a universal manner'—His knowledge is eternal and relates to existence and non-existence at specific times without renewal. This lesson explores the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector points out a seeming contradiction: the first part says God knows changing particulars (since He knows the causes), but the latter part denies that He perceives them as changing. How can He know them without knowing them as changing?
2
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objector reasons: If knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, and the effect is a changing particular, then God must know it as changing. But the author says He does not perceive it as changing. Hence, contradiction.
3
Analyze the author's resolution
The author accepts the first part (God knows particulars) but rejects the second part (that He knows them as changing). He argues that 'change' (tajaddud) is a property of the particulars, not of His knowledge. His knowledge is eternal and relates to existence and non-existence at specific times in a single manner.
4
Grasp the universal manner defense
The author uses the example of Zayd: God eternally knows that Zayd will exist at time t1 and die at time t2. This knowledge does not change; it is a single eternal truth about Zayd's temporal status.
5
Evaluate the critique
The author's critique of the objector is that they wrongly assume that knowing a changing thing requires change in the knower. He shows that God's knowledge is not a passive impression but an eternal relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته عالم بالجزئيات المتغيرة على وجه كلي. لأنه يعلم أسبابها علما تاما، فوجب أن يكون عالما بها؛ لأن من يعلم العلة علما تاما، وجب أن يعلم ما يلزم عنها لذاتها، وإلا لما كان عالما بها، لكن لا يدركها مع تغيرها، وإلا لكان يدرك منها تارة أنها موجودة. وجه كلي أي بواسطة مفهوم كلي منطبق على الجزئيات المتغيرة، متخصص بقيودات منحصر في الخارج في فرد واحد وإن كان في نفسه صالحا لكثرة، وليست الجزئيات المتغيرة من حيث إنها متغيرة صورها مرتسمة في ذات الواجب لذاته. أسبابها : أي أسباب الجزئيات المتغيرة، وأسبابها الكلية أمور أربعة: المادة والصورة والغاية والقوى الطبعية وهي كلها معلومة له تعالى كما ثبت من قبل أنه تعالى عالم بالكليات صادرة عنه تعالى بالإرادة فيعلم ما يلزم من تلك الأسباب من الجزئيات المتغيرة؛ لأن العلم بالعلة من حيث إنها علة يستلزم العلم بالمعلول. لكن هذا استدراك على الكلام السابق، أي الواجب لذاته يعلم الجزئيات المتغيرة، ولكن لا يعلمها من حيث إنها متغيرة؛ للزوم التغير في العلم. وبين الملازمة بقوله: " وإلا لكان يدرك" إلخ. لا يدركها قيل عليه إنه لا ريب أن الجزئيات المتغيرة معلولة له تعالى وإن كانت معلولة له بواسطة وقد قالوا إن العلم بالعلة علما تاما يوجب العلم بالمعلول، فأول الكلام يثبت العلم بالجزئيات المتغيرة من حيث إنها متغيرة وآخره ينفيه، فهذا تناقض، وعندي أول الكلام حق وآخره باطل، وما قالوا من لزوم التغير في الذات فباطل؛ لأن التغير التجدد في حدوث الجزئيات وأما علمه تعالى فمتعلق بالوجود والعدم المحدودين بحد الموقتين بوقت في الأزل على نحو واحد من غير تجدد وتعاقب، يعني يعلم سبحانه تعالى في الأزل أن زيدا يوجد في وقت كذا، ويموت في وقت كذا.
Section on that the Necessary Existent knows changing particulars in a universal manner. Because He knows their causes with perfect knowledge, so it is necessary that He knows them; for whoever knows the cause with perfect knowledge must know what follows from it essentially, otherwise he would not know it. But He does not perceive them with their change, otherwise He would perceive them sometimes as existent. A universal manner means through a universal concept applying to changing particulars, specialized by restrictions, confined externally to a single individual although in itself capable of multiplicity. And the forms of changing particulars insofar as they are changing are not imprinted in the essence of the Necessary Existent. Their causes: that is, the causes of changing particulars, and their universal causes are four: matter, form, end, and natural powers. All these are known to Him, as was previously established that He knows universals, issuing from Him by will, so He knows what follows from those causes of changing particulars; because knowledge of the cause insofar as it is a cause necessitates knowledge of the effect. But this is a qualification of the previous statement, i.e., the Necessary Existent knows changing particulars, but does not know them insofar as they are changing, due to the necessitation of change in knowledge. And he explains the implication by his saying: 'otherwise He would perceive' etc. He does not perceive them. It was objected to him: There is no doubt that changing particulars are caused by Him, even if through intermediaries, and they said that knowledge of the cause with perfect knowledge necessitates knowledge of the effect. So the first part of the statement affirms knowledge of changing particulars insofar as they are changing, and the latter denies it; this is a contradiction. In my view, the first part is true and the latter is false, and what they said about the necessitation of change in the essence is false; because change is renewal in the occurrence of particulars, while His knowledge is related to existence and non-existence delimited by the boundary of the two times at a moment in eternity in a single manner without renewal and succession. That is, He knows—glorified be He—in eternity that Zayd will exist at a certain time and die at a certain time.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical explanation of how the Necessary Existent (God) knows changing particulars (al-juz'iyyat al-mutaghayyira) without change in His essence. The author begins by stating that God knows the causes of these particulars with perfect knowledge, and since knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect, He must know the particulars. However, He does not perceive them as changing (i.e., with temporal succession), otherwise His knowledge would change. The phrase 'in a universal manner' (ala wajh kulli) is explained: it means through a universal concept that applies to particulars, confined externally to one individual but conceptually universal. The forms of changing particulars as changing are not imprinted in God. The four causes (matter, form, end, natural powers) are known to Him, and He knows what follows from them by His will. An objection is raised: the first part of the discussion affirms knowledge of changing particulars as changing, but the latter denies it, leading to contradiction. The author resolves this by stating that the first part is true—God knows particulars—but the denial is about knowing them as changing, which is false; change involves renewal in the particulars themselves, whereas God's knowledge is eternal, relating to existence and non-existence at specific times without succession. For example, God eternally knows that Zayd will exist at a certain time and die at a certain time, without His knowledge changing. The argument uses categorical reasoning about causality and knowledge, and the objection-answer structure reflects dialectical method. An Islamic example: God knows when the Day of Judgment will occur, but this knowledge does not change as time passes.

D) He says particulars are not real.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of changing particulars does not entail change in His essence, because knowledge is eternal and relates to temporal events through a universal manner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the objection: 'If God knows that I will commit a sin tomorrow, then He must know it as a future sin, and His knowledge changes when the sin occurs.' Using the author's resolution, explain why this objection fails.
?
Practice formulating a response: Use the example of the Day of Judgment to show that God's eternal knowledge of it does not imply change in His essence.

Refuting Change in God’s Essence from Knowledge of Particulars
Author refutes the charge that God's knowledge of changing particulars implies change in His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's refutation of the objection that God's knowledge of changing particulars implies change in His essence.

Core Idea
Change is in the objects, not in God's eternal knowledge.

A powerful objection against divine omniscience claims that if God knows changing particulars, His knowledge must change, implying change in His essence. The author dismantles this by distinguishing between the object's change and the subject's knowledge. Let us walk through the refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate the objection in the passage: it says that the first part (knowing particulars from their causes) affirms knowledge of changing particulars, but the latter part denies it, leading to contradiction. Also, the objectors claim knowledge of change necessitates change in the knower.
The objection is presented as 'قيل عليه'.
2
Extract the author's verdict
The author states: 'أول الكلام حق وآخره باطل' — the first part (knowing particulars through causes) is true, the latter part (denial or implication of change) is false. He directly rejects the inference of change in God's essence.
3
Analyze the distinction between object-change and knowledge-change
The author explains that change (التغير) is in the particulars' coming-to-be (حدوث الجزئيات), not in God's knowledge. God's knowledge is related to existence and non-existence 'في الأزل على نحو واحد' — in a single mode without renewal.
Example: God knows in eternity that Zayd exists at T and dies at T'. The renewal is in Zayd, not in the knowledge.
4
Understand the counterexample
The author gives the concrete example of Zayd: God knows eternally that Zayd will exist at a specific time and die at a specific time. Thus, any renewal or succession pertains only to the particular, not to the divine knowledge.
This shows how the same eternal knowledge relates to changing objects without itself changing.
5
Conclude the refutation
The author concludes that the charge of change in God's essence is false. Change is limited to the created particulars. God's knowledge remains immutable.
The final declaration: 'تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل عليه إنه لا ريب أن الجزئيات المتغيرة معلولة له تعالى وإن كانت معلولة له بواسطة وقد قالوا إن العلم بالعلة علما تاما يوجب العلم بالمعلول، فأول الكلام يثبت العلم بالجزئيات المتغيرة من حيث إنها متغيرة وآخره ينفيه، فهذا تناقض، وعندي أول الكلام حق وآخره باطل، وما قالوا من لزوم التغير في الذات فباطل؛ لأن التغير التجدد في حدوث الجزئيات وأما علمه تعالى فمتعلق بالوجود والعدم المحدودين بحد الموقتين بوقت في الأزل على نحو واحد من غير تجدد وتعاقب، يعني يعلم سبحانه تعالى في الأزل أن زيدا يوجد في وقت كذا، ويموت في وقت كذا، فالتجدد والتعاقب يرجع إلى حدوث الجزئيات دون علمه كما زعموا، تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا. لم يعلم إلخ : قال صاحب 'المحاكمات المراد بقولهم إنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات على وجه كلي أنه لا يعلمها من حيث أن بعضها واقع في الآن وبعضها في الماضي وبعضها في المستقبل، بل يعلمها علما تاما متعاليا عن الدخول تحت الأزمنة ثابتا أبد الدهر، وهذا كما أنه تعالى لما لم يكن مكانيا كان نسبته إلى جميع الأمكنة على السواء، فليس بالقياس إليه بعضها قريبا وبعضها بعيدا وبعضها متوسطا، كذلك لما لم يكن زمانيا كان نسبته إلى جميع الأزمنة على السواء، فليس بالقياس إليه بعضها ماضيا وبعضها حاضرا وبعضها مستقبلا، وكذا الأمور الواقعة في الزمان، فالموجودات من الأزل إلى الأبد معلومة له كل في وقته وليس في علمه كان وكائن وسيكون دائما حاضرة عنده تعالى في أوقاتها بلا تغير أصلا، وليس مرادهم ما توهمه البعض من أن علمه تعالى محيط بطبائع الجزئيات دون خصوصياتها وأحوالها.
It was objected: 'There is no doubt that the changing particulars are effects of Him, Exalted, even if they are effects through an intermediary. And they have said that knowledge of the cause, complete knowledge, necessitates knowledge of the effect. So the first part of the discourse affirms knowledge of the changing particulars insofar as they are changing, and the latter part denies it. This is a contradiction.' I say: The first part is true and the latter part is false. And what they said about the necessity of change in the Essence is false, because change is renewal in the coming-to-be of the particulars, whereas His knowledge, Exalted, is related to existence and non-existence delimited by the limit of two times in eternity in a single mode, without renewal and succession. That is, He, Glorious and Exalted, knows in eternity that Zayd will exist at such a time and die at such a time. So renewal and succession refer to the coming-to-be of the particulars, not to His knowledge, as they claimed. Exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. 'He does not know... etc.' The author of 'al-Muhakamat' said: The meaning of their statement that He, Exalted, knows particulars in a universal manner is that He does not know them insofar as some occur now, some in the past, and some in the future. Rather, He knows them with a complete knowledge that is transcendent over entering into times, permanent forever. This is just as He, Exalted, since He is not spatial, His relation to all places is equal; so in relation to Him, some are not near, some far, some intermediate. Similarly, since He is not temporal, His relation to all times is equal; so in relation to Him, some are not past, some present, some future. And likewise for things occurring in time. So existents from eternity to eternity are known to Him, each in its time, and in His knowledge there is no 'was', 'is', or 'will be'; they are always present to Him, Exalted, at their times without any change whatsoever. And what some misunderstood—that His knowledge encompasses only the natures of particulars without their specificities and states—is not their intended meaning.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a critical response to an objection raised against the doctrine that the Necessary Being (God) knows changing particulars in a universal manner. The objection charges that the initial argument (that God knows particulars because He knows their causes) leads to a contradiction: if He knows them as changing, His knowledge would change, implying change in His essence—which is impossible. The author addresses this by separating two aspects: the truth of knowing particulars from their causes, and the false inference of change in God. He explains that change is in the objects (their coming-to-be and passing away), not in God's knowledge. God's knowledge is eternal and immutable: He knows in eternity that Zayd exists at time T and dies at time T' without any renewal or succession in His knowledge. The author then cites al-Muhakamat to clarify the meaning of 'knowing particulars universally': it does not mean knowing only their generic natures, but rather knowing each particular in its specific identity and time, but in a manner that transcends temporal divisions. Just as God, being non-spatial, relates equally to all places, so He, being non-temporal, relates equally to all times. Hence, all events are ever-present to His knowledge without change. This resolves the apparent contradiction and defends God's omniscience without attributing change to His essence.

D) Because God's knowledge is passive and unaffected.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the charge of change in God's essence by distinguishing between change in the object of knowledge (particulars) and the immutable nature of divine knowledge.

Clarifying 'Universal Knowledge' of Particulars
Author clarifies the meaning of 'universal knowledge' of particulars via al-Muhakamat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the correct interpretation of 'God knows particulars in a universal manner' as taught by al-Muhakamat, and distinguish it from the misunderstanding that God knows only generic natures.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is universal not by abstraction but by transcendence of time.

A common misunderstanding of the philosophers' doctrine is that 'universal knowledge' means God knows only the general natures of things, not their specific identities. The author, citing al-Muhakamat, clarifies that it actually means God knows each particular in its specific state but in a manner that transcends temporal divisions. Let us unpack this key clarification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misunderstanding
The passage says: 'ليس مرادهم ما توهمه البعض من أن علمه تعالى محيط بطبائع الجزئيات دون خصوصياتها وأحوالها.' The misunderstanding is that God's universal knowledge covers only the natures (طبائع) of particulars, not their specificities (خصوصيات) and states (أحوال).
2
Understand the correct meaning via al-Muhakamat
The author quotes al-Muhakamat: the meaning is that God does not know particulars insofar as some are present, past, or future. Rather, He knows them with a knowledge that is 'متعاليا عن الدخول تحت الأزمنة' — transcendent over entering into times.
The key term is 'على وجه كلي' — in a universal manner, i.e., not bound by time.
3
Grasp the analogy of space and time
Just as God, being non-spatial (غير مكاني), relates equally to all places, so He, being non-temporal (غير زماني), relates equally to all times. His knowledge does not have 'past', 'present', or 'future' distinctions.
This analogy helps visualize how knowledge can be of particulars without temporal limitation.
4
Apply to the knowledge of existents
All existents from eternity to eternity are known to God 'كل في وقته' — each in its own time, yet in His knowledge there is no 'was', 'is', or 'will be'; they are always present to Him at their times without change.
This means God knows Zayd's creation, life, and death as they happen, but His knowledge is not segmented by time.
5
Distinguish the correct from the incorrect interpretation
The correct interpretation: 'universal' means not temporally conditioned. The incorrect: 'universal' means only general natures. The passage explicitly rejects the latter.
Thus, God knows each particular in its full specificity, but in a timeless, eternal act.

This passage is a critical response to an objection raised against the doctrine that the Necessary Being (God) knows changing particulars in a universal manner. The objection charges that the initial argument (that God knows particulars because He knows their causes) leads to a contradiction: if He knows them as changing, His knowledge would change, implying change in His essence—which is impossible. The author addresses this by separating two aspects: the truth of knowing particulars from their causes, and the false inference of change in God. He explains that change is in the objects (their coming-to-be and passing away), not in God's knowledge. God's knowledge is eternal and immutable: He knows in eternity that Zayd exists at time T and dies at time T' without any renewal or succession in His knowledge. The author then cites al-Muhakamat to clarify the meaning of 'knowing particulars universally': it does not mean knowing only their generic natures, but rather knowing each particular in its specific identity and time, but in a manner that transcends temporal divisions. Just as God, being non-spatial, relates equally to all places, so He, being non-temporal, relates equally to all times. Hence, all events are ever-present to His knowledge without change. This resolves the apparent contradiction and defends God's omniscience without attributing change to His essence.

Key Terms
العلم الكلي
الجزئيات
الأزمنة

C) He knows them as they are present, past, and future separately.
D) He knows them through intermediaries.

Key Takeaway

Disjunctive Proof: The First Emanation Is the Intellect
Proof that the first emanation is the intellect via disjunctive argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism proving that the first emanation from God must be an intellect, and verify its validity.

Core Idea
The first emanation is the intellect; every other candidate is eliminated by impossibility.

This passage presents a classic disjunctive argument in Islamic philosophy: the first emanation from the One must be one of five categories, and four are eliminated, leaving intellect as the only possibility. We will walk through the logical structure to see how the author eliminates each alternative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exhaustive set of candidates
The author lists five possibilities for the first emanation: matter, form, accident, soul, or intellect. Recognize that this is meant to be an exhaustive division of simple substances that could emanate directly from the First Principle.
The list is based on the categories of substance in Aristotelian philosophy.
2
Note the principle of emanation
The First Principle is absolutely simple, so only one thing can emanate directly. This principle is stated: 'the simple does not emanate from Him except one.' This frames the argument: we need to identify which one.
This is the principle 'from one, only one proceeds' (ex uno non fit nisi unum).
3
Eliminate each impossible candidate with its reason
The author gives reasons why matter, form, accident, and soul cannot be the first emanation. Matter cannot exist without form; form cannot be causally prior to matter; accident cannot exist before substance; soul would entail a problem (likely composition or relation to body). List each reason alongside the candidate.
The text cuts off for soul, but the logic implies soul is also composite or dependent.
4
Affirm the remaining candidate
After eliminating four, the only remaining candidate is intellect. By the rule of disjunctive syllogism, if the disjunction is exhaustive and all but one are false, the remaining must be true.
The argument is valid if the disjunction is truly exhaustive and the eliminations are sound.
5
Check validity: are there hidden assumptions?
Examine whether the list is truly exhaustive. Could there be another kind of simple substance? Also check if the reasons for elimination are philosophically sound according to the author's own principles.
This step strengthens critical engagement with the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث في الملائكة وهي العقول المجردة، وهو يشتمل على أربعة فصول.
فصل في إثبات العقل

The third art on the angels, which are the immaterial intellects, and it contains four chapters. Chapter on establishing the intellect. Its proof is that the emanation from the First Principle is only one, because He is simple, and the simple does not emanate from Him except one, as has passed. That one is either matter, or form, or accident, or soul, or intellect. It cannot be matter because matter does not subsist in act without form. It cannot be form because form does not precede matter in causality, as has passed. It cannot be accident because its existence before the existence of substance is impossible. Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic proof establishing that the first emanation (al-sadir al-awwal) from God must be an intellect ('aql). The argument: The First Principle is simple (basit), so only one thing can emanate from Him. That one thing must be one of the five categories: matter (hayula), form (sura), accident ('arad), soul (nafs), or intellect ('aql). Then the author eliminates the first four by showing they are impossible as first emanations: matter cannot exist without form, form cannot be causally prior to matter, accident cannot exist before substance, and soul (if it were the first) would entail a problem (the text cuts off, but the implication is that soul is also composite or related to body). Therefore, the first emanation must be intellect. This is a classic disjunctive syllogism in Islamic philosophy, drawn from the Neoplatonic emanation scheme.

Critiquing the First Emanation as Scientific Forms
A commentator questions the author's proof, arguing that the first emanation is not one of the five enumerated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the commentator's objection that the first emanation is the scientific forms and evaluate its logical force against the author's proof.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge is the source, the first emanation is the forms in His knowledge, not a separate intellect.

In this critical hashya, the commentator zeros in on a tension in the author's proof: the claim that the first emanation is one of five substances (e.g., intellect, soul) contradicts the author's own commitment that God's knowledge is the origin of all things. By unpacking the premises, you will see how a single inconsistency can challenge an entire emanation scheme.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim under fire
The author asserts that the first emanation is one of five enumerated things (likely intellect, soul, etc.). Find the reference in the Arabic: 'لا من الأشياء الخمسة التي ذكرها' marks the target.
2
Extract the commentator's major premise
The commentator states that 'the knowledge of the Necessary Existent encompasses things, and He is the agent and recipient of them.' This means God's knowledge is both the cause and the template of creation.
This premise is drawn from Ibn Sina and al-Farabi ('the two shaykhs').
3
Derive the logical conclusion
If God's knowledge is the source, then the first emanation must be the scientific forms (al-suwar al-'ilmiyya) — the archetypes in God's mind — not any of the five substances. The commentator says: 'فيكون الصادر الأول الصور العلمية'.
The conclusion directly contradicts the author's enumeration.
4
Assess the critique's validity
Does the critique succeed? It depends on whether God's knowledge is truly productive in the sense of being the very content of creation. If so, the author's list is incomplete. If not, the commentator may have misapplied the premise.
Think about the difference between God's knowledge as cause versus as exemplar.
5
Observe the grammatical correction
Correcting 'وإلا لكان فاعلا' to 'لكانت فاعلة' because the soul (النفس) is feminine. This shows how precise grammar guards against misattribution of agency.
Grammar here is not pedantic; it affects whether the soul is seen as an agent.

Commentary on the Multiplicity Proof: From One Only One Refined
Commentator refines the proof by showing the intellect's non-simplicity and reinforcing nobility hierarchy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the commentator's critique of the 'from one only one' principle and evaluate the hierarchical argument in the context of emanation of spheres.

Core Idea
Intellect is not simple, so the rule 'from one only one' fails to explain the nine spheres.

In this commentary on the proof of multiplicity, the scholar challenges a key premise of the emanation scheme: that from a simple cause only one effect proceeds. He argues that intellects, though unified, have an internal multiplicity (e.g., essence and intellection) that allows them to produce two effects. Moreover, the hierarchy of spheres demands that the higher, nobler sphere be the cause, not the lower. Let's unpack his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the spheres
The commentator lists the nine spheres in the philosophers' model: the outermost sphere, the sphere of the zodiac, and seven planetary spheres. Notice that this enumeration sets the stage for the critique: many spheres need many causes.
2
Spot the contested premise
The premise 'from one only one' (al-wahid la yasduru illa al-wahid) is central to the proof. The commentator declares it is of no use here. Why? Because the intellect is not simple in every respect; it has multiplicity, so it can produce more than one effect.
The commentator admits the intellect may have only two aspects, but that still cannot explain nine spheres.
3
Analyze the hierarchical argument
The commentator then argues that the lower sphere (baser, closer to elements of generation and corruption) cannot be the cause of the higher, nobler sphere. Causality requires the cause to be nobler than the effect. This supports the need for a chain of increasingly simple and noble intellects.
This is a refinement: even if the intellect had two aspects, it cannot directly cause all nine spheres; intermediate causes are required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage is a critical commentary on the proof of multiplicity. The Conditional Proof for Multiplicity of Intellects
Author proves multiplicity of intellects by impossibility of void

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional argument that proves the multiplicity of intellects by showing that the assumption of a single cause leads to the impossible conclusion that void is possible.

Core Idea
The impossibility of void forces the multiplicity of intellects: one intellect cannot be the complete cause of another.

In this passage, the author of Hidayat al-Hikma presents a burhān (demonstration) using conditional reasoning. The argument hinges on the causal relationship between two intellects and the impossibility of void (al-khalā'). By the end of this lesson, you will learn to trace this logical chain and see why the conclusion—that multiple intellects are necessary—is unavoidable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms and the causal assumption
The passage uses 'container' (al-ḥāwī) and 'contained' (al-maḥwī) to denote the first and second intellects. The initial assumption is that the container is the complete cause of the contained.
These are technical terms in Islamic cosmology.
2
Set up the conditional premise
If the container is the cause, then the existence of the contained must be posterior to the container's existence. Therefore, the non-existence of the contained alongside the existence of the container would be possible (since the effect is contingent).
3
Derive the impossible consequence
If that non-existence is possible, then void (al-khalā') would be possible—because void is defined as a place where the container exists but the contained does not. But void is impossible in itself (according to Aristotelian physics).
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Conclude the refutation
Since the consequence is impossible, the antecedent (that the container is the complete cause) must be false. Therefore, the container and contained coexist as two effects of a higher cause (the first intellect), establishing multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث في الملائكة فصل في إثبات كثرة العقول وجود الحاوي؛ لأن وجوب وجود المعلول مؤخر عن وجوب وجود العلة، وإذا كان كذلك فعدم المحوي مع وجود الحاوي لا يكون ممتنعا لذاته وإلا لكان وجوده معه لا متأخرا عنه، وقد فرضناه متأخرا، هذا خلف، وإذا كان عدم المحوي مع وجود الحاوي ممكنا كان وجود الخلاء ممكنا لذاته، هذا خلف، فظهر أن المؤثر في الأفلاك عقول متكثرة. هداية (۱) الحاوي وسبب المحوي وهو العقل الثاني معا السبب متقدم على المحوي، ولكن الحاوي ليس بمتقدم؛ لأن السبب مع أن متقدم بالعلية، وما مع المتقدم بالعلية لا يجب أن يكون متقدما بالعلية. هداية :(۲): الحاوي والمحوي كل واحد منهما ممكن لذاته، ولكن ذلك كذلك: حاصله : أنه إذا كان المحوي معلولا للحاوي، كان وجود المحوي متأخرا عن وجود الحاوي؛ ضرورة تأخر المعلول عن علته، وإذا لم يكن وجود المحوي في مرتبة وجود الحاوي كان عدمه فيها؛ لامتناع ارتفاع النقيضين. وجود الخلاء: لأن عدم المحوي مع وجود الحاوي ووجود الخلاء متلازمان، والخلاء ممتنع لذاته لا يتصور إمكانه أصلا في وقت وامتناع أحد المتلازمين يوجب امتناع الآخر، وإلا يبطل الملازمة، فعدم المحوي مع وجود الحاوي أيضا ممتنع لا يكون ممكنا معه، وقد كان عدم المحوي ممكنا مع وجود الحاوي على تقدير علية الحاوي، فتكون علية الحاوي باطلة معا أي موجودان معا؛ لكونهما معلولي علة واحدة، وهو العقل الأول. مع المتقدم والذي يكون مع المتقدم بالعلية لا يكون متقدما بالعلية؛ لأنه ليس بعلة حتى يكون له التقدم بالعلية هداية: دفع اشتباه يرد ههنا وهو أن الحاوي والمحوي كلاهما ممكنان فيمكن عدمهما، فبعدمهما يلزم الخلاء، فإمكان الخلاء لازم سواء كان الحاوي علة أو لا، فأجاب عنه بأن عدمهما معا لا يستلزم إمكان الخلاء؛ لأن الخلاء عبارة عن المكان الخالي عن الشاغل، وهو إنما يحصل بوجود الحاوي وعدم المحوي، وأما على تقدير عدمهما فليس هناك شيء يسمى مكانا، بل عدم صرف فالحال على تقدير عدمهما كحال ما وراء الفلك الأعظم الآن، فإن وراءه ليس خلاء ولا ملاء، بل عدم صرف وإن كان الوهم يأبى ذلك ويتصور خلاء؛ لاعتياده بالبعد الهوائي وظنه خلاء. لا يقتضى الخلاء؛ لأن الخلاء لا يلزم من ذلك، وإنما يلزم من اجتماع وجود الحاوي وعدم المحوي، وذلك غير ممكن.
The Third Art: On Angels. Chapter: On Proving the Multiplicity of Intellects. The existence of the container; because the necessity of the existence of the effect is posterior to the necessity of the existence of the cause. And if that is the case, then the non-existence of the contained with the existence of the container would not be impossible in itself; otherwise its existence with it would not be posterior to it, but we assumed it posterior; this is a contradiction. And if the non-existence of the contained with the existence of the container is possible, then the existence of void would be possible in itself; this is a contradiction. So it appears that the effect on the celestial spheres is from multiple intellects. Note (1): The container and the cause of the contained—which is the second intellect—together the cause is prior to the contained, but the container is not prior; because the cause is prior by causality, and what is together with that which is prior by causality need not be prior by causality. Note (2): The container and the contained are each possible in itself, but that is so. Summary: If the contained is an effect of the container, then the existence of the contained is posterior to the existence of the container by necessity of the effect being posterior to its cause. And if the existence of the contained is not in the same rank as the existence of the container, then its non-existence is in that rank due to the impossibility of the two contradictories being raised. The existence of void: because the non-existence of the contained with the existence of the container and the existence of void are two concomitants, and void is impossible in itself, its possibility is not conceived at all at any time; and the impossibility of one of two concomitants necessitates the impossibility of the other, otherwise the concomitance is invalid. So the non-existence of the contained with the existence of the container is also impossible, not possible with it. And the non-existence of the contained was possible with the existence of the container on the assumption of the container being a cause; thus the causality of the container is invalid. Rather, they exist together because they are effects of a single cause, which is the first intellect. What is together with what is prior by causality is not prior by causality, because it is not a cause so as to have priority by causality. Note: Repelling a confusion that occurs here: that the container and the contained are both possible, so their non-existence is possible, and by their non-existence void would be necessary; thus the possibility of void is necessary whether the container is a cause or not. He answered: that their non-existence together does not necessitate the possibility of void, because void is defined as a place empty of a filler, and it only occurs by the existence of the container and the non-existence of the contained. But on the assumption of their non-existence, there is nothing called a place, rather pure non-existence. So the situation on the assumption of their non-existence is like the situation beyond the highest sphere now: beyond it is neither void nor plenum, but pure non-existence, even though the imagination refuses that and imagines a void due to its habituation to aerial distance and its illusion of void. It does not necessitate void, because void does not follow from that; rather it follows from the conjunction of the existence of the container and the non-existence of the contained, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the multiplicity of intellects—specifically that the celestial spheres are moved by multiple intellects rather than a single one. The argument proceeds by considering a 'container' (al-ḥāwī) and a 'contained' (al-maḥwī), terms referring metaphorically to the first intellect and the second intellect, where the first is the cause of the second. The author sets up a conditional: if the container were the complete cause of the contained, then the contained's existence must be posterior to the container's existence. This is a standard causal principle: the effect is posterior to the cause. Given this, if the container exists and the contained does not, this non-existence is not impossible in itself, because the contained's existence is contingent. However, if the container's existence alone could cause the contained, then at the moment the container exists, the contained must exist; if it does not, the causality fails. The author shows that if the non-existence of the contained with the container is possible, then void (al-khalā') would be possible—an impossibility. Void is defined as a place empty of body, and its existence is impossible in itself according to Aristotlean physics. Since void is impossible, the non-existence of the contained with the container cannot be possible, meaning the container cannot be the complete cause. Therefore, the container and the contained must coexist as two effects of a higher cause (the first intellect). The passage then includes two notes (hidāya) clarifying subtle points: first, the container (first intellect) is not itself the cause of the contained; rather, the container and the cause of the contained (second intellect) together are prior, but the container is not prior by causality. Second, the objector says: both container and contained are possible, so their non-existence together is possible, and from their non-existence void would follow equally, regardless of causality. The author refutes this by saying that void only results from the existence of one and non-existence of the other, not from the non-existence of both. When both are absent, there is no 'place' at all—pure nothingness—which is not the same as void. He gives the example of the region beyond the outermost sphere: it is neither void nor plenum, just pure non-existence, though the imagination pictures a void out of habit. This argument establishes that the assumption of the container as cause leads to an impossible consequence, so the cause must be a higher intellect. The multiplicity of intellects is thus proven. The passage is a classic example of burhān (demonstration) using conditional reasoning and refutation of objections.

Key Terms
الحاوي
المحوي
الخلاء
العلة

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what is the impossible consequence if the container were the complete cause of the contained?
A) The contained would not exist.
B) Void would be possible.
C) The container would cease to exist.
D) The contained would become necessary.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of void proves that the first intellect cannot be the complete cause of the second; both must be effects of a higher cause, thus multiplicity of intellects is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional argument (using modus tollens) on a different causal claim in falsafa: e.g., 'If the First Cause were directly the cause of the material world, then the material world would be eternal. But the material world is temporally originated (according to some theologians). Therefore, the First Cause is not the direct cause of the material world.' Validate your steps.

Refuting the Void Objection: Distinguishing Void from Pure Non-Existence
Author refutes objection that void possibility is independent of causality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that the possibility of void follows from the non-existence of both intellects by distinguishing between the absence of both and the absence of one only.

Core Idea
Void requires a place; when both intellects are absent, there is no place—only pure non-existence.

An objector might argue: if both the container and contained are possible, then their joint non-existence is possible, and that would imply void just as well. In this passage, the author anticipates and refutes this confusion by clarifying the definition of void. This is a crucial exercise in conceptual clarification within logical debate.

Guided Path
1
State the objection clearly
The objector says: both intellects are possible in themselves, so we can imagine them both not existing. If they both do not exist, then there would be no body in that region, which seems to be void. Therefore, the possibility of void is independent of causality.
2
Identify the author's key distinction
The author distinguishes two scenarios: (a) the container exists and the contained does not—this would be a place (the container's location) empty of the contained, i.e., void. (b) both do not exist—then there is no place at all, only pure non-existence (ʿadam maḥḍ). Void is not pure non-existence; it is a place empty of a filler.
3
Apply the analogy of beyond the highest sphere
The author says: beyond the highest sphere (al-falak al-aʿẓam) is neither void nor plenum—just pure non-existence. This illustrates that the absence of both does not create void. The imagination mistakenly pictures void there due to habituation to airy distances.
4
Conclude that the objection fails
Since void only arises from the existence of one and non-existence of the other, and that scenario is impossible (as proven earlier), the objection is invalid. The possibility of both being absent does not entail the possibility of void.
This refutation protects the original proof from a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث في الملائكة فصل في إثبات كثرة العقول وجود الحاوي؛ لأن وجوب وجود المعلول مؤخر عن وجوب وجود العلة، وإذا كان كذلك فعدم المحوي مع وجود الحاوي لا يكون ممتنعا لذاته وإلا لكان وجوده معه لا متأخرا عنه، وقد فرضناه متأخرا، هذا خلف، وإذا كان عدم المحوي مع وجود الحاوي ممكنا كان وجود الخلاء ممكنا لذاته، هذا خلف، فظهر أن المؤثر في الأفلاك عقول متكثرة. هداية (۱) الحاوي وسبب المحوي وهو العقل الثاني معا السبب متقدم على المحوي، ولكن الحاوي ليس بمتقدم؛ لأن السبب مع أن متقدم بالعلية، وما مع المتقدم بالعلية لا يجب أن يكون متقدما بالعلية. هداية :(۲): الحاوي والمحوي كل واحد منهما ممكن لذاته، ولكن ذلك كذلك: حاصله : أنه إذا كان المحوي معلولا للحاوي، كان وجود المحوي متأخرا عن وجود الحاوي؛ ضرورة تأخر المعلول عن علته، وإذا لم يكن وجود المحوي في مرتبة وجود الحاوي كان عدمه فيها؛ لامتناع ارتفاع النقيضين. وجود الخلاء: لأن عدم المحوي مع وجود الحاوي ووجود الخلاء متلازمان، والخلاء ممتنع لذاته لا يتصور إمكانه أصلا في وقت وامتناع أحد المتلازمين يوجب امتناع الآخر، وإلا يبطل الملازمة، فعدم المحوي مع وجود الحاوي أيضا ممتنع لا يكون ممكنا معه، وقد كان عدم المحوي ممكنا مع وجود الحاوي على تقدير علية الحاوي، فتكون علية الحاوي باطلة معا أي موجودان معا؛ لكونهما معلولي علة واحدة، وهو العقل الأول. مع المتقدم والذي يكون مع المتقدم بالعلية لا يكون متقدما بالعلية؛ لأنه ليس بعلة حتى يكون له التقدم بالعلية هداية: دفع اشتباه يرد ههنا وهو أن الحاوي والمحوي كلاهما ممكنان فيمكن عدمهما، فبعدمهما يلزم الخلاء، فإمكان الخلاء لازم سواء كان الحاوي علة أو لا، فأجاب عنه بأن عدمهما معا لا يستلزم إمكان الخلاء؛ لأن الخلاء عبارة عن المكان الخالي عن الشاغل، وهو إنما يحصل بوجود الحاوي وعدم المحوي، وأما على تقدير عدمهما فليس هناك شيء يسمى مكانا، بل عدم صرف فالحال على تقدير عدمهما كحال ما وراء الفلك الأعظم الآن، فإن وراءه ليس خلاء ولا ملاء، بل عدم صرف وإن كان الوهم يأبى ذلك ويتصور خلاء؛ لاعتياده بالبعد الهوائي وظنه خلاء. لا يقتضى الخلاء؛ لأن الخلاء لا يلزم من ذلك، وإنما يلزم من اجتماع وجود الحاوي وعدم المحوي، وذلك غير ممكن.
The Third Art: On Angels. Chapter: On Proving the Multiplicity of Intellects. The existence of the container; because the necessity of the existence of the effect is posterior to the necessity of the existence of the cause. And if that is the case, then the non-existence of the contained with the existence of the container would not be impossible in itself; otherwise its existence with it would not be posterior to it, but we assumed it posterior; this is a contradiction. And if the non-existence of the contained with the existence of the container is possible, then the existence of void would be possible in itself; this is a contradiction. So it appears that the effect on the celestial spheres is from multiple intellects. Note (1): The container and the cause of the contained—which is the second intellect—together the cause is prior to the contained, but the container is not prior; because the cause is prior by causality, and what is together with that which is prior by causality need not be prior by causality. Note (2): The container and the contained are each possible in itself, but that is so. Summary: If the contained is an effect of the container, then the existence of the contained is posterior to the existence of the container by necessity of the effect being posterior to its cause. And if the existence of the contained is not in the same rank as the existence of the container, then its non-existence is in that rank due to the impossibility of the two contradictories being raised. The existence of void: because the non-existence of the contained with the existence of the container and the existence of void are two concomitants, and void is impossible in itself, its possibility is not conceived at all at any time; and the impossibility of one of two concomitants necessitates the impossibility of the other, otherwise the concomitance is invalid. So the non-existence of the contained with the existence of the container is also impossible, not possible with it. And the non-existence of the contained was possible with the existence of the container on the assumption of the container being a cause; thus the causality of the container is invalid. Rather, they exist together because they are effects of a single cause, which is the first intellect. What is together with what is prior by causality is not prior by causality, because it is not a cause so as to have priority by causality. The Conditional Argument for Full Actuality of Intellects
Conditional proof for intellects' full actuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional (istithnā'ī) argument proving that the intellects are fully actual and identify its modus tollens form.

Core Idea
Any potentiality in immaterial beings would imply materiality — an impossibility — so intellects must be pure act.

This passage uses a conditional argument to prove that the intellects cannot have any potentiality. Understanding this logical move is essential for grasping how Islamic philosophers establish the eternity of immaterial entities. We will break down the argument step by step and see how the logician uses the principle that potentiality and temporal origination are marks of matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the statement: 'if any of its attributes are potential and then occur, it would necessitate that the immaterial being is material.' This is the key conditional (If P then Q).
The antecedent is 'potentiality exists in an immaterial being' and the consequent is 'it becomes material'.
2
Find the denial of the consequent
The author asserts that the intellects are immaterial (non-material). This denies the consequent (not Q).
Immateriality is taken as established from elsewhere.
3
Apply modus tollens
From 'If P then Q' and 'not Q', we conclude 'not P'. Therefore, the intellects have no potentiality; they are fully actual.
This is a valid conditional inference (modus tollens).
4
Draw the consequence for eternity
Full actuality means the intellect never lacked any condition for existence. Since the complete cause (God's self and attributes) is eternal, the effect (the intellect) must be co-eternal.
The link to the complete cause principle is used to extend full actuality to eternal existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أزلية العقول وأبديتها أما كونها أزلية فلوجوه أحدها: أن واجب الوجود مستجمع الجملة ما لا بد منه في تأثيره في معلوله وإلا لكان له تعالى حالة منتظرة، هذا خلف، والعقول أيضا مستلزمة لجملة ما لا بد منه في تأثير بعضها في بعض؛ لأن كل ما يمكن لها فهو حاصل لها بالفعل، وإلا لكان شيء منها حادثا، وكل حادث مسبوق بمادة فتكون هي مادية، هذا خلف ويلزمه من هذا أزليتها؛ لأن المعلول يجب وجوده عند وجود علته التامة. أزلية العقول وهو عبارة عن استمرار الوجود في الجانب الماضي بحيث لا يكون مسبوقا بالعدم أبديتها هو عبارة عن استمرار الوجود في الجانب المستقبل بحيث لا يلحقه العدم. لجملة : فيه إيماء إلى أن ذاته تعالى ليست علة تامة للعقل الأول، بل هناك معها أمور أخر حاصلة في الأزل، ولعل المراد بها صفاته تعالى من الإرادة والقدرة. يمكن إلخ خلاصة المرام أن جميع صفات المجرد حاصلة له بالفعل، وإن كان شيء من صفاته بالقوة ثم يحدث لزم كون المجرد ماديا؛ لأن القوة والحدوث من خصائص المادة.
Chapter: On the Eternity and Perpetuity of Intellects. As for their being eternal, [it is proven] by several arguments. One of them: that the Necessary Being possesses the entirety of what is necessary for His effecting His effect; otherwise He would have a waiting state—this is a contradiction. And the intellects also entail the entirety of what is necessary for their effecting one another, because everything that is possible for them is actually possessed by them; otherwise some of them would be temporally originated, and every temporally originated thing is preceded by matter, so they would be material—this is a contradiction. And from this follows their eternity, because the effect must exist when its complete cause exists. The eternity of the intellects is the continuation of existence in the past direction such that it is not preceded by non-existence. Their perpetuity is the continuation of existence in the future direction such that non-existence does not attach to it. As for 'the entirety': it alludes to the fact that His Essence is not the complete cause of the first intellect; rather, there are other things with it that come to be in eternity, and perhaps what is meant by them is His attributes of will and power. 'Possible etc.': the gist is that all attributes of an immaterial being are actually possessed by it; if any of its attributes are potential and then occur, it would necessitate that the immaterial being is material, because potentiality and temporal origination are characteristics of matter.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage demonstrates the eternity (azaliyya) and perpetuity (abadiyya) of the intellects—specifically the separate intellects that move the celestial spheres. The author presents a key argument: the Necessary Being (wājib al-wujūd) is fully actual and lacks nothing needed for His effect. If He had a waiting state (ḥāla muntaẓira), i.e., needed something to occur later, He would be temporal, which is impossible. Similarly, each intellect must possess everything it needs to cause its effect; otherwise, it would have potentiality. Potentiality in an immaterial substance would necessitate materiality, because only matter has potentiality. Since the intellects are immaterial, they must be fully actual. This full actuality implies that they are eternal: they cannot begin to exist because they never lacked any condition. The author defines azaliyya as existence with no beginning and abadiyya as existence with no end. He also notes that 'the entirety of what is necessary' alludes to the fact that God's essence alone is not the complete cause of the first intellect; His attributes (will and power) are also involved. The final note emphasizes that an immaterial being cannot have potential attributes; if it did, it would be material. This argument is reminiscent of the principle that an immaterial substance is pure act. The proof uses conditional reasoning: if there were any potentiality, then the intellect would be temporal and material, which is contradictory; therefore, it is fully actual and eternal. This is a standard argument in Islamic metaphysics for the eternity of the separate intellects.

Key Terms
أزلية
أبدية
وجوب الوجود
علة تامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the conditional argument in the passage, what conclusion do we reach about the attributes of immaterial intellects?
A) They are partly potential and partly actual.
B) They are fully actual because any potentiality would entail materiality.
C) They are only actual after temporal origination.
D) They are potential because intellects are created.

Key Takeaway
Conditional reasoning (modus tollens) proves that immaterial intellects are fully actual, which is the basis for their eternity and perpetuity.
