The Complete Cause and the Eternity of Effects
Application of complete cause to prove eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of complete cause ('illa tāmma) to determine whether an effect is eternal or temporal.

Core Idea
If the complete cause is eternal and unchanging, its effect must be co-eternal.

This passage explains that the intellects are eternal because they are the effect of a complete cause that is eternally present. The concept of 'complete cause' is central to Avicennan metaphysics and has direct applications in theology and jurisprudence. We will see how the eternity or temporality of an effect depends on whether its cause is complete and eternal.

Guided Path
1
Define 'complete cause'
A complete cause ('illa tāmma) is the full set of conditions that necessitates the existence of the effect. If any condition is missing, the effect does not exist.
In the text, God's essence and attributes together form the complete cause of the first intellect.
2
Identify the cause in the argument
The author says the Necessary Being possesses 'the entirety of what is necessary for His effecting His effect'. This includes not just His essence but also His attributes (will, power) as the commentary notes.
The phrase 'jimla' (totality) indicates that the complete cause includes additional factors beyond the essence.
3
Apply the causal principle
The principle: when the complete cause exists, the effect necessarily exists. Since the complete cause (God's essence and attributes) is eternal, the effect (the first intellect) must also exist eternally.
This is why the intellect is eternal (azali) and perpetual (abadi).
4
Contrast with temporal effects
For temporal things, their complete cause involves a temporal condition that was not always present. For intellects, no such condition is missing; therefore they are not temporal.
This distinguishes the intellects from sublunary things, which have potentiality and depend on matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Their perpetuity is the continuation of existence in the future direction such that non-existence does not attach to it. As for 'the entirety': it alludes to the fact that His Essence is not the complete cause of the first intellect; rather, there are other things with it that come to be in eternity, and perhaps what is meant by them is His attributes of will and power. 'Possible etc.': the gist is that all attributes of an immaterial being are actually possessed by it; if any of its attributes are potential and then occur, it would necessitate that the immaterial being is material, because potentiality and temporal origination are characteristics of matter.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage demonstrates the eternity (azaliyya) and perpetuity (abadiyya) of the intellects—specifically the separate intellects that move the celestial spheres. The author presents a key argument: the Necessary Being (wājib al-wujūd) is fully actual and lacks nothing needed for His effect. If He had a waiting state (ḥāla muntaẓira), i.e., needed something to occur later, He would be temporal, which is impossible. Similarly, each intellect must possess everything it needs to cause its effect; otherwise, it would have potentiality. Potentiality in an immaterial substance would necessitate materiality, because only matter has potentiality. Since the intellects are immaterial, they must be fully actual. This full actuality implies that they are eternal: they cannot begin to exist because they never lacked any condition. The author defines azaliyya as existence with no beginning and abadiyya as existence with no end. He also notes that 'the entirety of what is necessary' alludes to the fact that God's essence alone is not the complete cause of the first intellect; His attributes (will and power) are also involved. The final note emphasizes that an immaterial being cannot have potential attributes; if it did, it would be material. This argument is reminiscent of the principle that an immaterial substance is pure act. The proof uses conditional reasoning: if there were any potentiality, then the intellect would be temporal and material, which is contradictory; therefore, it is fully actual and eternal. This is a standard argument in Islamic metaphysics for the eternity of the separate intellects.

Key Terms
علة تامة
معلول
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the fact that the intellects are fully actual lead to their eternity?
A) Because full actuality means they cannot have a beginning.
B) Because the complete cause of the intellects (God's attributes) is eternal, and the effect exists whenever its complete cause exists.
C) Because potentiality is a mark of temporality, and they lack potentiality.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of the intellects follows from the eternal existence of their complete cause, which includes God's essence and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Conditional Proof for the Eternity of Intellects
Author presents the first argument that the intellects are eternal by analyzing the conditions of causal efficacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditional structure of the proof for the eternity of intellects and evaluate its logical validity.

Core Idea
If all conditions are actual, the effect is necessary; thus the intellects are eternal.

This passage presents the first argument for the eternity of the intellects. It uses a conditional form: if all necessary conditions are present, the effect necessarily exists. The author applies this to the Necessary Being and then to the intellects, showing that any unrealized potential would entail matter, which is impossible for immaterial intellects. Understanding this conditional reasoning is key to grasping the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional principle
Find the statement that the effect exists when its complete cause exists. This is the main conditional premise: 'المعلول يجب وجوده عند وجود علته التامة'.
This is a fundamental causal principle in Islamic philosophy.
2
Apply to the Necessary Being
The Necessary Being possesses all conditions for causing His effect. The antecedent: He has all necessary conditions. The consequent: He is not in a state of waiting (otherwise absurd). Note the conditional form: if He lacked something, He would be waiting, which is impossible.
3
Apply to the intellects
For the intellects, all their possible attributes are actual. If any were unrealized, that would be an originated event (حادث). Every originated event requires matter, so the intellects would be material—this is absurd. Thus, all potential is actual.
The conditional: if not fully actual, then originated; if originated, then material; material is impossible; so fully actual.
4
Derive the conclusion
Since the intellects are fully actual, the complete cause for their effects is always present. Therefore, their effects (the next intellects) must exist perpetually. This implies the intellects are eternal.
The conclusion follows from the principle that when the complete cause exists, the effect exists necessarily and perpetually.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما كونها أزلية فلوجوه أحدها: أن واجب الوجود مستجمع الجملة ما لا بد منه في تأثيره في معلوله وإلا لكان له تعالى حالة منتظرة، هذا خلف، والعقول أيضا مستلزمة لجملة ما لا بد منه في تأثير بعضها في بعض؛ لأن كل ما يمكن لها فهو حاصل لها بالفعل، وإلا لكان شيء منها حادثا، وكل حادث مسبوق بمادة فتكون هي مادية، هذا خلف ويلزمه من هذا أزليتها؛ لأن المعلول يجب وجوده عند وجود علته التامة.
As for their being eternal, there are several aspects; one of them: that the Necessary Being possesses the entire set of what is necessary for His effecting His effect, otherwise He would have a state of waiting, which is absurd. And the intellects also possess the entire set of what is necessary for their effecting one another, because whatever is possible for them is already actual for them; otherwise something of them would be originated, and every originated thing is preceded by matter, so they would be material, which is absurd. And from this follows their eternity, because the effect must exist at the existence of its complete cause.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage gives the first proof for the eternity of the intellects. It begins by establishing that the Necessary Being (واجب الوجود) has all the necessary conditions for causing His effect; otherwise He would be in a state of waiting, which is impossible. Similarly, the intellects, which are effects of the Necessary Being, also have all the necessary conditions for causing one another. The key point is that whatever is possible for them is already actual (حاصل بالفعل). If any potential were unrealized, it would result in an originated event (حادث) in them. Since every originated event requires matter (as argued earlier), this would make the intellects material, which contradicts their immaterial nature. Therefore, they must be fully actual and thus eternal, because the effect exists when its complete cause exists. This argument relies on the principle that the cause and its complete cause are together (teleologists).

Key Terms
علة تامة (‘illa tāmma): Complete cause: the set of all conditions necessary for the existence of the effect; when present, the effect necessarily exists. [Note: In later logic, the distinction between complete cause and partial cause is refined, especially in discussions of contingency.]
حادث (ḥādith): Originated: something that comes into existence after not existing; temporally originated. [Note: In kalām, ḥādith often means 'non-eternal' and is opposed to qadīm; here it implies a temporal beginning.]
مادة (mādda): Matter: the substratum that underlies change; in this context, it is the potentiality that precedes origination. [Note: This is a philosophical term; in logic, it is not typically used. Here it links to Aristotelian hylomorphism.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that if the intellects lacked any actual attribute, they would be material?
A) Because every potential attribute implies matter
B) Because every originated thing is preceded by matter
C) Because the intellects are immaterial by definition
D) Because the Necessary Being is immaterial

Key Takeaway
The eternity of the intellects follows from the actualization of all their potential and the impossibility of matter in immaterial beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional argument for the eternity of the human soul using the same principle: if the soul's final perfection is actualized, then it cannot cease to exist. Use the format: If A, then B; A is true; therefore B.
?

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
5 min
Proof for the Perpetuity of Intellects
Author demonstrates that the intellects are perpetual because they are effects of an eternal cause with complete causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof for the perpetuity of the intellects using the principle of causal concomitance and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
The effect's perpetuity follows from its complete cause's perpetuity due to necessary concomitance.

In this passage, the author of Hidayat al-Hikma presents a rigorous proof for the perpetuity of the intellects, a key doctrine in Islamic metaphysics. He builds his argument on the principle that an effect and its complete cause are necessarily concomitant: the existence of the complete cause guarantees the existence of the effect, and the absence of the effect implies the absence of something in its complete cause. By applying this principle to the Creator and the chain of intellects, he derives their eternal and perpetual existence. Let's walk through the steps of this demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key principle
The author states that 'the effect and its complete cause are concomitant; neither is separated from the other.' This means that if the complete cause exists, the effect must exist, and if the effect ceases, the complete cause must have ceased in some respect. Recognize this as a necessary causal relationship.
This principle is rooted in the metaphysics of causation.
2
Apply the principle to the Creator
The Creator (al-Bāri') is described as both eternal (azalī) and perpetual (abadī). Since the Creator is the complete cause of the first intellect, that intellect must also be eternal and perpetual by the principle of concomitance.
The Creator's attributes of azal and abad are taken as given from earlier discussions.
3
Chain the reasoning to subsequent intellects
The first intellect is itself a cause of the second intellect. Since the first intellect is eternal and perpetual, its effect (the second intellect) must also be eternal and perpetual. This reasoning extends down the hierarchy of intellects.
The chain is linear: each intellect is an effect of the one above and a cause of the one below.
4
Consider the reductio argument
If any intellect were to cease to exist, then by the principle of concomitance, something of its complete cause (which includes the Creator and higher intellects) would also cease to exist. This would imply that the Creator and intellects undergo change and are subject to temporal events.
The author calls this conclusion 'absurd' (khulf) because it contradicts the immateriality of these beings.
5
Conclude the perpetuity of all intellects
Since the reductio leads to an impossibility, the intellects must be perpetual. They exist as long as their complete cause exists, and since the Creator is eternal and perpetual, the entire chain of intellects shares that attribute.
The proof relies on the stability of the causal relationship and the attributes of the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أبدية قد ثبت في مقامه أن المعلول وعلته التامة متلازمان لا يتخلف أحد منهما عن الآخر، فلما كان البارئ تعالى أزليا وأبديا كان معلوله كذلك، ولما كان معلوله أزليا وأبديا كان معلول معلوله أيضا كذلك، ولو انعدم المعلول لكان شيء من علته التامة منعدما فكان البارئ والعقول محلا للحوادث والتغير هذا خلف؛ لأنه يستلزم كون المجرد ماديا.
Perpetuity: It has been established in its place that the effect and its complete cause are concomitant; neither of them is separated from the other. Since the Creator, exalted, is eternal and perpetual, His effect is likewise. And since His effect is eternal and perpetual, the effect of His effect is also likewise. And if the effect were to cease to exist, then something of its complete cause would cease to exist, so the Creator and the intellects would be loci of events and change, which is absurd; because that would imply that the immaterial is material.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents the proof for the perpetuity (abadiyya) of the intellects. The author starts from a principle established earlier: that an effect and its complete cause are necessarily concomitant (mutalāzimān); when the complete cause exists, the effect must exist, and when the effect is absent, the complete cause must be absent. Applying this: the Creator (al-Bāri’) is both eternal (azalī) and perpetual (abadī). Therefore, His immediate effect (the first intellect) must also be eternal and perpetual. By the same reasoning, since that intellect is eternal and perpetual, its own effect (the next intellect) is also eternal and perpetual, and so on down the chain. Then the author argues by contradiction: if any effect (intellect) were to cease to exist, that would imply that something of its complete cause (which includes the Creator and the higher intellects) would also cease to exist (because of the concomitance). This would make the Creator and the intellects subject to temporal change (hawādith), which is absurd because it would entail that the immaterial beings become material. Thus, the intellects must be perpetual.

Key Terms
متلازمان
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are self-subsistent and independent

C) They are material and thus subject to change
D) They are contingent and could be annihilated

Key Takeaway
The perpetuity of the intellects is derived from the necessary concomitance between an effect and its complete cause, combined with the Creator's eternity and perpetuity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
